The Ambiguity of Judgment and Related Terms
Commentator clarifies the ambiguity of terms related to judgment and defends that judgment is a quality, not an act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two senses of terms like 'iqa' and explain why later scholars considered judgment an act, and why the correct view is that it is a quality.

Core Idea
Judgment is not an act but an assent; terms for 'predication' can refer either to the proposition or its perception.

The passage opens by noting that several technical terms in logic are ambiguous. They can refer either to the complete propositional relation itself or to the mental perception of that relation. This ambiguity led later scholars to mistakenly treat judgment as an act of the soul. The commentator clarifies the correct view and discusses the implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous terms
The passage lists: al-ijab, al-salb, al-iqa', al-intiza', al-isnad. Each can refer to two things.
2
Separate the two referents
One is the complete relation (al-nisba al-tamma), the other is the perception of that relation (idrak) in a convincing manner.
3
Understand the later scholars' mistake
Because the terms suggest an act, most later scholars said judgment is an act (fi'l) of the soul.
4
Learn the correct view from Sharh al-Mutali'
The reality is that there is no act; it is merely assenting (idh'an) and acceptance (qabul) of the relation, which is a quality (kayf).
5
Recognize the extension to tasawwur
Judgment in this sense can also be found in tasawwur (conception), as in imagining and doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إيقاعا: اعلم أن لفظ الإيجاب والسلب والإيقاع والانتزاع والإسناد كل واحد منها قد يطلق على النسبة التامة حملية كانت أو شرطية، اتصالية أو انفصالية، وقد يطلق على إدراك تلك النسبة على وجه الإذعان ولما كان هذه الألفاظ موهمة بحسب اللغة أن للنفس بعد تصور النسبة فعلا صادرا عنها زعم أكثر المتأخرين أن الحكم فعل من أفعال النفس، والحق ما قال شارح "المطالع": إن الحكم وإيقاع النسبة والإسناد كلها عبارات ،وألفاظ والتحقيق أنه ليس للنفس ههنا تأثير فعل، بل إذعان وقبول النسبة وهو إدراك أن النسبة واقعة أو ليست بواقعة فهو من مقولة الكيف. وقد يفسر إلخ والحكم بهذا المعنى قد يوجد في التصور أيضا كما في التخييل والشك الذين هما من أقسام التصور، وظاهر التفسير لا يصح إلا على رأي المتأخرين ضرورة أن وقوع النسبة نفسها وهم قد قالوا بالنسبة التقيدية التي هي مورد الوقوع واللاوقوع وسموها النسبة بين بين. واعلم أن الحكم يطلق على أربعة معان: ١- المحكوم به. ٢- ووقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها. ٣- والنسبة من حيث اشتمالها على الربط. ٤- وإدراك وقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها.
['Iqa'an'] Know that the terms 'positive' and 'negative', 'affirmation' and 'negation', and 'predication' – each of them may be applied to the complete relation, whether categorical or conditional, conjunctive or disjunctive, and they may be applied to the perception of that relation in a convincing manner. Since these expressions are ambiguous in language, suggesting that after conceiving the relation the soul performs an act, most later scholars held that judgment is an act of the soul. But the truth is what the commentator of al-Mutali' said: 'Judgment, affirmation of the relation, and predication – all are expressions and words. The reality is that there is no effect of action by the soul here, rather it is assenting to and accepting the relation, which is perceiving that the relation exists or does not exist, so it belongs to the category of quality.' 'And it is also interpreted etc.': Judgment in this sense may also be found in tasawwur, as in imagining and doubt, which are kinds of tasawwur. The apparent interpretation is only valid according to the later scholars, necessarily because the occurrence of the relation itself – they said concerning the restrictive relation which is subject to occurrence and non-occurrence, and they called it the relation between the two. Know that 'judgment' is used in four senses: 1) The thing judged (the predicate). 2) The occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence. 3) The relation insofar as it includes the copula. 4) The perception of the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a terminological clarification regarding the term 'hukm' (judgment) and related expressions. The commentator begins by noting that terms like 'positive/negative', 'affirmation/negation', and 'predication' are ambiguous: they can refer either to the complete propositional relation itself (whether categorical or conditional, conjunctive or disjunctive) or to the perception of that relation in an assentive manner. Because these terms linguistically suggest an act of the soul after conceiving the relation, most later scholars considered judgment to be an actual act of the soul. However, the commentator, citing the author of Sharh al-Mutali', argues that there is no such act; rather, judgment is simply the assenting to and acceptance of the relation – a perception that the relation holds or does not hold, which is a quality (kayf) of the mind, not an action. He then links this to the interpretation of judgment as 'occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation', noting that this interpretation only works according to the later scholars who introduced the concept of 'relation between the two' (al-nisba bayn bayn). Finally, he enumerates four distinct meanings of the term 'hukm': (1) the predicate itself (al-mahkum bihi), (2) the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation, (3) the relation as including the copula, and (4) the perception of that occurrence or non-occurrence. This clarifies that 'hukm' is used in multiple senses and one must be careful to distinguish them.

السلب
الإيقاع
الانتزاع
الإسناد
النسبة التامة
إدراك
الإذعان
الكيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct view of judgment according to the commentator of al-Mutali'?
A) It is an act of the soul.
B) It is a quality (kayf) of assenting to the relation.
C) It is the predicate itself.
D) It is the complete relation.

Key Takeaway
The logical terms for judgment are ambiguous; the correct view is that judgment is an assentive quality, not an act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about the nature of hukm in usul al-fiqh, two scholars argue: one says hukm is an act of the mujtahid, the other says it is the divine address. Using the distinction in this lesson, explain which sense each is using and whether they are actually in disagreement.

The Four Meanings of Hukm (Judgment)
Commentator enumerates and explains the four distinct meanings of the term hukm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the four distinct meanings of the term 'hukm' as enumerated in the passage.

Core Idea
Hukm has four meanings: the predicate, the occurrence of the relation, the relation as copula, and the perception of the relation's occurrence.

After clarifying the terminological ambiguity, the commentator directly enumerates the four distinct senses in which the term 'hukm' is used in logical discourse. This enumeration is crucial for reading classical texts accurately, as authors often shift between these meanings without explicit notice.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the four meanings
The passage lists: (1) al-mahkum bihi (the predicate), (2) wuqu' al-nisba aw la wuqu'aha (occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation), (3) al-nisba min haythu ishtimaluha 'ala al-rabt (the relation insofar as it includes the copula), (4) idrak wuqu' al-nisba aw la wuqu'aha (perception of the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation).
2
Understand each meaning with examples
For meaning 1, in 'Zaid is standing', the predicate 'standing' is called hukm. For meaning 2, the actual occurrence of the relation between subject and predicate. For meaning 3, the relation itself acting as the copula. For meaning 4, the mental act of perceiving that relation.
3
Identify which meaning is considered the real judgment
From the previous discussion, the commentator favors meaning 4 (perception) as the genuine judgment, though the term is used in all four ways.
4
Apply the distinction when reading logical texts
When you encounter 'hukm' in a text, determine which sense is intended by context. This avoids misinterpretation.

Complete Compound: Proposition vs. Performative
Defines complete compound and its subdivision into proposition and performative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a proposition (khabar) from a performative (inshāʼ) and explain why truth and falsehood apply to propositions even for necessarily true statements.

Core Idea
A complete compound either narrates (proposition) or performs an action (performative); only propositions can be true or false.

Every sentence we utter either tells us something about the world or does something like commanding, asking, or hoping. The Islamic logical tradition calls these two types proposition (khabar) and performative (inshāʼ). In this passage from the Mīrqāt, the author defines the complete compound—a sentence that makes sense on its own—and then divides it into these two fundamental categories. He also tackles a tricky objection: what about a statement like 'There is no god but Allah,' which is always true? Does it even count as a proposition? The answer reveals a key insight about how truth and falsity work in logic.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish complete from incomplete compound
Find the definition of 'complete compound' (murakkab tāmm): an expression upon which it is correct to stop, i.e., it gives complete meaning independently. Contrast with incomplete compound (nāqiṣ), which does not. Examples: 'Zayd is standing' (complete) vs. 'standing' (incomplete).
2
Identify the two types of complete compound
The passage splits the complete compound into two kinds: (1) khabar (also called qaḍiyya), which aims to narrate and admits truth and falsehood, and (2) inshāʼ (performative), which does not narrate and has no truth value. Note that the speaker of a khabar can be called truthful or lying.
Examples of khabar: 'The sky is above us', 'The world is temporally originated'. Examples of inshāʼ: command, prohibition, wish, etc.
3
Address the objection about necessarily true statements
Consider the objection: 'There is no god but Allah' is a proposition (khabar), yet it cannot be false. Does that contradict the definition? The author replies: the mere wording (linguistic form) admits falsehood, even if in reality (due to its content being necessarily true) it is always true. Commentator adds: truth/falsehood is based on the concept alone, not on actual occurrence or the speaker's state.
This preserves the definition: the admission of truth/falsehood is about the proposition's form, not its external truth value.
4
Classify inshāʼ subtypes
The passage lists six divisions of inshāʼ: command (amr), prohibition (nahy), wish (tamannī), hope (tarajjī), question (istifhām), and address (nidāʼ). Each is briefly defined. Note that the enumeration is said to be based on induction (istiqrāʼ).
Performatives do not have a 'narrated content' (maḥkī ʿanhu) at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: المركب قسمان: أحدهما: المركب التام، وهو ما يصح السكوت عليه كزيد قائم، وثانيهما: المركب الناقص، هو ما ليس كذلك. فصل: المركب التام ضربان يقال لأحدهما الخبر والقضية، وهو ما قصد به الحكاية ويحتمل الصدق والكذب، ويقال لقائله : إنه صادق أو كاذب، نحو: السماء فوقنا، والعالم حادث. فإن قيل: قولنا: "لا إله إلا الله" قضية وخبر مع أنه لا يحتمل الكذب؟ قلت : مجرد اللفظ يحتمله وإن كان بالنظر إلى خصوصية الحاشيتين غير محتمل للكذب. ويقال لثاني القسمين: الإنشاء، وللإنشاء أقسام أمر ولهي وتمن وترجّ واستفهام ونداء. وهو ما قصد إلخ لا يقال الخبر إما أن يكون صادقا فلا يحتمل الكذب، أو كاذبا فلا يحتمل الصدق؛ لأنا نقول : المراد احتمال الصدق والكذب بحسب مفهومه وتعيين أحدهما بحسب الخارج لا ينافيه. وإن كان إلخ: يعني أن الخبر عبارة عما يحتمل الصدق والكذب بمجرد النظر إلى مفهومه مع قطع النظر عن خصوصية الحاشيتين أي عن خصوصية وقوع مدلول الكلام في نفس الأمر ولا وقوعه، وعن خصوصية المتكلم، فلا يضره تعين أحدهما بحسب الوقوع واللاوقوع ولا بحسب حال المتكلم. لثاني :القسمين: أي ما لا يقصد فيه الحكاية ولا يكون له محكي عنه أصلا، لا أن يكون له محكي عنه ولكن لا يقصد عنه الحكاية حصر الإنشاء في هذه الأقسام استقرائي. أمر إلخ الأمر: ما وضع لطلب الفعل على سبيل الاستعلاء والنهي: ما وضع لطلب الكف على سبيل الاستعلاء والتمني: طلب حصول شيء على سبيل المحبة، ولا يشترط إمكان المتمنى والترجي: طلب حصول شيء ممكن على سبيل المحبة والاستفهام: وهو ما يدل على طلب الفهم والنداء: ما وضع لطلب الإقبال.
Section: The compound is of two types: one is the complete compound (tāmm), which is that upon which it is correct to stop (i.e., to remain silent), like 'Zayd is standing'; and the second is the incomplete compound (nāqiṣ), which is not like that. Section: The complete compound is of two kinds: one is called the statement (khabar) and proposition (qaḍiyya), and it is that by which narration is intended and it admits truth and falsehood, and its speaker is said to be truthful or lying, like 'The sky is above us' and 'The world is temporally originated'. If it is said: Our statement 'There is no god but Allah' is a proposition and a statement even though it does not admit falsehood? I say: The mere wording admits it, even if, considering the particularity of the two sides, it does not admit falsehood. The second of the two kinds is called performative (inshāʼ), and performative has divisions: command (amr), prohibition (nahy), wish (tamannī), hope (tarajjī), question (istifhām), and address (nidāʼ). 'That by which narration is intended' etc. It cannot be said that the statement is either truthful so it does not admit falsehood, or false so it does not admit truth, because we say: The meaning is the admission of truth and falsehood according to its concept, and the determination of one of them according to external reality does not negate it. 'If it is' etc.: It means that the statement is an expression that admits truth and falsehood merely by looking at its concept, regardless of the particularity of the two sides, i.e., regardless of the particularity of the occurrence of the meaning of the speech in external reality or its non-occurrence, and regardless of the particularity of the speaker. So it is not harmed by the determination of one of them according to occurrence or non-occurrence, nor according to the state of the speaker. 'The second of the two kinds': i.e., that in which narration is not intended and which has no narrated content at all, not that it has narrated content but narration is not intended. The enumeration of performative into these divisions is inductive. 'Command' etc.: Command is that which is established for requesting the act with superiority; prohibition is that which is established for requesting cessation with superiority; wish is the request for obtaining something out of love, and possibility of the wished thing is not a condition; hope is the request for obtaining something possible out of love; question is that which indicates request for understanding; address is that which is established for requesting approach.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines the complete compound (al-murakkab al-tāmm) as a meaningful expression that can stand alone, like 'Zayd is standing'. It then divides it into two types: news (khabar/qaḍiyya) and performative (inshāʼ). The news is intended to convey information and admits truth and falsehood. An objection is raised: the statement 'There is no god but Allah' is a true proposition but cannot be false. The author responds that in itself, the wording can admit falsehood, even if due to external factors (its metaphysical truth) it is actually always true. The commentator expands: the admission of truth/falsehood is based on the concept alone, not on actual occurrence or the speaker's state. The performative does not aim to narrate; its divisions are given: command, prohibition, wish, hope, question, and address, each defined briefly. This classification is standard in Arabic grammar and logic. Examples include 'Zayd is standing' (news) and 'Do this' (command).

Key Terms
مركب تام
خبر (قضية)
إنشاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a performative (inshāʼ) utterance?
A) The world is temporally originated.
B) There is no god but Allah.
C) Do not commit injustice.
D) The sky is above us.

Key Takeaway
The complete compound is either a truth-apt proposition or a non-truth-apt performative; propositions admit truth/falsehood by their concept, not external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following as khabar or inshāʼ and explain your reasoning: a) 'Allah is One.' b) 'Do not consume intoxicants.' c) 'If only I had repented earlier.' d) 'Is the prayer obligatory?'
?
Explain why the statement 'There is no god but Allah' is considered a proposition despite being necessarily true, using the distinction between conceptual admission and external reality.

The Two Types of Complete Compound: Khabar vs Insha
Author distinguishes between assertive (khabar) and performative (insha) complete compounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given complete compound as khabar (assertive) or insha (performative) based on whether it admits truth and falsehood.

Core Idea
A complete compound is either truth-evaluable or performative.

This lesson introduces the first major division of complete compounds in logic: khabar (informative statement) and insha (performative speech). The passage from Mirqat defines khabar and contrasts it with insha, using examples. Understanding this distinction is essential for later work on propositions and validity in Islamic logical traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
The author states that the complete compound is of two types. List them: khabar (also called qadiyya) and insha.
2
Understand the defining feature of khabar
Khabar is intended for narration (hikāya) and admits truth and falsehood. The speaker can be called truthful or lying.
This is the key criterion.
3
Recognize the characteristic of insha
Insha is the opposite: it does not admit truth or falsehood because it is performative (e.g., commands, requests).
Insha is not detailed here but is contrasted.
4
Apply to examples
The passage gives examples of khabar: 'the sky is above us' and 'the world is originated'. Classify them yourself.
Both are clearly khabar because they can be true or false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: المركب التام ضربان يقال لأحدهما الخبر والقضية، وهو ما قصد به الحكاية ويحتمل الصدق والكذب، ويقال لقائله : إنه صادق أو كاذب، نحو: السماء فوقنا، والعالم حادث. فإن قيل: قولنا: 'لا إله إلا الله' قضية وخبر مع أنه لا يحتمل الكذب؟ قلت : مجرد اللفظ يحتمله وإن كان بالنظر إلى خصوصية الحاشيتين غير محتمل للكذب.
Chapter: The complete compound is two types: one of them is called khabar and proposition (qadiyya), which is what is intended by it narration and it admits truth and falsehood, and its speaker is said to be truthful or lying, like 'the sky is above us' and 'the world is originated'. If it is said: our saying 'There is no god but Allah' is a proposition and khabar while it does not admit falsehood? I say: the mere wording admits it, even though with consideration to the particularity of the two sides it does not admit falsehood.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of complete compound. The first is khabar (informative statement) also called qadiyya (proposition). It is characterized by being a narration (hikāya) of a state of affairs, and it can be true or false. The speaker is described as truthful or lying. Examples: 'the sky is above us' and 'the world is originated'. The author then addresses a possible objection: the statement 'There is no god but Allah' is considered a proposition and khabar, yet it cannot be false due to its content. The response clarifies that the mere wording (mujarrad al-lafẓ) considered in itself can admit falsehood, even if when considering the particularity of the two sides (i.e., the actual state of affairs and the speaker's intent) it is not false. This is because truth and falsity are assessed based on correspondence with reality, and a statement like 'La ilaha illa Allah' is necessarily true, but the possibility of falsehood is inherent in the form of the statement itself.

Key Terms
الخبر
القضية
الإنشاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a characteristic of khabar according to the passage?
A) It is a complete compound
B) It admits only truth
C) Its speaker can be called truthful or lying

Key Takeaway
A complete compound is either khabar, which can be true or false, or insha, which cannot; this distinction is foundational for logical analysis.

Necessarily True Propositions: The Case of 'La ilaha illa Allah'
Author addresses the puzzle of necessarily true propositions like 'La ilaha illa Allah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain and apply the author's reasoning that even necessarily true statements are propositions because their wording admits truth and falsehood in principle.

Core Idea
A statement's form, not its content, determines its status as a proposition.

A common confusion arises when we encounter statements that seem too true to be called propositions, like the shahada. The author of Mirqat addresses this directly, clarifying that the mere wording of such statements still admits truth and falsehood in principle, even if in reality it is always true. This lesson digs into that response and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Someone objects: 'There is no god but Allah' is a khabar, yet it cannot be false. So how can it be a proposition?
2
Analyze the author's response
The author says: the mere wording (mujarrad al-lafẓ) admits truth and falsehood, even if given the particularity of the two sides (the actual state of affairs and the speaker's intent) it does not admit falsehood.
Distinguish between the form of the statement and its actual referent.
3
Apply the distinction
Consider another statement: 'The whole is greater than the part'. It is necessarily true, but it is still a proposition because its form can be evaluated for truth.
All necessary truths are still propositions.
4
Reinforce the definition
Even if a statement is always true, it is still a khabar as long as it can be expressed in a form that is truth-evaluable.
This protects the universality of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: المركب التام ضربان يقال لأحدهما الخبر والقضية، وهو ما قصد به الحكاية ويحتمل الصدق والكذب، ويقال لقائله : إنه صادق أو كاذب، نحو: السماء فوقنا، والعالم حادث. فإن قيل: قولنا: 'لا إله إلا الله' قضية وخبر مع أنه لا يحتمل الكذب؟ قلت : مجرد اللفظ يحتمله وإن كان بالنظر إلى خصوصية الحاشيتين غير محتمل للكذب.
Chapter: The complete compound is two types: one of them is called khabar and proposition (qadiyya), which is what is intended by it narration and it admits truth and falsehood, and its speaker is said to be truthful or lying, like 'the sky is above us' and 'the world is originated'. If it is said: our saying 'There is no god but Allah' is a proposition and khabar while it does not admit falsehood? I say: the mere wording admits it, even though with consideration to the particularity of the two sides it does not admit falsehood.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of complete compound. The first is khabar (informative statement) also called qadiyya (proposition). It is characterized by being a narration (hikāya) of a state of affairs, and it can be true or false. The speaker is described as truthful or lying. Examples: 'the sky is above us' and 'the world is originated'. The author then addresses a possible objection: the statement 'There is no god but Allah' is considered a proposition and khabar, yet it cannot be false due to its content. The response clarifies that the mere wording (mujarrad al-lafẓ) considered in itself can admit falsehood, even if when considering the particularity of the two sides (i.e., the actual state of affairs and the speaker's intent) it is not false. This is because truth and falsity are assessed based on correspondence with reality, and a statement like 'La ilaha illa Allah' is necessarily true, but the possibility of falsehood is inherent in the form of the statement itself.

Key Terms
الخبر
القضية
مجرد اللفظ

Key Takeaway
A statement is a proposition if its wording can be evaluated for truth, regardless of its actual truth value.

The Definition of a Proposition (Qadiyya)
Defines proposition and its two definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two classical definitions of a proposition and distinguish their focus (the proposition itself vs. the speaker).

Core Idea
A proposition can be defined by its own truth-value or by the speaker's truthfulness.

Every logical argument rests on propositions. But what exactly is a proposition? The author gives two definitions: one focuses on the proposition itself admitting truth and falsehood, the other on the speaker being called truthful or lying. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitions
In the source Arabic, find the first definition: 'القضية قول يحتمل الصدق والكذب' and the second: 'وقيل: هو قول يقال لقائله : إنه صادق فيه أو كاذب'.
The second definition is introduced with 'وقيل'.
2
Identify the subject of each definition
The first defines the proposition itself as capable of truth/falsehood. The second defines it relative to the speaker being truthful/lying. Which attribute is primary?
The commentator prefers the first, considering truth/falsehood as attributes of the proposition.
3
Understand the mental vs. uttered distinction
The commentary explains that 'proposition' primarily refers to the mental proposition; the uttered is metaphorical. This distinction is key when analyzing actual arguments in fiqh or kalam.
This avoids confusion when a statement is not literally a proposition.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the statement 'Prayer is obligatory'. Which definition applies? Can it be true or false? Can the speaker be called truthful or lying?
Both definitions apply, but the first is more direct for logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني في الحجة وما يتعلق بها فصل في القضايا القضية قول يحتمل الصدق والكذب. وقيل: هو قول يقال لقائله : إنه صادق فيه أو كاذب وهي قسمان: حملية، وشرطية. أما الحملية فهو ما حكم فيها بثبوت شيء لشيء أو نفيه عنه كقولك: "زيد قائم، وزيد ليس بقائم". وأما الشرطية فما لا يكون فيه ذلك الحكم. وقيل: الشرطية ما ينحل إلى قضيتين، كقولنا: "إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وليس ألبتة إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود . فإذا حذف الأدوات بقي الشمس طالعة، والنهار موجود".
فصل في القضايا إلخ: لما فرغ عن مباحث القول الشارح شرع في مباحث الحجة، ولكن لما كانت لها مبادٍ يتوقف معرفتها على معرفة تلك المبادئ، وهي القضايا وأحكامها قدم الكلام في ذكرها فقال: فصل في القضايا إلخ. القضية قول إلخ : اعلم أن القضية تطلق تارة على الملفوظة وتارة على المعقولة إما بالاشتراك أو حقيقة في المعقولة ومجازا في الملفوظة. والثاني أولى؛ لأن المعتبر عندهم هو القضية المعقولة، وإطلاق القضية على الملفوظة تسمية الدال باسم المدلول وكذا لفظ القول يطلق على الملفوظ والمعقول؛ فالقول الملفوظ جنس الملفوظة، والقول المعقول جنس للقضية المعقولة. وقيل إلخ: هذا التعريف باعتبار أن الصدق والكذب وصفان للمتكلم، والأول باعتبار أنهما وصفان للقضية. وهي قسمان :إلخ اعلم أن القضية على قسمين؛ لأنها إن لم يوجد في شيء من طرفيها الدلالة على النسبة التامة فهي حملية كقولك الإنسان حيوان. وإن وجدت فإما أن توجد في أحد الطرفين أو في كليهما. فإن وجدت في أحد الطرفين فهي أيضا حملية كقولنا: زيد أبوه قائم. وإن وجدت في كليهما فإما أن تكون ملحوظة إجمالا أو تفصيلا، فإن كانت ملحوظة إجمالا فهي أيضا حملية نحو زيد عالم نقيضه زيد ليس بعالم؛ لأنه بمنزلة أن يقال: هذه القضية نقيض تلك القضية. وإن كانت ملحوظة تفصيلا فهي شرطية. كذا أفاد السيد - قدّس سرّه - وهذا أحسن الطرق في تقسيم القضية؛ فلا يرد ما أورد ما ينحل معنى انحلال القضية أن تحذف الأدوات الدالة على ارتباط أحدهما بالآخر .
The second chapter on proof and what pertains to it. Section on propositions. The proposition is a statement that admits truth and falsehood. And it is said: it is a statement about which the speaker is said to be truthful or lying. And it is two divisions: categorical and conditional. As for the categorical, it is that in which judgment is made of the affirmation of something for something or its negation, like your saying: 'Zayd is standing, and Zayd is not standing.' As for the conditional, it is that in which that judgment is not found. And it is said: the conditional is that which resolves into two propositions, like our saying: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists, and never if the sun is shining, then nighttime exists.' So when the particles are removed, what remains is 'the sun is shining' and 'daytime exists'. Section on propositions etc.: When he finished the investigations of the explanatory statement, he began the investigations of proof, but since it has principles whose knowledge depends on knowing those principles, namely propositions and their judgments, he preceded the discussion by mentioning them, saying: 'Section on propositions etc.' The proposition is a statement etc.: Know that the term 'proposition' is sometimes applied to the uttered and sometimes to the mental, either by homonymy or as a reality in the mental and metaphorically in the uttered. The second is more appropriate because what is considered by them is the mental proposition, and applying the term to the uttered is naming the indicator by the name of the indicated. Likewise, the term 'statement' is applied to the uttered and the mental. So the uttered statement is the genus of the uttered [proposition], and the mental statement is the genus of the mental proposition. 'And it is said' etc.: This definition considers that truth and falsehood are attributes of the speaker, while the first considers them as attributes of the proposition. 'And it is two divisions' etc.: Know that the proposition is of two divisions because if in none of its two ends is there an indication of a complete predication, then it is categorical, like your saying 'Man is an animal.' And if it exists, then either it exists in one of the two ends or in both. If it exists in one of the ends, then it is also categorical, like our saying: 'Zayd, his father is standing.' And if it exists in both, then either it is considered summarily or in detail. If it is considered summarily, then it is also categorical, like 'Zayd is knowledgeable; its contradictory is Zayd is not knowledgeable' because it is like saying 'This proposition is the contradictory of that proposition.' And if it is considered in detail, then it is conditional. Thus informed al-Sayyid (may his secret be sanctified), and this is the best way in dividing the proposition, so what was objected to does not arise. The meaning of 'resolves' is that the particles indicating the connection of one to the other are removed.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on proof (al-hujja) by defining the proposition (qadiyya). The main text gives two definitions: one focusing on the proposition itself as admitting truth/falsehood, another focusing on the speaker being truthful/lying. The proposition is then divided into categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). Categorical involves affirming or denying something of something (e.g., 'Zayd is standing'). Conditional does not have that direct judgment; rather it resolves into two component propositions (e.g., 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists'). The commentary explains why this section comes after definitions: the sciences of proof depend on propositions. It clarifies that 'proposition' primarily refers to the mental proposition, and the uttered proposition is metaphorically called so. The commentary then provides a more nuanced division: if neither side indicates a complete predication, it's categorical; if one side does, it's still categorical (as in 'Zayd, his father is standing'); if both sides do, it's either summarily considered (still categorical) or in detail (conditional). This refined division, attributed to al-Sayyid (likely al-Sharif al-Jurjani), is presented as the best way to divide propositions, avoiding objections about the simple definition.

قول
يحتمل الصدق والكذب

A) A statement that makes the speaker truthful or lying.

Key Takeaway
A proposition is fundamentally a statement that can be true or false, and this property belongs to the proposition itself, not primarily to the speaker.

Basic Division: Categorical vs. Conditional
Divides propositions into categorical and conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish categorical from conditional propositions and provide an example of each from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A categorical proposition directly affirms or denies; a conditional joins two complete propositions.

Once we understand what a proposition is, we need to classify it. The author divides propositions into categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). This basic division is the foundation for all complex reasoning in logic. Categorical propositions make a direct judgment, while conditionals involve a connection between two propositions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of hamliyya and shartiyya
In the source, find: 'أما الحملية فهو ما حكم فيها بثبوت شيء لشيء أو نفيه عنه' and 'أما الشرطية فما لا يكون فيه ذلك الحكم'. Understand that categorical affirms/negates one thing of another, conditional does not.
The conditional is further defined as resolving into two propositions (تنحل إلى قضيتين).
2
Examine the examples
For categorical: 'زيد قائم' (Zayd is standing). For conditional: 'إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود' (If the sun is shining, then daytime exists). Note the presence of the particle 'إن' and removal of which leaves two separate propositions.
The conditional example includes a negation too: 'وليس ألبتة إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود'.
3
Identify the key difference
A categorical proposition has a single subject-predicate structure. A conditional proposition has two component propositions linked by a conditional particle. If you remove the particles, you get two independent propositions.
This is the core distinction: direct judgment vs. compound with connection.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider 'Salat is obligatory' (categorical) and 'If one prays with sincerity, then one is rewarded' (conditional). Classify each and explain why.
Use examples from fiqh or kalam.

The Refined Division of Propositions (al-Sayyid's Method)
Refined division of propositions by al-Sayyid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply al-Sayyid's refined division to determine whether a proposition is categorical or conditional based on the presence and consideration of complete predication.

Core Idea
A proposition is conditional only when both parts contain a complete predication considered in detail.

The basic division into categorical and conditional is useful, but the commentator presents a more refined method from al-Sayyid (al-Sharif al-Jurjani). This method examines whether the two ends (subject and predicate) indicate a complete predication (nisba tamma). If neither does, it's categorical. If one does, it's still categorical. If both do but considered summarily, it remains categorical. Only when both do and are considered in detail is it conditional. This precise classification resolves many objections.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'complete predication' (nisba tamma)
Complete predication occurs when an expression alone can form a complete proposition. For example, 'Zayd is standing' has a complete predication; 'Zayd's father' does not, because 'father' needs a predicate.
This is a technical term; a proposition has two parts: subject and predicate (or in conditional, two whole propositions).
2
Examine the four cases from the commentary
The commentary lists cases: (1) Neither end has complete predication: categorical (e.g., 'Man is an animal' which is simple). (2) One end has complete predication: still categorical (e.g., 'Zayd, his father is standing' – 'Zayd' alone is incomplete, but 'his father is standing' is complete). (3) Both ends have complete predication but considered summarily: categorical (e.g., 'Zayd is knowledgeable; its contradictory is Zayd is not knowledgeable' – the two are treated as one unit). (4) Both ends have complete predication and considered in detail: conditional (e.g., 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists').
The key is how the parts are considered: summarily or in detail.
3
Apply the refined division to examples
Take 'Zayd is praying' – both 'Zayd' and 'is praying' do not have complete predication individually? Actually 'Zayd' alone is not a proposition, so this is case 1? Wait, careful: In 'Zayd is praying', the subject 'Zayd' is not a complete predication, the predicate 'is praying' is not either; the combination is. So this is categorical (case 1). But for 'Zayd, his father is standing', the part 'his father is standing' is a complete predication (it can stand alone). So this is case 2: categorical.
This method requires careful analysis of the internal structure.
4
Test with a conditional example
Consider 'If the adhan is given, then the prayer is obligatory'. Both 'the adhan is given' and 'the prayer is obligatory' are complete predications. They are considered in detail, so it is conditional.
This matches the simple definition of conditional as resolving into two propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني في الحجة وما يتعلق بها فصل في القضايا القضية قول يحتمل الصدق والكذب. وقيل: هو قول يقال لقائله : إنه صادق فيه أو كاذب وهي قسمان: حملية، وشرطية. أما الحملية فهو ما حكم فيها بثبوت شيء لشيء أو نفيه عنه كقولك: "زيد قائم، وزيد ليس بقائم". وأما الشرطية فما لا يكون فيه ذلك الحكم. وقيل: الشرطية ما ينحل إلى قضيتين، كقولنا: "إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وليس ألبتة إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود . فإذا حذف الأدوات بقي الشمس طالعة، والنهار موجود".
فصل في القضايا إلخ: لما فرغ عن مباحث القول الشارح شرع في مباحث الحجة، ولكن لما كانت لها مبادٍ يتوقف معرفتها على معرفة تلك المبادئ، وهي القضايا وأحكامها قدم الكلام في ذكرها فقال: فصل في القضايا إلخ. القضية قول إلخ : اعلم أن القضية تطلق تارة على الملفوظة وتارة على المعقولة إما بالاشتراك أو حقيقة في المعقولة ومجازا في الملفوظة. والثاني أولى؛ لأن المعتبر عندهم هو القضية المعقولة، وإطلاق القضية على الملفوظة تسمية الدال باسم المدلول وكذا لفظ القول يطلق على الملفوظ والمعقول؛ فالقول الملفوظ جنس الملفوظة، والقول المعقول جنس للقضية المعقولة. وقيل إلخ: هذا التعريف باعتبار أن الصدق والكذب وصفان للمتكلم، والأول باعتبار أنهما وصفان للقضية. وهي قسمان :إلخ اعلم أن القضية على قسمين؛ لأنها إن لم يوجد في شيء من طرفيها الدلالة على النسبة التامة فهي حملية كقولك الإنسان حيوان. وإن وجدت فإما أن توجد في أحد الطرفين أو في كليهما. فإن وجدت في أحد الطرفين فهي أيضا حملية كقولنا: زيد أبوه قائم. وإن وجدت في كليهما فإما أن تكون ملحوظة إجمالا أو تفصيلا، فإن كانت ملحوظة إجمالا فهي أيضا حملية نحو زيد عالم نقيضه زيد ليس بعالم؛ لأنه بمنزلة أن يقال: هذه القضية نقيض تلك القضية. وإن كانت ملحوظة تفصيلا فهي شرطية. كذا أفاد السيد - قدّس سرّه - وهذا أحسن الطرق في تقسيم القضية؛ فلا يرد ما أورد ما ينحل معنى انحلال القضية أن تحذف الأدوات الدالة على ارتباط أحدهما بالآخر .
The second chapter on proof and what pertains to it. Section on propositions. The proposition is a statement that admits truth and falsehood. And it is said: it is a statement about which the speaker is said to be truthful or lying. And it is two divisions: categorical and conditional. As for the categorical, it is that in which judgment is made of the affirmation of something for something or its negation, like your saying: 'Zayd is standing, and Zayd is not standing.' As for the conditional, it is that in which that judgment is not found. And it is said: the conditional is that which resolves into two propositions, like our saying: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists, and never if the sun is shining, then nighttime exists.' So when the particles are removed, what remains is 'the sun is shining' and 'daytime exists'. Section on propositions etc.: When he finished the investigations of the explanatory statement, he began the investigations of proof, but since it has principles whose knowledge depends on knowing those principles, namely propositions and their judgments, he preceded the discussion by mentioning them, saying: 'Section on propositions etc.' The proposition is a statement etc.: Know that the term 'proposition' is sometimes applied to the uttered and sometimes to the mental, either by homonymy or as a reality in the mental and metaphorically in the uttered. The second is more appropriate because what is considered by them is the mental proposition, and applying the term to the uttered is naming the indicator by the name of the indicated. Likewise, the term 'statement' is applied to the uttered and the mental. So the uttered statement is the genus of the uttered [proposition], and the mental statement is the genus of the mental proposition. 'And it is said' etc.: This definition considers that truth and falsehood are attributes of the speaker, while the first considers them as attributes of the proposition. 'And it is two divisions' etc.: Know that the proposition is of two divisions because if in none of its two ends is there an indication of a complete predication, then it is categorical, like your saying 'Man is an animal.' And if it exists, then either it exists in one of the two ends or in both. If it exists in one of the ends, then it is also categorical, like our saying: 'Zayd, his father is standing.' And if it exists in both, then either it is considered summarily or in detail. If it is considered summarily, then it is also categorical, like 'Zayd is knowledgeable; its contradictory is Zayd is not knowledgeable' because it is like saying 'This proposition is the contradictory of that proposition.' And if it is considered in detail, then it is conditional. Thus informed al-Sayyid (may his secret be sanctified), and this is the best way in dividing the proposition, so what was objected to does not arise. The meaning of 'resolves' is that the particles indicating the connection of one to the other are removed.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on proof (al-hujja) by defining the proposition (qadiyya). The main text gives two definitions: one focusing on the proposition itself as admitting truth/falsehood, another focusing on the speaker being truthful/lying. The proposition is then divided into categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). Categorical involves affirming or denying something of something (e.g., 'Zayd is standing'). Conditional does not have that direct judgment; rather it resolves into two component propositions (e.g., 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists'). The commentary explains why this section comes after definitions: the sciences of proof depend on propositions. It clarifies that 'proposition' primarily refers to the mental proposition, and the uttered proposition is metaphorically called so. The commentary then provides a more nuanced division: if neither side indicates a complete predication, it's categorical; if one side does, it's still categorical (as in 'Zayd, his father is standing'); if both sides do, it's either summarily considered (still categorical) or in detail (conditional). This refined division, attributed to al-Sayyid (likely al-Sharif al-Jurjani), is presented as the best way to divide propositions, avoiding objections about the simple definition.

ملحوظة إجمالا
ملحوظة تفصيلا

Affirmative and Negative Categorical Propositions
Author divides categorical propositions into affirmative and negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical proposition is affirmative or negative based on its relational judgment.

Core Idea
The quality of a proposition is determined by whether it affirms or denies the predicate of the subject.

Every proposition in logic has a quality: it is either affirmative or negative. This division is not arbitrary; it is based on the very nature of the judgment expressed. In this lesson, we will see how the author defines these two types and how the relational judgment (the copula) determines the quality. Understanding this will allow you to classify any categorical proposition correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the copula
Look for the verb 'to be' or its negation in the proposition (e.g., 'is' or 'is not' in translation, or the Arabic copula). The copula expresses the relational judgment.
In Arabic, the copula is often implicit but can be inferred.
2
Determine the relational judgment
If the copula affirms existence (thubut) of the predicate for the subject, the proposition is affirmative. If it negates (nafy), it is negative.
The author defines affirmative as 'judgment of something for something' and negative as 'judgment of negation of something from something'.
3
Check against the definitions
Compare the proposition to the definitions: affirmative judges existence, negative judges negation.
Apply this to the examples given: 'Man is an animal' (affirmative) and 'Man is not a horse' (negative).
4
Apply to new examples
Practise by classifying propositions from Islamic sciences, such as fiqh or kalam, using the same method.
For instance, 'The world is created' is affirmative; 'No stone is a plant' is negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل الحملية ضربان موجبة وهي التي حكم فيها بثبوت شيء لشيء. وسالبة: وهي التي حكم فيها بنفي شيء عن شيء، نحو: الإنسان حيوان، والإنسان ليس بفرس. هذا تقسيم ثانٍ للحملية باعتبار النسبة الحكمية التي هي مدلول الرابطة، فتلك النسبة إن كانت نسبة بها يصح أن يقال: "إن الموضوع محمول" كانت القضية موجبة. وإن كانت نسبة بها أن يقال: "إن الموضوع ليس محمول" فالقضية سالبة كالمثالين المذكورين في المتن.
Section: The categorical proposition is of two types: affirmative, which is that in which judgment is made of the establishment of something for something; and negative, which is that in which judgment is made of the negation of something from something, e.g., 'Man is an animal' and 'Man is not a horse'. This is a second division of the categorical proposition with respect to the relational judgment which is the meaning of the copula; that relation, if it is a relation by which it is correct to say 'the subject is a predicate', the proposition is affirmative; if it is a relation by which it is correct to say 'the subject is not a predicate', the proposition is negative, as in the two examples mentioned in the text.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents the division of categorical propositions into affirmative (mūjiba) and negative (sāliba). The author first gives the basic definitions: an affirmative judges the existence of something for something, while a negative judges the negation of something from something. Examples are provided: 'Man is an animal' (affirmative) and 'Man is not a horse' (negative). Then the commentary explains that this division is based on the relational judgment indicated by the copula. If the relation allows saying the subject is the predicate, the proposition is affirmative; if it allows saying the subject is not the predicate, it is negative. This lesson is foundational for understanding propositional quality in logic, and it connects to later discussions on opposition and conversion.

The Three Components of a Categorical Proposition
Author details the three parts of categorical proposition and the binary/ternary distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject, predicate, and copula in any categorical proposition and determine whether it is binary or ternary.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition has three logical parts, even if the copula is not spoken.

The categorical proposition is the backbone of logical reasoning. Understanding its three components — subject, predicate, and copula — allows you to analyze any statement's logical structure. This lesson also introduces the debate about a fourth part, the restrictive relation, as raised by later logicians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main components
Look for what is being judged (subject) and what is being predicated (predicate). In Arabic, these are called mawḍū‘ and maḥmūl.
The subject is that about which something is said; the predicate is what is said about it.
2
Locate the copula
Is there a linking word like 'is' or 'was'? In Arabic, it could be 'huwa', 'kāna', or be implied. The copula indicates the relation between subject and predicate.
The copula can be temporal (like 'kāna') or non-temporal (like 'huwa').
3
Consider the fourth part
Later scholars added the 'restrictive relation' (al-nisba al-taqyīdiyya) as a fourth component. Think about how this might refine the proposition's meaning.
This addition is debated; it is not universally accepted.
4
Determine binary or ternary
If the copula is explicitly mentioned, the proposition is ternary; if omitted but understood, it is binary. Arabic often omits the copula when context suffices.
Compare with Persian and Greek, which treat the copula differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحملية تلتئم من أجزاء ثلاثة أحدها المحكوم عليه، ويسمّى موضوعا، والثاني : المحكوم به ويسمّى محمولا والثالث : الدال على الرابط، ويسمّى رابطة. اعلم أن أجزاء القضية عند القدماء ثلاثة. وأما المتأخرون فإنهم يزعمون أن أجزاءها أربعة، رابعها: النسبة التقييدية التي هي مورد الحكم وتفصيل المقام في المطولات. ويسمى موضوعاً إلخ: إنما سمي المحكوم عليه موضوعا؛ لأنه قد وضع ليحكم عليه بشيء، والمحكوم به محمولا ؛ لحمله على شيء، والدال على النسبة رابطة ؛ لدلالتها على النسبة الرابطة تسمية الدال باسم المدلول. رابطة إلخ : اعلم أن الرابطة تكون أداة؛ لأنها تدل على النسبة وهي غير مستقلة لتوقفها على المحكوم به، لكنها قد تكون في قالب الاسم كـ "هو" في "زيد" هو قائم"، وتسمّى غير زمانية، وقد تكون في قالب الكلمة كـ"كان" ففي قولك: "زيد" كان قائما، وتسمّى زمانية. ويرد عليه أن "هو"، وضع المعنى كما أجمع عليه أهل العربية، فلا يكون رابطة ؟ والجواب ما قال التفتازاني: إن المنطقيين لما لم يجدوا في كلام العرب لفظا دالا على الرابط الغير الزماني نحو "است" في الفارسية و"استن" في اليونانية، استعاروا لهذا المعنى لفظة "هو". في الأصل وضع لمعنى اسمي كسائر الضمائر، ثم نقل عنه إلى معنى غير مستقل بالمفهومية على سبيل الاستعارة. والحملية باعتبار الرابطة تكون ثنائية أو ثلاثية؛ لأنها إن ذكرت فيها الرابطة كانت ثلاثية لاشتمالها على ثلاثة ألفاظ لثلاثة معانٍ. وإن حذفت لشعور الذهن بمعناها كانت ثنائية ؛ لعدم اشتمالها إلا على جزئين بإزاء معنيين. وقد تحذف إلخ: هذا في لغة العرب؛ فإنها قد تحذف بشهادة القرائن الدالة عليها. وأما لغة اليونان فإنها توجب ذكر الرابطة الزمانية دون غيرها على ما نقله الشيخ وأما لغة العجم فإنها لا تستعمل القضية خالية عنها، إما بلفظ كقولهم: "بست وبود"، وإما بحركة كقولهم: "زيد" "دبير" بالكسر.
The categorical proposition consists of three parts: one is the subject of judgment, called subject; second is the predicate of judgment, called predicate; third is that which indicates the copula, called copula. Know that according to the ancients, the parts of a proposition are three. As for the later scholars, they claim that its parts are four, the fourth being the restrictive relation which is the locus of judgment, and the detailed discussion is in the lengthy books. 'Called subject' etc.: The subject of judgment is called subject because it is placed to be judged about something, the predicate of judgment is called predicate because it is predicated of something, and that which indicates the relation is called copula because it indicates the relation, naming the indicator by the name of the indicated. 'Copula' etc.: Know that the copula is a particle because it indicates the relation and is not independent due to its dependence on the predicate, but it may be in the form of a noun like 'huwa' in 'Zaid is standing', called non-temporal, and it may be in the form of a verb like 'kāna' in your saying 'Zaid was standing', called temporal. It is objected that 'huwa' has a notional meaning according to the consensus of Arabic grammarians, so it cannot be a copula. The answer is what al-Taftazani said: Since the logicians did not find in the Arabic language a word indicating the non-temporal copula like 'ast' in Persian and 'estin' in Greek, they borrowed the word 'huwa' for this meaning. Originally it was used for a notional meaning like other pronouns, then it was transferred to a meaning that is not independent in concept by way of metaphor. The categorical proposition, with respect to the copula, is either binary or ternary: because if the copula is mentioned, it is ternary, containing three words for three meanings; and if it is omitted due to the mind's comprehension of its meaning, it is binary, containing only two parts corresponding to two meanings. 'And it may be omitted' etc.: This is in the Arabic language; it may be omitted when there are contextual indicators. As for the Greek language, it requires the mention of the temporal copula only, as reported by the Shaykh. As for the Persian language, it does not use the proposition free from it, either with a word like 'bast' and 'bud', or with a vowel like in 'Zaid dabīr' with a kasra.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage explains the three components of a categorical proposition: subject (mawḍū‘), predicate (maḥmūl), and copula (rābiṭa). The author first states the standard view from the ancients that the proposition has three parts, noting that later scholars add a fourth part (the restrictive relation). Then he provides the rationale for naming each part: the subject is that which is placed for judgment, the predicate is carried onto something, and the copula indicates the relation. The discussion then turns to the copula itself: it is a particle indicating the relation and is dependent on the predicate. It can be non-temporal (like 'huwa') or temporal (like 'kāna'). An objection is raised: 'huwa' is a pronoun with a notional meaning according to Arabic grammar, so it shouldn't serve as a copula. The answer, from al-Taftazani, is that logicians borrowed 'huwa' from common usage metaphorically because Arabic lacks a dedicated word for the non-temporal copula, unlike Persian ('ast') and Greek ('estin'). Finally, the passage discusses binary vs. ternary propositions based on whether the copula is explicit: Arabic may omit the copula when context gives the meaning, while Greek requires the temporal copula, and Persian always includes either a word or a vowel. This lesson guards against misunderstanding the linguistic nature of the copula in Arabic logic, and it emphasizes the difference between the logical function and the grammatical form.

رابطة

The Nature of the Copula and the Borrowing of 'Huwa'
Author discusses the copula, its types, the objection to 'huwa', and al-Taftazani's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between temporal and non-temporal copulae and describe why Arabic logicians borrowed 'huwa' as a copula.

Core Idea
Arabic logicians borrowed 'huwa' for the copula because the language lacked a dedicated non-temporal copula word.

The copula is the glue that binds subject and predicate. In Arabic, the copula can be temporal (like 'kāna') or non-temporal (like 'huwa'). But 'huwa' is originally a pronoun — how did it become a copula? Al-Taftazani explains that logicians borrowed it metaphorically. This lesson explores that linguistic adaptation and how different languages handle the copula.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of copula
A temporal copula (like 'kāna') indicates a time-bound relation; a non-temporal copula (like 'huwa') indicates a timeless, essential linking. Recognize which is used in a given proposition.
The passage calls these 'zamāniyya' and 'ghayr zamāniyya'.
2
Identify the linguistic problem
Arabic has no dedicated word for the non-temporal copula, unlike Persian ('ast') and Greek ('estin'). This creates a gap for logicians who need a term for the timeless copula.
The Greek 'estin' and Persian 'ast' are pure copulae, not burdened with other meanings.
3
Understand al-Taftazani's solution
Logicians borrowed the common pronoun 'huwa' (he) and repurposed it metaphorically to serve as the non-temporal copula. This is justified by the need for a technical term.
The objection from grammarians is that 'huwa' has a notional meaning (ḍamīr), but the logicians override this by metaphorical transfer.
4
Compare with other languages
Arabic may omit the copula if context is clear; Greek requires the temporal copula; Persian always includes a copula either as a word or a vowel ending. Recognize these variations.
This shows that the treatment of the copula is language-dependent, not universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحملية تلتئم من أجزاء ثلاثة أحدها المحكوم عليه، ويسمّى موضوعا، والثاني : المحكوم به ويسمّى محمولا والثالث : الدال على الرابط، ويسمّى رابطة. اعلم أن أجزاء القضية عند القدماء ثلاثة. وأما المتأخرون فإنهم يزعمون أن أجزاءها أربعة، رابعها: النسبة التقييدية التي هي مورد الحكم وتفصيل المقام في المطولات. ويسمى موضوعاً إلخ: إنما سمي المحكوم عليه موضوعا؛ لأنه قد وضع ليحكم عليه بشيء، والمحكوم به محمولا ؛ لحمله على شيء، والدال على النسبة رابطة ؛ لدلالتها على النسبة الرابطة تسمية الدال باسم المدلول. رابطة إلخ : اعلم أن الرابطة تكون أداة؛ لأنها تدل على النسبة وهي غير مستقلة لتوقفها على المحكوم به، لكنها قد تكون في قالب الاسم كـ "هو" في "زيد" هو قائم"، وتسمّى غير زمانية، وقد تكون في قالب الكلمة كـ"كان" ففي قولك: "زيد" كان قائما، وتسمّى زمانية. ويرد عليه أن "هو"، وضع المعنى كما أجمع عليه أهل العربية، فلا يكون رابطة ؟ والجواب ما قال التفتازاني: إن المنطقيين لما لم يجدوا في كلام العرب لفظا دالا على الرابط الغير الزماني نحو "است" في الفارسية و"استن" في اليونانية، استعاروا لهذا المعنى لفظة "هو". في الأصل وضع لمعنى اسمي كسائر الضمائر، ثم نقل عنه إلى معنى غير مستقل بالمفهومية على سبيل الاستعارة. والحملية باعتبار الرابطة تكون ثنائية أو ثلاثية؛ لأنها إن ذكرت فيها الرابطة كانت ثلاثية لاشتمالها على ثلاثة ألفاظ لثلاثة معانٍ. وإن حذفت لشعور الذهن بمعناها كانت ثنائية ؛ لعدم اشتمالها إلا على جزئين بإزاء معنيين. وقد تحذف إلخ: هذا في لغة العرب؛ فإنها قد تحذف بشهادة القرائن الدالة عليها. وأما لغة اليونان فإنها توجب ذكر الرابطة الزمانية دون غيرها على ما نقله الشيخ وأما لغة العجم فإنها لا تستعمل القضية خالية عنها، إما بلفظ كقولهم: "بست وبود"، وإما بحركة كقولهم: "زيد" "دبير" بالكسر.
The categorical proposition consists of three parts: one is the subject of judgment, called subject; second is the predicate of judgment, called predicate; third is that which indicates the copula, called copula. Know that according to the ancients, the parts of a proposition are three. As for the later scholars, they claim that its parts are four, the fourth being the restrictive relation which is the locus of judgment, and the detailed discussion is in the lengthy books. 'Called subject' etc.: The subject of judgment is called subject because it is placed to be judged about something, the predicate of judgment is called predicate because it is predicated of something, and that which indicates the relation is called copula because it indicates the relation, naming the indicator by the name of the indicated. 'Copula' etc.: Know that the copula is a particle because it indicates the relation and is not independent due to its dependence on the predicate, but it may be in the form of a noun like 'huwa' in 'Zaid is standing', called non-temporal, and it may be in the form of a verb like 'kāna' in your saying 'Zaid was standing', called temporal. It is objected that 'huwa' has a notional meaning according to the consensus of Arabic grammarians, so it cannot be a copula. The answer is what al-Taftazani said: Since the logicians did not find in the Arabic language a word indicating the non-temporal copula like 'ast' in Persian and 'estin' in Greek, they borrowed the word 'huwa' for this meaning. Originally it was used for a notional meaning like other pronouns, then it was transferred to a meaning that is not independent in concept by way of metaphor. The categorical proposition, with respect to the copula, is either binary or ternary: because if the copula is mentioned, it is ternary, containing three words for three meanings; and if it is omitted due to the mind's comprehension of its meaning, it is binary, containing only two parts corresponding to two meanings. 'And it may be omitted' etc.: This is in the Arabic language; it may be omitted when there are contextual indicators. As for the Greek language, it requires the mention of the temporal copula only, as reported by the Shaykh. As for the Persian language, it does not use the proposition free from it, either with a word like 'bast' and 'bud', or with a vowel like in 'Zaid dabīr' with a kasra.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage explains the three components of a categorical proposition: subject (mawḍū‘), predicate (maḥmūl), and copula (rābiṭa). The author first states the standard view from the ancients that the proposition has three parts, noting that later scholars add a fourth part (the restrictive relation). Then he provides the rationale for naming each part: the subject is that which is placed for judgment, the predicate is carried onto something, and the copula indicates the relation. The discussion then turns to the copula itself: it is a particle indicating the relation and is dependent on the predicate. It can be non-temporal (like 'huwa') or temporal (like 'kāna'). An objection is raised: 'huwa' is a pronoun with a notional meaning according to Arabic grammar, so it shouldn't serve as a copula. The answer, from al-Taftazani, is that logicians borrowed 'huwa' from common usage metaphorically because Arabic lacks a dedicated word for the non-temporal copula, unlike Persian ('ast') and Greek ('estin'). Finally, the passage discusses binary vs. ternary propositions based on whether the copula is explicit: Arabic may omit the copula when context gives the meaning, while Greek requires the temporal copula, and Persian always includes either a word or a vowel. This lesson guards against misunderstanding the linguistic nature of the copula in Arabic logic, and it emphasizes the difference between the logical function and the grammatical form.

Key Terms
رابطة
غير زمانية
زمانية
استعارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because Arabic lacks a word for the non-temporal copula
C) Because grammarians recommended it
D) Because Greek uses a similar word

Key Takeaway
The Arabic non-temporal copula 'huwa' is a metaphorical borrowing, demonstrating how logical terminology adapts to linguistic gaps.

Anatomy of a Conditional Proposition
Author defines the conditional proposition and names its parts: antecedent and consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent and consequent in any conditional proposition from Islamic legal or theological texts.

Core Idea
Every conditional has two parts: the condition (antecedent) and the result (consequent).

In logic, a conditional proposition connects two statements with 'if...then'. This structure appears throughout Islamic sciences — from legal rulings to theological arguments. This lesson teaches you to recognize its two essential parts: the antecedent (muqaddam) and the consequent (tālī).

Guided Path
1
1. Locate the conditional connective
Find the word 'if' (in Arabic 'in' or 'idhā') that indicates the condition. The clause immediately following it is the antecedent.
In other languages, the connective might be 'if', 'whenever', etc.
2
2. Identify the antecedent
The antecedent (muqaddam) is the clause that states the condition. It comes first after 'if'.
Example: In 'If the sun has risen, then daytime exists', the antecedent is 'the sun has risen'.
3
3. Identify the consequent
The consequent (tālī) is the clause that follows the condition and states the result. It usually comes after 'then'.
In the same example, the consequent is 'daytime exists'.
4
4. Practice with an Islamic science example
Take a conditional from usul al-fiqh: 'If the cause ('illah) is present, then the ruling applies.' Identify the antecedent ('the cause is present') and consequent ('the ruling applies').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للشرطية أيضا أجزاء، ويسمّى الجزء الأول منها مقدَّما، والجزء الثاني منها تاليا، ففي قولك: "إن كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودا، قولك: "إن كانت الشمس طالعة مقدَّم، وقولك: "كان" النهار موجودا تال، والرابطة هي الحكم بينهما.
The conditional proposition also has parts, and the first part is called antecedent, and the second part is called consequent. For example, in your saying: 'If the sun has risen, then daytime exists', your saying 'If the sun has risen' is the antecedent, and your saying 'daytime exists' is the consequent, and the copula is the judgment between them.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage introduces the conditional proposition (al-sharṭiyya) and its two parts: the antecedent (muqaddam) and the consequent (tālī). The example given is the conditional 'If the sun has risen, then daytime exists', where 'If the sun has risen' is the antecedent and 'daytime exists' is the consequent. The copula in a conditional is the judgment of implication that connects them. This lesson is foundational for understanding the structure of hypothetical syllogisms and their analysis in logic.

Key Terms
شرطية (shartiyya)
مقدم (muqaddam)
تالي (tālī)
رابطة (rābiṭa)

Key Takeaway
Every conditional has a distinct antecedent and consequent; recognizing them is the first step in analyzing conditional arguments.

Division of Propositions by Subject: Particular, Universal, and Natural
Author classifies categorical propositions based on whether the subject is particular, universal, or natural.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each type of proposition (singular, natural, quantified) based on its subject and distinguish natural propositions from quantified ones.

Core Idea
A natural proposition judges the essence itself, not its individuals – it is neither universal nor particular.

In this lesson, we learn how logicians classify propositions based on the subject term. The subject may be a specific individual, a universal nature judged in itself, or the individuals of a universal. This distinction is crucial for understanding how propositions about essences (like definitions) differ from quantified statements about instances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject term
Look at the subject of the proposition. Is it a specific individual (like Zaid) or a general term (like man, animal)?
2
Determine if the subject is particular or universal
If the subject is a specific individual, the proposition is singular (shakhṣiyya). If the subject is a universal term (kullī), proceed.
3
Check the nature of the judgment
Does the proposition predicate something of the essence itself (e.g., 'Man is a species') or of the individuals of that essence (e.g., 'All men are standing')?
4
Classify accordingly
If the judgment is on the essence, it is a natural proposition (ṭabī‘iyya). If on individuals, it is a quantified proposition (universal or particular).
5
Test with examples
Practice with examples from logic and Islamic sciences to solidify the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: وقد تقسم القضية باعتبار الموضوع، فالموضوع إن كان جزئيا وشخصا معينا سميت القضية شخصية ومخصوصة كقولك: زيد قائم . وإن لم يكن جزئيا، بل كان كليا؛ فهو على أنحاء؛ لأنها إن كان الحكم فيها على نفس الحقيقة تسمّى القضية طبعية نحو الإنسان ،نوع والحيوان .جنس. وإن كان على أفرادها فلا يخلو... طبعية إلخ إنما سمّي طبعية؛ لأن الحكم فيها على نفس الطبعية لا على الأفراد؛ ولذا لا يصلح لأن يصدق كلية وجزئية.
Section: And the proposition is sometimes divided with respect to the subject. If the subject is particular and a specific individual, the proposition is called individual and specific, like your saying: 'Zaid is standing'. If it is not particular but rather universal, then it varies. For if the judgment is about the essence itself, the proposition is called natural, e.g., 'Man is a species' and 'Animal is a genus'. If it is about its individuals, then ... 'Natural' etc.: It is called natural because the judgment is about the nature itself, not about individuals; therefore it cannot be considered universal or particular.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage classifies categorical propositions based on the nature of the subject. The subject may be particular (juz’ī) and specific (shakhṣī), yielding a particular/singular proposition (e.g., 'Zaid is standing'). If the subject is universal (kullī), two further types arise. First, if the judgment is about the essence itself (dhāt al-ḥaqīqa), the proposition is called 'natural' (ṭabī‘iyya), as in 'Man is a species' or 'Animal is a genus' — these are statements about the nature of man or animal regardless of individuals. Second, if the judgment concerns the individuals of that universal, then the proposition may be either universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz’iyya) in the technical sense, but the text here is cut off. The commentary clarifies that a natural proposition is called such because the judgment is on the nature itself, not on individuals, and therefore it cannot be classified as universal or particular in the usual sense (i.e., it does not distribute over individuals). This lesson is important for understanding how logicians treat statements about essences versus statements about instances.

طبعية
كلي

Parts of Categorical and Conditional Propositions
Defines parts of categorical and conditional propositions, with commentary on copula

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject, predicate, and copula in a categorical proposition, and the antecedent and consequent in a conditional proposition, and explain the metaphorical nature of the copula 'huwa' in Arabic logic.

Core Idea
The copula in Arabic logic is borrowed from the pronoun 'huwa' because Arabic lacks a dedicated copula.

In this lesson, we break down the proposition into its logical components. Categorical propositions have three parts: subject, predicate, and copula, while conditionals have two parts: antecedent and consequent. A key insight is that the Arabic word 'huwa' functions as a copula by metaphorical extension, a point clarified by al-Taftazani in response to a linguistic objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate in a categorical proposition
Look for the term being judged (subject) and what is judged about it (predicate). In 'Zayd is standing', 'Zayd' is the subject, 'standing' is the predicate.
2
Locate the copula
In 'Zayd is standing', the copula is 'huwa'. It links subject and predicate. Note that in Arabic it may be omitted but is understood, as in 'Zayd qa'im'.
3
Identify the antecedent and consequent in a conditional
In 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists', the antecedent is 'the sun is shining' and the consequent is 'daytime exists'. The relation between them is the copula.
4
Understand the copula issue
'Huwa' is originally a pronoun with independent meaning. Logicians borrowed it metaphorically because Arabic lacks a non-temporal copula. Compare with Persian 'ast' and Greek 'estin'.
This addresses a potential objection: how can a pronoun serve as a mere relation?
5
Recognize ternary vs binary forms
When the copula is explicit, the proposition is ternary (three words for three meanings). When omitted, it is binary but the copula is understood.
Arabic allows omission due to contextual clues; Greek and Persian require it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففي قولك: "زيد" هو "قائم" "زيد" محكوم عليه وموضوع، و"قائم" محكوم به ومحمول ولفظة "هو" نسبة ورابطة. وقد تحذف الرابطة في اللفظ دون المراد؛ فيقال: "زيد قائم". فصل : للشرطية أيضا أجزاء، ويسمّى الجزء الأول منها مقدَّما، والجزء الثاني منها تاليا، ففي قولك: "إن كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودا، قولك: "إن كانت الشمس طالعة مقدَّم، وقولك: "كان" النهار موجودا تال، والرابطة هي الحكم بينهما. اسمي في قولنا "زيد" كان قائما، وتسمّى زمانية. ويرد عليه أن "هو"، وضع المعنى كما أجمع عليه أهل العربية، فلا يكون رابطة ؟ والجواب ما قال التفتازاني: إن المنطقيين لما لم يجدوا في كلام العرب لفظا دالا على الرابط الغير الزماني نحو "است" في الفارسية و"استن" في اليونانية، استعاروا لهذا المعنى لفظة "هو". في الأصل موضوع لمعنى اسمي كسائر الضمائر، ثم نقل عنه إلى معنى غير مستقل بالمفهومية على سبيل الاستعارة. والحملية باعتبار الرابطة تكون ثنائية أو ثلاثية؛ لأنها إن ذكرت فيها الرابطة كانت ثُلاثية لاشتمالها على ثلاثة ألفاظ لثلاثة معانٍ. وإن حذفت لشعور الذهن بمعناها كانت ثنائية ؛ لعدم اشتمالها إلا على جزئين بإزاء معنيين. وقد تحذف إلخ: هذا في لغة العرب؛ فإنها قد تحذف بشهادة القرائن الدالة عليها. وأما لغة اليونان فإنها توجب ذكر الرابطة الزمانية دون غيرها على ما نقله الشيخ وأما لغة العجم فإنها لا تستعمل القضية خالية عنها، إما بلفظ كقولهم: "بست وبود"، وإما بحر ، وإما بحركة كقولهم: "زيد" "دبير" بالكسر. مقدَّما: أي القضية الأولى من الشرطية تسمّى مقدما لتقدمها في الذكر، والثانية تاليا لتلوّها إياها
In your statement: 'Zayd is standing', 'Zayd' is the subject of judgment and the subject, and 'standing' is the predicate and the predicated, and the word 'is' (huwa) is a relation and a copula. And the copula may be omitted in pronunciation without omitting it from the intended meaning; so it is said: 'Zayd standing'. Section: The conditional also has parts; the first part is called the antecedent (muqaddam), and the second part is called the consequent (tali). So in your statement: 'If the sun is shining then daytime exists', your statement 'If the sun is shining' is the antecedent, and your statement 'daytime exists' is the consequent, and the copula is the judgment between them. [Commentary:] The nominal [copula] in our statement 'Zayd was standing' is called temporal. It is objected against this that 'huwa' is placed for an independent meaning as agreed upon by the Arabic scholars, so it cannot be a relational copula? The answer is what al-Taftazani said: The logicians, since they did not find in Arabic speech a word indicating the non-temporal copula like 'ast' in Persian and 'estin' in Greek, borrowed for this meaning the word 'huwa'. Originally it was placed for a nominal meaning like other pronouns, then it was transferred from it to a meaning not independent in conceptuality by way of metaphor. The categorical proposition, with respect to the copula, is either binary or ternary; because if the copula is mentioned it is ternary, since it includes three words for three meanings. And if it is omitted due to the mind's awareness of its meaning, it is binary, since it includes only two parts corresponding to two meanings. 'And it may be omitted etc.': This is in the Arabic language; for it may be omitted due to the presence of contextual clues indicating it. As for the Greek language, it requires mentioning the temporal copula only, according to what the Shaykh transmitted. And as for the Persian language, it never uses the proposition devoid of it, either with a word like 'bist wa bud', or with a vowel, or with a movement like their saying 'Zayd dabir' with a kasra. 'Muqaddam': That is, the first proposition of the conditional is called muqaddam because it precedes in mention, and the second is called tali because it follows it.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of the parts of propositions, both categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). The author first identifies the three components of a categorical proposition: the subject (mahkum 'alayh), the predicate (mahkum bih), and the copula (rabita) which joins them, exemplified by the word 'huwa' in 'Zayd is standing'. He notes that the copula may be omitted in Arabic, as in 'Zayd qa'im'. Then he introduces the conditional proposition, which consists of the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali), with the relation (al-hukm baynahuma) serving as the copula. The commentary, attributed to al-Taftazani, addresses a critical objection: the word 'huwa' in Arabic is a pronoun with an independent meaning, so how can it function as a mere copula? The answer is that logicians borrowed 'huwa' metaphorically to fill a gap in Arabic, since Arabic lacks a dedicated copula word like Persian 'ast' or Greek 'estin'. The commentary further clarifies the ternary (with copula) and binary (without copula) forms of categorical propositions, and contrasts Arabic's optional omission of the copula with Greek and Persian obligatory usage. Finally, the terms 'muqaddam' and 'tali' are defined based on order of mention. The teacher should emphasize that the copula is a relational term, not an independent entity, and that understanding this distinction is foundational for logical analysis.

Key Terms
محكوم عليه (mahkum 'alayh): The subject of a categorical proposition, that which is judged upon.
محكوم به (mahkum bih): The predicate of a categorical proposition, that which is judged.
رابطة (rabita): The copula linking subject and predicate; in Arabic, often the word 'huwa' used metaphorically. [Note: In later texts, especially in translation philosophy, the copula is more strictly identified with temporal verbs.]
مقدَّم (muqaddam): The antecedent in a conditional proposition, the first part.
تال (tali): The consequent in a conditional proposition, the second part.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is a metaphorical copula loaned from the meaning of a pronoun.
C) It is a temporal verb indicating past tense.
D) It is a predicate describing Zayd.

Key Takeaway
The parts of a proposition are essential for logical analysis; the copula, though sometimes omitted, is a relational component, and its Arabic expression 'huwa' is a metaphorical borrowing from the pronoun.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
