Personal, Natural, and Universal Propositions
Classifies propositions by subject into personal, natural, and universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between personal, natural, and universal propositions based on the subject's nature and the expression of quantity.

Core Idea
The subject type and quantity expression determine the proposition's classification.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified according to their subject. This lesson walks you through the three main categories: personal (shakhsiyya), natural (tabi'iyya), and universal with its two subtypes: quantified (mahsura) and unquantified (muhmala). Understanding this classification is essential for evaluating the scope of logical statements in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject type
Determine if the subject is a specific individual (e.g., 'Zayd') or a universal term. If it is a specific individual, the proposition is personal (shakhsiyya).
2
Distinguish natural from universal
If the subject is universal, check whether the judgment is on the nature itself (natural) or on its individuals (universal). The natural proposition judges the essence, e.g., 'Man is a species'.
Natural propositions are neither universal nor particular.
3
Check for expression of quantity
For universal propositions on individuals, see if the quantity (all, some, none) is explicitly stated. If yes, it is quantified (mahsura); if not, it is unquantified (muhmala).
4
Test with examples
Apply these criteria to the examples: 'Zayd is standing' (personal), 'Man is a species' (natural), 'Every human is an animal' (quantified), 'Man is in loss' (unquantified).

فصل : وقد تقسم القضية باعتبار الموضوع، فالموضوع إن كان جزئيا وشخصا معينا سميت القضية شخصية ومخصوصة كقولك: زيد قائم . وإن لم يكن جزئيا، بل كان كليا؛ فهو على أنحاء؛ لأنها إن كان الحكم فيها على نفس الحقيقة تسمّى القضية طبعية نحو الإنسان ،نوع والحيوان .جنس. وإن كان على أفرادها فلا يخلو إما أن يكون كمّية الأفراد فيها مبينا، أو لم يكن. فإن بين كمية الأفراد تسمّى القضية محصورة كقولك: "كل إنسان حيوان وبعض الحيوان إنسان". وإن لم يُبين يسمّى القضية مهملة نحو الإنسان في خسر. فصل المحصورات أربع إحداها الموجبة الكلية كقولك: "كل إنسان حيوان". والثانية الموجبة الجزئية نحو: "بعض الحيوان أسود والثالثة السالبة الكلية نحو: "لا شيء من الزنجي بأبيض. والرابعة: السالبة الجزئية، نحو: "بعض الإنسان ليس بأسود". فصل: الذي يُبيّن به كمية الأفراد من الكلية والبعضية يسمّى سُورا، وهو مأخوذ من سور البلد وسور الموجبة الكلية "كل، ولام الاستغراق"، وسور الموجبة الجزئية "بعض، وواحد من الجسم جماد. وسور السالبة الكلية "لا شيء، ولا واحد" نحو: لاشيء من الغراب بأبيض، ولا واحد من النار ببارد

إن كان جزئيا لم يقل: "علما" ليشمل "هذا" عالم" و "أنا قائم" وأمثالهما؛ ولأن العلم لا يكون إلا لفظا ظاهرا، فلو قال: "علما يفهم حصر القضية الشخصية في الملفوظة. شخصية ومخصوصة إلخ أما تسميتها شخصية؛ فلأن موضوعها شخص معين، وأما مخصوصة فلخصوص موضوعها. طبعية إلخ إنما سمي طبعية؛ لأن الحكم فيها على نفس الطبعية لا على الأفراد؛ ولذا لا يصلح لأن يصدق كلية وجزئية. محصورة إلخ: إنما سميت محصورة؛ لحصر أفراد موضوعها ويقال: مسوَّرة أيضا؛ لاشتمالها على السور. مهملة إلخ إنما سميت بها ؛ لأن الحكم فيها على الأفراد، وقد أهمل بيان كميتها، وهي تلازم الجزئية عند المتأخرين. وأما مهملة القدماء ففي كونه متلازما للجزئية كلام مشروح في شروح تصديقات "السلّم" وغيرها، فليراجع ثَمّه. المحصورات أربع إلخ لأن الحكم فيها إما بالإيجاب أو بالسلب وعلى التقديرين إما على كل الأفراد أو على بعضها. فإن حكم بالإيجاب على كل الأفراد فموجبة كلية وإن حكم بالإيجاب على بعضها فموجبة جزئية، وإن حكم بالسلب على كلها فسالبة كلية، وإن حكم بالسلب على بعضها فسالبة جزئية. اعلم أن أهم المهمات في هذا الباب تحقيق المحصورات؛ لابتناء معرفة الحجج التي المطلب الأعلى من هذا الفن عليها، ووقوع الخبط العظيم لسبب الغفلة عنها، وإنما وقع البداية بتحقيق الموجبة الكلية لشرفها وتأدية معرفتها إلى إدراك البواقي بالمقايسة. يسمّى سُورا إلخ مأخوذ من "سور البلد" كما أنه يحصر البلد ويحيط به، كذلك اللفظ الدال على كمية الأفراد يحصرها ويحيط بها.
Section: The proposition may also be divided with respect to the subject. If the subject is particular and a specific individual, the proposition is called personal (shakhsiyya) and specific (makhsusa), like your saying: 'Zayd is standing'. And if it is not particular but rather universal, then it is of various kinds; because if the judgment is on the nature itself, the proposition is called natural (tabi'iyya), like 'Man is a species' and 'Animal is a genus'. And if it is on its individuals, then it is either that the quantity of individuals is expressed, or not. If the quantity of individuals is expressed, the proposition is called quantified (mahsura), like your saying: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some animal is a human'. And if it is not expressed, the proposition is called unquantified (muhmala), like 'Man is in loss'. Section: The quantified propositions are four: first, the universal affirmative, like your saying: 'Every human is an animal'. Second, the particular affirmative, like: 'Some animal is black'. Third, the universal negative, like: 'No Zanj is white'. Fourth, the particular negative, like: 'Some human is not black'. Section: That by which the quantity of individuals, universality and partiality, is expressed is called a quantifier (sur), taken from the wall of a city. The quantifier of the universal affirmative is 'every' and the particle of generality; the quantifier of the particular affirmative is 'some' and 'one of' like 'one body is inanimate'; the quantifier of the universal negative is 'none' and 'not one', like 'No raven is white' and 'Not one fire is cold'. [Commentary:] '[If it is] particular, he did not say 'knowledge' to include 'this is a scholar' and 'I am standing' and their like; and because knowledge is only a manifest word, if he said 'knowledge' it would be understood as restricting the personal proposition to the pronounced. 'Personal and specific etc.': As for its being called personal, because its subject is a specific person; and specific, because of the specificity of its subject. 'Natural etc.': It is called natural only because the judgment is on the nature itself, not on the individuals; therefore it is not fit to be considered universal or particular. 'Quantified etc.': It is called quantified only because it confines the individuals of its subject; and it is also called musawwara because it contains the quantifier. 'Unquantified etc.': It is called thus because the judgment is on the individuals, and its quantity has been neglected; and it is concomitant with the particular among the later scholars. As for the unquantified of the ancients, there is discussion about its concomitancy with the particular in the commentaries on the tasdiqat of al-Sullam and others, so refer there. 'The quantified are four etc.': Because the judgment is either by affirmation or negation, and in both cases it is either on all individuals or on some of them. If it judges by affirmation on all individuals, it is universal affirmative; if by affirmation on some, it is particular affirmative; if by negation on all, it is universal negative; if by negation on some, it is particular negative. Know that the most important matters in this chapter are the verification of the quantified propositions, because upon it is built the knowledge of the arguments which are the highest aim of this art, and great error occurs due to neglect of them. And he began with the verification of the universal affirmative because of its nobility and because knowing it leads to knowing the rest by comparison. 'Is called a quantifier etc.': Taken from 'the wall of a city', just as it confines and surrounds the city, so the word indicating the quantity of individuals confines and surrounds them.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a classification of propositions based on the nature of the subject and the expression of quantity. It begins by dividing propositions into personal (shakhsiyya) where the subject is a specific individual, natural (tabi'iyya) where the judgment is on the essence itself, and universal where the judgment is on individuals. The universal is further divided into quantified (mahsura) when the quantity is explicitly stated, and unquantified (muhmala) when it is omitted. The commentary clarifies why the term 'personal' is used and distinguishes it from 'specific'. It also explains that the natural proposition judges the nature itself, so it is neither universal nor particular. The text then enumerates the four quantified propositions: universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every human is an animal'), particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some animal is black'), universal negative (e.g., 'No Zanj is white'), and particular negative (e.g., 'Some human is not black'). The quantifiers (sur) are defined, with examples: 'kull' and 'lam al-istighraq' for universal affirmative, 'ba'd' and 'wahid min' for particular affirmative, and 'la shay' and 'la wahid' for universal negative. The commentary emphasizes the importance of understanding quantified propositions, as they are fundamental for constructing valid arguments. It also notes the difference between the later scholars' view that unquantified propositions are treated as particular and the earlier scholars' different position. The teacher should stress that mastering the four types of quantified propositions is essential for logical reasoning in Islamic sciences, such as in legal analogies where universal premises are used.

Propositions
intermediate
8 min
The Four Quantified Propositions and Their Quantifiers
Enumerates the four quantified propositions with their quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of quantified propositions (mahsura) and match each with its correct quantifier.

Core Idea
The four quantified propositions cover all combinations of affirmation/negation and universality/particularity.

This lesson dives into the four quantified propositions that are the building blocks of syllogistic logic. Each is defined by its quality (affirmative or negative) and quantity (universal or particular). You will learn the quantifiers (sur) that signal each type and see examples from the text. Mastery of these is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish quality (affirmative vs negative)
Check if the proposition asserts (affirms) or denies something about the subject. Affirmative uses 'is' or 'are'; negative uses 'is not' or 'none'.
2
Distinguish quantity (universal vs particular)
Determine if the judgment applies to all individuals (universal) or only some (particular). Quantifiers like 'every' (kull) indicate universal; 'some' (ba'd) indicate particular.
3
Combine to get four types
The combination yields: universal affirmative (all + affirmative), particular affirmative (some + affirmative), universal negative (none + negative), particular negative (some + negative).
4
Identify quantifiers
For universal affirmative: 'kull' or 'lam al-istighraq'. For particular affirmative: 'ba'd' or 'wahid min'. For universal negative: 'la shay' or 'la wahid'. For particular negative: no explicit quantifier given but uses 'ba'd' with negation.
The text also mentions 'lam al-istighraq' as a quantifier for universal affirmative.
5
Test with examples
Match each example from the text: 'Every human is an animal' (universal affirmative), 'Some animal is black' (particular affirmative), 'No Zanj is white' (universal negative), 'Some human is not black' (particular negative).

Propositions
beginner

Quantified vs Unquantified Propositions
Defining quantified and unquantified propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between quantified (mahsoora) and unquantified (muhmala) propositions in Arabic logical texts and correctly identify the implied quantity of a muhmala proposition.

Core Idea
Knowing whether a proposition is quantified determines its role in building proofs.

In this passage, the author introduces a fundamental distinction: some propositions explicitly state how many individuals of the subject are included (quantified), while others leave that unspecified (unquantified). This distinction is crucial because later logicians treat unquantified propositions as particular, which affects how we interpret and use them in arguments. We will learn to recognize these two types and apply the later consensus rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantity indicator
Look for explicit quantifier words like كل (every), بعض (some), لا شيء (nothing), ليس بعض (not some) in the proposition. If one of these is present, the proposition is quantified (mahsoora).
In Arabic, even the absence of a quantifier is a signal—it means muhmala.
2
Check if quantity is specified
If the proposition includes a word that indicates how many individuals are intended (e.g., كل for all, بعض for some), it is mahsoora. If not, it is muhmala.
3
Apply the later rule for muhmala
Later logicians (متأخرون) treat muhmala as equivalent to a particular proposition. This means that unless context forces a universal reading, assume the judgment applies to only some individuals.
Early logicians debated this; the later consensus simplifies analysis.

المحصورات الأربعة/السور إما أن يكون كمّية الأفراد فيها مبينا، أو لم يكن. فإن بين كمية الأفراد تسمّى القضية محصورة كقولك: "كل إنسان حيوان وبعض الحيوان إنسان". وإن لم يُبين يسمّى القضية مهملة نحو الإنسان في خسر. فصل المحصورات أربع إحداها الموجبة الكلية كقولك: "كل إنسان حيوان". والثانية الموجبة الجزئية نحو: "بعض الحيوان أسود" والثالثة السالبة الكلية نحو: "لا شيء من الزنجي بأبيض. والرابعة: السالبة الجزئية، نحو: "بعض الإنسان ليس بأسود". محصورة إلخ: إنما سمّيت محصورة؛ لحصر أفراد موضوعها ويقال: مسوَّرة أيضا؛ لاشتمالها على السور. مهملة إلخ إنما سمّيت بها ؛ لأن الحكم فيها على الأفراد، وقد أهمل بيان كميتها، وهي تلازم الجزئية عند المتأخرين. وأما مهملة القدماء ففي كونه متلازما للجزئية كلام مشروح في شروح تصديقات "السلّم" وغيرها، فليراجع ثَمّه. المحصورات أربع إلخ لأن الحكم فيها إما بالإيجاب أو بالسلب وعلى التقديرين إما على كل الأفراد أو على بعضها. فإن حكم بالإيجاب على كل الأفراد فموجبة كلية وإن حكم بالإيجاب على بعضها فموجبة جزئية، وإن حكم بالسلب على كلها فسالبة كلية، وإن حكم بالسلب على بعضها فسالبة جزئية. اعلم أن أهم المهمات في هذا الباب تحقيق المحصورات؛ لابتناء معرفة الحجج التي المطلب الأعلى من هذا الفن عليها، ووقوع الخبط العظيم لسبب الغفلة عنها، وإنما وقع البداية بتحقيق الموجبة الكلية لشرفها وتأدية معرفتها إلى إدراك البواقي بالمقايسة.
The four quantified propositions: Either the quantity of individuals in them is made clear or not. If the quantity of individuals is made clear, the proposition is called quantified (mahsoora), as in your saying: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some animal is human'. And if it is not made clear, the proposition is called unquantified (muhmala), like 'Man is in loss'. Section: The quantified propositions are four: first, the universal affirmative, as in your saying: 'Every human is an animal'. Second, the particular affirmative, like 'Some animal is black'. Third, the universal negative, like 'Nothing of the Zanj is white'. Fourth, the particular negative, like 'Some human is not black'. Commentary: 'Mahsoora etc.' It is called mahsoora because the individuals of its subject are restricted, and it is also called musawwara because it includes a quantifier. 'Muhmala etc.' It is called muhmala because the judgment is on individuals, but the statement of its quantity is neglected. And according to the later scholars, it is concomitant with particularity. As for the muhmala of the ancients, there is a detailed discussion in the commentaries on the tasdiqat of al-Sullam and others, so refer there. 'The quantified are four etc.' Because the judgment is either affirmative or negative, and in either case either on all individuals or on some. If the judgment is affirmative on all individuals, it is universal affirmative; if affirmative on some, particular affirmative; if negative on all, universal negative; if negative on some, particular negative. Know that the most important matters in this chapter are the verification of quantified propositions, because knowledge of the proofs, which are the highest aim of this art, depends on them, and great confusion occurs due to heedlessness of them. And they began with verification of the universal affirmative due to its nobility and because its knowledge leads to grasping the rest by analogy.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage, from the Sharh of the matn on propositions, defines two key concepts: quantified (mahsoora) and unquantified (muhmala) propositions, and then enumerates the four standard categorical propositions (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative). The author begins by distinguishing propositions based on whether the quantity (i.e., how many individuals of the subject are included) is specified. If it is specified, the proposition is 'mahsoora'; if not, 'muhmala'. Examples are given for each. Then the four types are listed with their Arabic quantifier words: كل for universal affirmative, some for particular affirmative, لا شيء for universal negative, and ليس بعض for particular negative. The commentary that follows explains the etymology of mahsoora and muhmala, and clarifies that muhmala in later usage is taken to be equivalent to particular, though early scholars debated this. The passage also highlights the importance of understanding quantified propositions for constructing proofs, emphasizing the primacy of the universal affirmative.

The Four Quantified Proposition Types
Listing the four quantified proposition types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four types of quantified propositions, identify each from its Arabic quantifier, and explain why the universal affirmative is the foundation for grasping the others.

Core Idea
All categorical syllogisms rest on the four proposition types: universal/particular, affirmative/negative.

Now that we know what a quantified proposition is, the passage lists exactly four kinds. These are the building blocks of every categorical syllogism in Islamic logic. The author also explains why the universal affirmative is taught first — because knowing one leads to the others by analogy. We will learn to recognize each type and appreciate their central role in proof construction.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two dimensions
Every quantified proposition has two dimensions: quality (affirmative or negative) and quantity (universal or particular). Combine them to get four types.
2
Memorize the Arabic quantifier words
Universal affirmative: كل (kull); particular affirmative: بعض (ba'd); universal negative: لا شيء (la shay'); particular negative: ليس بعض (laysa ba'd). Practice identifying these in sentences.
3
Construct Islamic examples for each type
For each type, produce an example from a real Islamic science. For instance, universal affirmative: 'كل مسكر حرام' (Every intoxicant is forbidden). Particular affirmative: 'بعض الصلوات فرض' (Some prayers are obligatory). Universal negative: 'لا شيء من الشرك بمغفور' (Nothing of shirk is forgiven). Particular negative: 'بعض الناس ليسوا مؤمنين' (Some people are not believers).
Use examples that are doctrinally sound.
4
Appreciate the primacy of universal affirmative
The passage notes that verification of propositions begins with the universal affirmative because of its nobility and because knowing it leads to understanding the rest by analogy. Reflect on why that might be: in syllogisms, universal affirmatives are the most powerful premises.

Quantifiers (Sawr) in Categorical Propositions
Author defines sawr and lists quantifiers with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantifier (sawr) of any categorical proposition and distinguish between the quantifiers for universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, and particular negative, including the nuanced use of 'laysa ba'd'.

Core Idea
The sawr encloses the subject like a city wall, determining universal or particular scope.

In the Islamic logical tradition, every categorical proposition has a quantifier that specifies how many individuals the subject includes. This lesson introduces the term 'sawr', its derivation, and the specific quantifiers used in Arabic and Persian. Understanding the sawr is essential for correctly interpreting and converting propositions in syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Define the sawr
Recognize that sawr is the term that clarifies quantity (universal or particular). Note its derivation from 'sūr' (wall) because it encloses the subject.
2
List the quantifiers
For each of the four categorical propositions (A, I, E, O), identify the Arabic quantifier: 'kull' for A, 'ba'd' for I, 'lā shay'a' for E, and 'laysa ba'd' or 'ba'd laysa' for O.
3
Examine the nuance for O
Understand that 'laysa ba'd' can sometimes imply universal negation when the indefinite (ba'd) falls under negation, whereas 'ba'd laysa' is strictly particular negative.
4
Apply to Persian
Recognize that Persian uses 'har' for universal affirmative, as shown in the example.
5
Test your understanding
Given a proposition, extract its sawr and determine the quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: الذي يُبيّن به كمية الأفراد من الكلية والبعضية يسمّى سُورا، وهو مأخوذ من سور البلد وسور الموجبة الكلية "كل، ولام الاستغراق"، وسور الموجبة الجزئية "بعض، وواحد من الجسم جماد. وسور السالبة الكلية "لا شيء، ولا واحد" نحو: لاشيء من الغراب بأبيض، ولا واحد من النار ببارد. يسمّى سُورا إلخ مأخوذ من "سور البلد" كما أنه يحصر البلد ويحيط به، كذلك اللفظ الدال على كمية الأفراد يحصرها ويحيط بها. اعلم أن في كل لسان سورا يخصها؛ ففي الفارسية لفظ "هر" سور الموجبة الكلية، كقول الشاعر : بيت "هر آن کس که در بند حرص افتاد دهد خر من زندگانی بیاد". ووقوع النكرة تحت النفي نحو: ما من ماء إلا وهو رطب، وسور السالبة الجزئية "ليس بعض" كقولك: "ليس بعض الحيوان بحمار"، و"بعض ليس" كما تقول: بعض الفواكه ليس بحلو .
Section: That which makes clear the quantity of individuals, whether universal or particular, is called a quantifier (sawr), and it is derived from the walls of a city. The quantifier of the universal affirmative is 'kull' and the lām of generality; the quantifier of the particular affirmative is 'ba'd' and 'one of the body inanimate'; the quantifier of the universal negative is 'la shay'a' and 'la wāhid', like 'Nothing of the raven is white' and 'No fire is cold'. Commentary: 'It is called sawr etc.' It is derived from 'the wall of a city' just as it encloses the city and surrounds it, so the word indicating the quantity of individuals encloses and surrounds them. Know that every language has its own quantifiers. In Persian, the word 'har' is the quantifier of the universal affirmative, as the poet said: 'Every person who falls into the trap of greed, give him my life as a remembrance.' And the occurrence of the indefinite in the scope of negation, like 'There is no water except that it is wet.' The quantifier of the particular negative is 'laysa ba'd', as you say 'Not some animal is a donkey', and 'ba'd laysa' as you say 'Some fruits are not sweet.'
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'sawr' (quantifier) as the term that specifies the quantity of individuals in a categorical proposition. It explains that the quantifier is derived from the Arabic word for wall (sūr) because it encloses the subject. The quantifiers for each of the four types are listed: 'kull' for universal affirmative, 'ba'd' for particular affirmative, 'lā shay'a' for universal negative, and 'laysa ba'd' or 'ba'd laysa' for particular negative. The commentary also notes that each language has its own quantifiers, giving the example of Persian where 'har' is used for universal affirmative. Additionally, there is a discussion of the quantifiers for particular negative, distinguishing between 'laysa ba'd' and 'ba'd laysa', explaining that the former can also serve as a universal negative due to the indefinite nature, while the latter is strictly particular negative. The passage also touches on the use of the indefinite in negation to express universal scope.

Quantifiers for Negative Partial Propositions and Language-Specific Quantifiers
Author explains quantifiers for negative partial propositions and gives Persian examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'laysa ba'd' and 'ba'd laysa' and identify Persian quantifiers for the four categorical proposition types.

Core Idea
Not every 'some not' is the same—scope and language matter for correct logical interpretation.

In this passage, the author delves into the subtle quantifiers for negative partial propositions and emphasizes that each language has its own quantifier words. Understanding the difference between 'laysa ba'd' (not some) and 'ba'd laysa' (some not) is crucial for correctly interpreting negative partial statements in Islamic texts. The author also provides Persian equivalents, which is valuable for engaging with Persian logical and theological works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier types for negative partial propositions
Locate 'laysa ba'd' and 'ba'd laysa' in the Arabic passage. Note that 'laysa ba'd' places the negation before 'some', while 'ba'd laysa' places 'some' before the negation.
These are the two quantifiers discussed for negative partial propositions.
2
Distinguish the logical implications
Understand that 'laysa ba'd' can imply universal negation because the indefinite 'some' under negation behaves like an indefinite noun (nakira) in a negative context, which yields generality. In contrast, 'ba'd laysa' does not have this force and may even be used for privative affirmation (affirming non-humanity for some).
This distinction is critical for correct logical analysis.
3
Recognize the phenomenon of indefinite noun under negation
Recall that an indefinite noun under negation (like 'water' in 'no water') signifies universal negation because negating an unspecified individual requires negating all individuals. The same logic applies to 'laysa ba'd'.
This is the basis for the generalization effect.
4
Apply the language-specific quantifier knowledge
Map the Persian quantifiers to the logical types: 'har' for universal affirmative, 'hich' for universal negative, 'barkhi hast' for partial affirmative, and 'barkhi nist' for partial negative.
This helps in reading Persian logical texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوع النكرة تحت النفي نحو: ما من ماء إلا وهو رطب، وسور السالبة الجزئية "ليس بعض" كقولك: "ليس بعض الحيوان بحمار"، و"بعض ليس" كما تقول: بعض الفواكه ليس بحلو. اعلم أن في كل لسان سورا يخصها؛ ففي الفارسية لفظ "هر" سور الموجبة الكلية، كقول الشاعر: بيت " هر آن کس که در بند حرص افتاد دهد خر من زندگانی بیاد". وقوع النكرة إلخ لأن نفي الفرد المبهم لا يكون إلا بانتفاء جميع الأفراد، وهذا من قبيل التعميم بعد التخصيص وسور السالبة: اعلم أن المصنف لم يذكر "ليس كل" من أسوار السالبة الجزئية مع أنه مناسب لأن "ليس كل" يدل بالمطابقة على رفع الإيجاب الكلي، وبالالتزام على السلب الجزئي. و"ليس بعض" و"بعض ليس" بالعكس أي يدلان على السلب الجزئي بالمطابقة، وعلى الإيجاب الكلي بالالتزام فقنع بذكرهما. والفرق بينهما أن "ليس بعض" قد يذكر للسلب الكلي؛ لأن البعض غير معين؛ فأشبه النكرة في سياق النفي، فكما أنها تفيد العموم كذلك ههنا، بخلاف "بعض ليس" فإن البعض ههنا وإن كان أيضا غير معين إلا أنه ليس واقعا في سياق النفي، بل السلب وارد عليه. و"بعض ليس" قد يذكر للإيجاب العدولي كقولك: بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان، تريد إثبات اللا إنسانية لبعض الحيوان بخلاف "ليس بعض"؛ إذ لا يمكن تصور الإيجاب مع تقدم حرف السلب على الموضع. ففي الفارسية لفظ "ہر" سور الموجبة الكلية، وكذلك لفظ "ہم". وللسلب الكلي لفظ "هیچ" و"برخی هست" للإيجاب الجزئي، و"برخی نیست" للسلب الجزئي. كذا في "شرح المطالع".
The occurrence of the indefinite noun under negation, like: 'There is no water except that it is wet.' And the quantifiers of the negative partial are 'not some', as in your saying: 'Not some animal is a donkey', and 'some not', as you say: 'Some fruits are not sweet.' Know that in every language there are quantifiers specific to it; so in Persian the word 'har' is the quantifier of the universal affirmative, as the poet says: 'Har aan kas ke dar band-e hirs oftad, dahad khar man zindagi biyaad.' The occurrence of the indefinite etc. because the negation of an unspecified individual is only by the non-existence of all individuals, and this is of the type of generalization after particularization. And the quantifiers of the negative: know that the author did not mention 'not every' among the quantifiers of the negative partial even though it is appropriate, because 'not every' denotes by conformity the removal of universal affirmation, and by implication the partial negation. And 'not some' and 'some not' are the opposite, i.e., they denote partial negation by conformity, and universal affirmation by implication, so he sufficed with mentioning them. The difference between them is that 'not some' may be used for universal negation because 'some' is unspecified; thus it resembles an indefinite noun in the context of negation, just as it conveys generality, so also here. Unlike 'some not', for here 'some' although also unspecified is not in the context of negation, rather negation is applied to it. And 'some not' may be used for privative affirmation, as in your saying: 'Some animal is not human', intending to affirm non-humanity for some animal, unlike 'not some', for it is impossible to conceive affirmation while the negative particle precedes the subject. So in Persian the word 'har' is the quantifier of universal affirmative, and likewise the word 'ham'. For universal negative the word 'hich', and 'barkhi hast' for partial affirmative, and 'barkhi nist' for partial negative. As mentioned in 'Sharh al-Mutali''.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage explains the quantifiers for negative partial propositions and emphasizes that each language has its own quantifier words. The author begins by clarifying that an indefinite noun under negation (like 'water' in 'no water') signifies universal negation because negation of an unspecified individual requires all individuals to be negated. Then he discusses the two quantifiers for negative partial: 'laysa ba'd' (not some) and 'ba'd laysa' (some not). He notes that the author of the original text omitted 'laysa kull' (not every) from the list, but it is acceptable because it implies partial negation via implication. The difference between 'laysa ba'd' and 'ba'd laysa' is subtle: 'laysa ba'd' can be used for universal negation because the 'some' is indefinite, similar to an indefinite noun under negation, which conveys generality. In contrast, 'ba'd laysa' does not have that force; it can even express privative affirmation, as in 'some animal is not human', affirming the absence of humanity. The passage then gives Persian equivalents: 'har' for universal affirmative, 'hich' for universal negative, 'barkhi hast' for partial affirmative, and 'barkhi nist' for partial negative. This is relevant for students of logic who may encounter Persian texts. Example: In fiqh, the quantifier 'kull' (every) is used in universal affirmative rulings like 'every intoxicant is forbidden'; here, the corresponding quantifiers in Persian are provided.

Why Logicians Use Letters J and B in Universal Affirmatives
Author explains the dual purpose of using variables in universal affirmative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two reasons logicians use abstract variables in categorical propositions and apply them to avoid material-specific illusions.

Core Idea
Variables free logic from the illusion that rules apply only to one example.

In this passage, the author explains a common practice in logic: why use the letters ج (J) and ب (B) instead of real words? The answer reveals a deep insight into how logic achieves universality. By stripping away concrete content, logicians ensure their rules apply everywhere.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two purposes
Locate in the Arabic the two reasons: الإيجاز (brevity) and دفع توهم الانحصار (repelling illusion of restriction). These are the core motivations for using abstract variables.
The text explicitly states 'ومقصودهم من ذلك الإيجاز، ودفع توهم الانحصار'.
2
Contrast with a concrete example
Compare 'كل ج ب' with 'كل إنسان حيوان'. Notice that the abstract form is shorter and does not refer to any specific subject or predicate. This illustrates both brevity and the removal of illusion.
The author says 'كل ج ب أخصر من قولنا كل إنسان حيوان'.
3
Understand the epistemological move
Recognize that using variables signals that the logical form is independent of any specific subject matter. This prevents the mistaken belief that a logical ruling applies only to the example used.
The text warns against 'توهم الانحصار', meaning one might think the ruling is restricted to that material.
4
Note the scholarly context
The author mentions that this method is preferred by the original text's author and contemporary scholars. He also discusses pronunciation details, showing that notation is a deliberate pedagogical choice.
Reference is made to 'شرح السلم السنديلي' for further details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضايا وأقسامها [اب]، فمتى أرادوا التعبير عن الموجبة الكلية يقولون: "كل جب"، ومقصودهم من ذلك الإيجاز، ودفع توهم الانحصار. وهو الحق؛ لأن الاختصار حاصل به وأما التلفظ باسمهما – أعني الجيم والباء – فهو تلفظ باسمين ثلاثيين يشاركهما سائر الأسماء الثلاثية؛ فإنه إذا تلفظ باسمهما يفهم منهما الحرفان المخصوصان، بخلاف ما إذا تُلفّظا بسيطين. هذا هو المرضي عند المصنف بحبه، وعليه قراءة علماء عصرنا والتفصيل بما له وما عليه في "شرح السلم" السنديلي. الإيجاز ودفع إلخ: أما الإيجاز؛ فلأن قولنا: "كل ج ب" أخصر من قولنا: "كل إنسان حيوان" وهو ظاهر، وإما دفع توهم الانحصار؛ لأنهم لو وضعوا للكلية مثلا: قولنا كل إنسان حيوان"، وأجروا عليه الأحكام أمكن أن تلك الأحكام إنما هي في هذه المادة دون الموجبات الكليات الأخر؛ فتصوّروا المفهوم القضية وجردوها عن المواد، وعبّروا عن طرفيها بـ"ج" "وب" تنبيها على أن الأحكام الجارية عليها شاملة لجميع جزئياتها، غير مقصودة على البعض دون البعض.
Propositions and their divisions [ab]: Whenever they want to express the universal affirmative, they say 'Every J B', and their intention in that is brevity and to repel the illusion of restriction. And it is correct because conciseness is achieved by it. As for pronouncing their names—namely the jīm and bā—it is a pronunciation of two triliteral names in which all other triliteral names share; for if one pronounces their names, the two specific letters are understood from them, unlike if they were pronounced as two simple letters. This is the preferred view of the author, and upon it is the reading of the scholars of our time; the detailed account of its pros and cons is in the 'Sharh al-Sullam' al-Sandīlī. [Explanation of] brevity and repelling illusion: As for brevity: because our saying 'Every J B' is more concise than our saying 'Every human is an animal', which is obvious. As for repelling the illusion of restriction: because if they had assigned for the universal, for example, our saying 'Every human is an animal', and applied the rulings to it, it could be thought that those rulings are only in this matter and not in other universal affirmatives; so they conceptualized the meaning of the proposition and stripped it of matters, and expressed its two extremes by 'J' and 'B' as a notification that the rulings applied to them are inclusive of all its particulars, not intended for some to the exclusion of others.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage explains why logicians use abstract letters (ج، ب) instead of concrete terms when formulating propositional forms. The author states that the purpose is twofold: brevity (al-ījāz) and repelling the illusion of restriction (daf' tawahhum al-inḥiṣār). He clarifies that using letters makes the expression shorter, for example 'كل ج ب' is shorter than 'كل إنسان حيوان'. More importantly, it prevents the misconception that the logical judgment applies only to the specific subject matter used in the example. By stripping the proposition of material content, logicians ensure that the ruling is understood to apply to all possible subjects and predicates of that type. The author then discusses the pronunciation of the letters themselves, noting that they are triliteral names (like any other) and that this method is preferred by the author of the matn (al-Sullam) and followed by contemporary scholars. References are given to the supercommentary for further details.

الإيجاز

Division of Categorical Propositions: External, Mental, and Real
Author divides categorical propositions into external, mental, and real based on the domain of the judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between external, mental, and real categorical propositions and correctly classify any given proposition based on the domain of its subject.

Core Idea
The domain of the subject determines the type of categorical proposition.

Categorical propositions can be divided based on how the subject is considered: does it exist in the external world, in the mind, or absolutely? This division is crucial for understanding the scope of judgments in logic and the Islamic sciences. The passage provides clear definitions and examples for each type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the passage and note the three types: external (khārijiyya), mental (dhihniyya), and real (ḥaqīqiyya). Understand the basis for each: existence in the external world, existence in the mind, or absolute reality.
2
Analyze the examples
For each type, the passage gives an example. 'Man is a writer' is external because it assumes men exist externally. 'Man is a universal' is mental because it considers the concept of man as a mental universal. 'Four is even' is real because it holds regardless of whether four exists externally or mentally.
3
Apply the criterion
Given a new proposition, determine the domain of its subject. Ask: Is the subject considered as existing externally, mentally, or absolutely? Classify accordingly.
4
Recognize the real proposition's importance
The real proposition (ḥaqīqiyya) is based on absolute reality (nafs al-amr). It is used in sciences where truth is independent of existence, such as mathematics and necessary truths in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم آخر للحملية موضوع الحملية إن كان موجودا في الخارج، وكان الحكم فيها باعتبار تحقق الموضوع ووجوده في الخارج، كانت القضية خارجية نحو: الإنسان كاتب. وإن كان موجودا في الذهن وكان الحكم باعتبار خصوص وجوده في الذهن كانت ذهنية نحو الإنسان كلي. وإن كان الحكم باعتبار تقرّره في الواقع مع عزل النظر عن خصوصية ظرف الخارج أو الذهن سمّيت القضية حقيقية نحو: الأربعة زوج، والستة ضعف الثلاثة. الحمل بالمواطأة إلخ اعلم أن الأولى والحمل المتعارف من أقسام هذا الحمل وتعريفهما أن الحمل إن عُني به أن الموضوع بعينه المحمول ذاتا ووجودا فيسمى ذلك الحمل الحمل الأولى مثل: الإنسان إنسان. فإن قلت: إن الحمل الأولى لا تغاير فيه بين الموضوع والمحمول، ولا بدّ في الحمل من التغاير كما عرفت في تعريفه؟ قلت: فيه أيضا تغاير؛ فإن الإنسان المتعقل مرة أولى مغاير للإنسان المتعقل مرة أخرى وهذا القدر من التغاير يكفي. وإن اقتصر فيه على مجرد الاتحاد في الوجود لا في الذات فيسمّى الحمل الشائع المتعارف؛ لشيوع استعماله وتعارفه وشهرته كقولنا: الإنسان نوع وهذا القسم من الحمل هو المعتبر في العلوم؛ لكثرة استعماله فيها، وإفادته في الأقيسة للإنتاج، ثم الحمل المتعارف ينقسم بحسب كون المحمول ذاتيا إلى الحمل بالذات كقولنا: الإنسان حيوان، والإنسان ناطق، أو عرضيا إلى الحمل بالعرض كقولنا: الإنسان كاتب، والحيوان ماش، فاحفظه. [Note: The repetition in the OCR may indicate a marginal note; we include only the new division.]
Another division of the categorical proposition: If the subject of the categorical proposition exists in the external world and the judgment is made with consideration of the subject's realization and existence in the external world, then the proposition is external (khārijiyya) like 'Man is a writer'. If it exists in the mind and the judgment is made with consideration of its specific existence in the mind, then it is mental (dhihniyya) like 'Man is a universal'. If the judgment is made with consideration of its determination in reality, disregarding the specific situation of the external or the mind, then the proposition is called real (ḥaqīqiyya) like 'Four is even' and 'Six is double of three'.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage provides another division of the categorical proposition (al-ḥamliyya) based on the domain in which the subject is considered to exist. The author enumerates three types: (1) External proposition (khārijiyya): the subject is taken as existing in the external world, and the judgment is about that external existence. Example: 'Man is a writer' (assuming men exist in the external world). (2) Mental proposition (dhihniyya): the subject is taken as existing in the mind, and the judgment is about its mental existence. Example: 'Man is a universal' (the concept of man, as a mental universal, is the subject). (3) Real proposition (ḥaqīqiyya): the subject is considered absolutely, without regard to whether it exists externally or mentally, and the judgment is about its reality in itself. Example: 'Four is even' (the truth holds whether or not four is actually instantiated externally). This division is important for understanding the scope of judgments in logic and the sciences. The author emphasizes that the real proposition is based on the absolute reality (nafs al-amr) of the subject.

القضية الذهنية
القضية الحقيقية

Types of Predication: Primary vs. Common
Author explains types of haml and their definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between primary predication (al-haml al-awwal) and common predication (al-haml al-muta'arif), and identify essential vs. accidental predication in Islamic logical texts.

Core Idea
Predication is either identity in essence and existence or mere union in existence, with common predication further split into essential and accidental.

When we say 'Man is man,' is that the same kind of predication as 'Man is a writer'? Not at all. This lesson unpacks the two main types of predication used in Islamic sciences, clarifying when a predicate is identical to the subject and when it merely coexists. The author also addresses a subtle objection about difference in identity statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types of haml
Find where the author introduces 'al-haml al-awwal' and 'al-haml al-muta'arif'. Note the defining criteria: identity in essence and existence vs. union in existence only.
2
Analyze the objection about primary predication
The author anticipates a critique: primary predication seems to lack difference (taghayur). How does he resolve it? He says the repeated conceptualization provides a minimal difference.
This shows that even identity statements have a degree of difference in mental consideration.
3
Distinguish essential vs. accidental in common predication
Within common predication, the author divides based on whether the predicate is essential (dhati) or accidental ('aradi). 'Man is animal' is essential; 'Man is writer' is accidental.
Essential predicates are part of the definition; accidental are not.
4
Apply the classification to given examples
Take examples from Islamic sciences (e.g., 'Wine is an intoxicant' – is it essential or accidental? Is it primary or common?). Justify your answer using the criteria from the text.
Practice with examples from Fiqh and Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضايا وأقسامها يقال له: الحمل بالمواطأة نحو عمرو طبيب، وبكر فصيح.
القضية الحملية
فصل تقسيم آخر للحملية موضوع الحملية إن كان موجودا في الخارج، وكان الحكم فيها باعتبار تحقق الموضوع ووجوده في الخارج، كانت القضية خارجية نحو: الإنسان كاتب. وإن كان موجودا في الذهن وكان الحكم باعتبار خصوص وجوده في الذهن كانت ذهنية نحو الإنسان كلي. وإن كان الحكم باعتبار تقرّره في الواقع مع عزل النظر عن خصوصية ظرف الخارج أو الذهن سمّيت القضية حقيقية نحو: الأربعة زوج، والستة ضعف الثلاثة.
الحمل بالمواطأة إلخ اعلم أن الأولى والحمل المتعارف من أقسام هذا الحمل وتعريفهما أن الحمل إن عُني به أن الموضوع بعينه المحمول ذاتا ووجودا فيسمى ذلك الحمل الحمل الأولى مثل: الإنسان إنسان. فإن قلت: إن الحمل الأولى لا تغاير فيه بين الموضوع ،والمحمول، ولا بدّ في الحمل من التغائر كما عرفت في تعريفه؟ قلت: فيه أيضا تغاير؛ فإن الإنسان المتعقل مرة أولى مغائر للإنسان المتعقل مرة أخرى وهذا القدر من التغاير يكفي. وإن اقتصر فيه على مجرد الاتحاد في الوجود لا في الذات فيسمّى الحمل الشائع المتعارف؛ لشيوع استعماله وتعارفه وشهرته كقولنا: الإنسان نوع وهذا القسم من الحمل هو المعتبر في العلوم؛ لكثرة استعماله فيها، وإفادته في الأقيسة للإنتاج، ثم الحمل المتعارف ينقسم بحسب كون المحمول ذاتيا إلى الحمل بالذات كقولنا: الإنسان حيوان، والإنسان ناطق، أو عرضيا إلى الحمل بالعرض كقولنا: الإنسان كاتب، والحيوان ماش، فاحفظه. تقسيم آخر للحملية إلخ : هذا تقسيم للحملية باعتبار المحكي عنه وتفصيله أن القضية الحملية على ثلاثة أقسام: الأول الخارجية، والثاني الذهنية والثالث الحقيقية؛ لأن الحكم في القضية الحملية الموجبة بثبوت المحمول للموضوع، وفي الحملية السالبة بسلب المحمول عن الموضوع. فإن كان الحكم في الموجبة بثبوت المحمول للموضوع بحسب الخارج، وفي السالبة بسلب المحمول عن الموضوع بحسب الخارج فالقضية خارجية، كقولنا: زيد كاتب، وزيد ليس بكاتب. وإن كان الحكم في الموجبة بثبوت المحمول للموضوع بحسب الذهن، وفي السالبة بسلب المحمول عن الموضوع بحسب ظرف الذهن، فالقضية ذهنية. وإن كان الحكم في الموجبة بثبوت المحمول للموضوع بحسب مطلق نفس الأمر وفي السالبة بسلب المحمول عن الموضوع بحسب مطلق نفس الأمر، فالقضية حقيقية كقولنا: الأربعة زوج، والأربعة ليس بفرد خارج إلخ : المراد بالخارج الخارج عن المشاعر: أي القوى الدراكة.
Propositions and their divisions: it is said: predication by correspondence (haml bil-muwata'a) like 'Amr is a physician' and 'Bakr is eloquent'.
The categorical proposition.
Chapter: Another division of the categorical: If the subject of the categorical exists externally, and the judgment is based on the actuality and existence of the subject externally, then the proposition is external (kharijiyya), e.g., 'Man is a writer'. If it exists mentally and the judgment is based particularly on its mental existence, then it is mental (dhihniyya), e.g., 'Man is a species'. If the judgment is based on its establishment in reality, disregarding the particular circumstance of external or mental, then the proposition is called true (haqiqiyya), e.g., 'Four is even' and 'Six is double three'.
Predication by correspondence etc.: Know that the primary (al-ula) and the common predication (al-muta'arif) are divisions of this predication. Their definition: if by predication it is meant that the subject is identical to the predicate in essence and existence, then that predication is called primary predication (al-haml al-awwal), like 'Man is man'. If you say: In primary predication there is no difference (taghayur) between subject and predicate, and difference is necessary in predication as you learned in its definition? I say: In it there is also difference; for 'man' apprehended once is different from 'man' apprehended another time, and this amount of difference suffices. If it is confined to mere union in existence not in essence, then it is called common, customary predication (al-haml al-sha'i' al-muta'arif), due to its widespread use and familiarity and renown, like our saying 'Man is a species'. This type of predication is what is considered in the sciences, due to its frequent use and its utility in producing conclusions in syllogisms. Then common predication is divided according to whether the predicate is essential (dhati) into essential predication (al-haml bi-l-dhat), like 'Man is an animal' and 'Man is rational', or accidental ('aradi) into accidental predication (al-haml bi-l-'arad), like 'Man is a writer' and 'Animal is walking'. So memorize it. Another division of the categorical etc.: This is a division of the categorical with respect to what is reported (al-mahki 'anhu). Its detail: The categorical proposition has three divisions: first external, second mental, third true. Because the judgment in the affirmative categorical is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject, and in the negative categorical is the negation of the predicate from the subject. If the judgment in the affirmative is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject according to external, and in the negative is the negation of the predicate from the subject according to external, then the proposition is external, like 'Zayd is a writer' and 'Zayd is not a writer'. If the judgment in the affirmative is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject according to the mind, and in the negative is the negation of the predicate from the subject according to the circumstance of the mind, then the proposition is mental. If the judgment in the affirmative is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject according to absolute reality (muthlaq nafs al-amr), and in the negative is the negation of the predicate from the subject according to absolute reality, then the proposition is true, like 'Four is even' and 'Four is not odd'. External etc.: By 'external' is meant external to the senses (al-masha'ir), i.e., the perceiving faculties.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory section that first explains the types of predication (haml) and then divides categorical propositions into three types based on the mode of existence of the subject. The author begins by mentioning the division of predication into primary (al-awwal) and common (al-muta'arif). Primary predication involves identity in essence and existence, like 'Man is man'. Though seemingly lacking difference, the author argues that the repeated conceptualization provides a minimal difference. Common predication is mere union in existence, like 'Man is a species', which is widely used in the sciences. Then common predication is subdivided into essential (when the predicate is part of the essence, e.g., 'Man is animal') and accidental (when the predicate is not part of the essence, e.g., 'Man is writer'). After that, the author presents another division of categorical propositions based on the mode of the subject's existence: external (kharijiyya) when judgment is based on external existence, mental (dhihniyya) when based on mental existence, and true (haqiqiyya) when based on absolute reality regardless of external or mental context. Examples are given: 'Zayd is a writer' is external, 'Man is a species' is mental, and 'Four is even' is true. The passage concludes by clarifying that 'external' refers to what is outside the perceiving faculties.

Key Terms
الحمل الأولى
الحمل المتعارف
الحمل بالذات
الحمل بالعرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of common predication (al-haml al-muta'arif)?
A) الإنسان إنسان (Man is man)
B) الإنسان نوع (Man is a species)
C) الإنسان حيوان (Man is an animal)
D) الإنسان كاتب (Man is a writer)

Key Takeaway
Predication is either identical (primary) or existential (common), and common predication splits into essential and accidental, forming the basis of scientific discourse.

Three Types of Categorical Propositions: External, Mental, and True
Author divides categorical propositions into three types based on mode of existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize any categorical proposition as external (kharijiyya), mental (dhihniyya), or true (haqiqiyya) based on the mode of existence of its subject.

Core Idea
A proposition's truth depends on the domain: external existence, mental existence, or absolute reality.

The same statement can be true in one context and false in another. This lesson introduces the critical distinction between propositions that are true in the external world, those true only in the mind, and those true unconditionally. The author provides clear criteria and examples to distinguish them.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis of division
The author divides categorical propositions by the mode of existence of the subject: external, mental, or absolute reality (nafs al-amr). The judgment (affirmation or negation) is made relative to that mode.
2
Identify the external proposition
When the judgment is based on the subject's actual external existence, it is external (kharijiyya). Example from the text: 'Zayd is a writer' (assuming Zayd exists externally and writes).
External propositions are contingent on the actual world.
3
Identify the mental proposition
When the judgment is based on the subject's existence in the mind (as a concept), it is mental (dhihniyya). Example: 'Man is a species' – this is true in the mind because the concept 'man' has the property 'species' mentally.
Mental propositions can be true even if the subject has no external existence.
4
Identify the true proposition (haqiqiyya)
When the judgment is based on absolute reality, disregarding any particular context (external or mental), it is a true proposition. Example: 'Four is even' – this holds regardless of whether four exists externally or in the mind.
True propositions are necessary and context-independent.
5
Apply the classification to new examples
Take a proposition from Islamic theology, e.g., 'God is knowing'. Determine whether it is external (if considering God's actual existence), mental (as a concept), or true (as a necessary attribute). Justify using the criteria.
Practice with examples from Kalam and Fiqh.

القضايا وأقسامها يقال له: الحمل بالمواطأة نحو عمرو طبيب، وبكر فصيح.
القضية الحملية
فصل تقسيم آخر للحملية موضوع الحملية إن كان موجودا في الخارج، وكان الحكم فيها باعتبار تحقق الموضوع ووجوده في الخارج، كانت القضية خارجية نحو: الإنسان كاتب. وإن كان موجودا في الذهن وكان الحكم باعتبار خصوص وجوده في الذهن كانت ذهنية نحو الإنسان كلي. وإن كان الحكم باعتبار تقرّره في الواقع مع عزل النظر عن خصوصية ظرف الخارج أو الذهن سمّيت القضية حقيقية نحو: الأربعة زوج، والستة ضعف الثلاثة.
الحمل بالمواطأة إلخ اعلم أن الأولى والحمل المتعارف من أقسام هذا الحمل وتعريفهما أن الحمل إن عُني به أن الموضوع بعينه المحمول ذاتا ووجودا فيسمى ذلك الحمل الحمل الأولى مثل: الإنسان إنسان. فإن قلت: إن الحمل الأولى لا تغاير فيه بين الموضوع ،والمحمول، ولا بدّ في الحمل من التغائر كما عرفت في تعريفه؟ قلت: فيه أيضا تغاير؛ فإن الإنسان المتعقل مرة أولى مغائر للإنسان المتعقل مرة أخرى وهذا القدر من التغاير يكفي. وإن اقتصر فيه على مجرد الاتحاد في الوجود لا في الذات فيسمّى الحمل الشائع المتعارف؛ لشيوع استعماله وتعارفه وشهرته كقولنا: الإنسان نوع وهذا القسم من الحمل هو المعتبر في العلوم؛ لكثرة استعماله فيها، وإفادته في الأقيسة للإنتاج، ثم الحمل المتعارف ينقسم بحسب كون المحمول ذاتيا إلى الحمل بالذات كقولنا: الإنسان حيوان، والإنسان ناطق، أو عرضيا إلى الحمل بالعرض كقولنا: الإنسان كاتب، والحيوان ماش، فاحفظه. تقسيم آخر للحملية إلخ : هذا تقسيم للحملية باعتبار المحكي عنه وتفصيله أن القضية الحملية على ثلاثة أقسام: الأول الخارجية، والثاني الذهنية والثالث الحقيقية؛ لأن الحكم في القضية الحملية الموجبة بثبوت المحمول للموضوع، وفي الحملية السالبة بسلب المحمول عن الموضوع. فإن كان الحكم في الموجبة بثبوت المحمول للموضوع بحسب الخارج، وفي السالبة بسلب المحمول عن الموضوع بحسب الخارج فالقضية خارجية، كقولنا: زيد كاتب، وزيد ليس بكاتب. وإن كان الحكم في الموجبة بثبوت المحمول للموضوع بحسب الذهن، وفي السالبة بسلب المحمول عن الموضوع بحسب ظرف الذهن، فالقضية ذهنية. وإن كان الحكم في الموجبة بثبوت المحمول للموضوع بحسب مطلق نفس الأمر وفي السالبة بسلب المحمول عن الموضوع بحسب مطلق نفس الأمر، فالقضية حقيقية كقولنا: الأربعة زوج، والأربعة ليس بفرد خارج إلخ : المراد بالخارج الخارج عن المشاعر: أي القوى الدراكة.
Propositions and their divisions: it is said: predication by correspondence (haml bil-muwata'a) like 'Amr is a physician' and 'Bakr is eloquent'.
The categorical proposition.
Chapter: Another division of the categorical: If the subject of the categorical exists externally, and the judgment is based on the actuality and existence of the subject externally, then the proposition is external (kharijiyya), e.g., 'Man is a writer'. If it exists mentally and the judgment is based particularly on its mental existence, then it is mental (dhihniyya), e.g., 'Man is a species'. If the judgment is based on its establishment in reality, disregarding the particular circumstance of external or mental, then the proposition is called true (haqiqiyya), e.g., 'Four is even' and 'Six is double three'.
Predication by correspondence etc.: Know that the primary (al-ula) and the common predication (al-muta'arif) are divisions of this predication. Their definition: if by predication it is meant that the subject is identical to the predicate in essence and existence, then that predication is called primary predication (al-haml al-awwal), like 'Man is man'. If you say: In primary predication there is no difference (taghayur) between subject and predicate, and difference is necessary in predication as you learned in its definition? I say: In it there is also difference; for 'man' apprehended once is different from 'man' apprehended another time, and this amount of difference suffices. If it is confined to mere union in existence not in essence, then it is called common, customary predication (al-haml al-sha'i' al-muta'arif), due to its widespread use and familiarity and renown, like our saying 'Man is a species'. This type of predication is what is considered in the sciences, due to its frequent use and its utility in producing conclusions in syllogisms. Then common predication is divided according to whether the predicate is essential (dhati) into essential predication (al-haml bi-l-dhat), like 'Man is an animal' and 'Man is rational', or accidental ('aradi) into accidental predication (al-haml bi-l-'arad), like 'Man is a writer' and 'Animal is walking'. So memorize it. Another division of the categorical etc.: This is a division of the categorical with respect to what is reported (al-mahki 'anhu). Its detail: The categorical proposition has three divisions: first external, second mental, third true. Because the judgment in the affirmative categorical is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject, and in the negative categorical is the negation of the predicate from the subject. If the judgment in the affirmative is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject according to external, and in the negative is the negation of the predicate from the subject according to external, then the proposition is external, like 'Zayd is a writer' and 'Zayd is not a writer'. If the judgment in the affirmative is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject according to the mind, and in the negative is the negation of the predicate from the subject according to the circumstance of the mind, then the proposition is mental. If the judgment in the affirmative is the affirmation of the predicate to the subject according to absolute reality (muthlaq nafs al-amr), and in the negative is the negation of the predicate from the subject according to absolute reality, then the proposition is true, like 'Four is even' and 'Four is not odd'. External etc.: By 'external' is meant external to the senses (al-masha'ir), i.e., the perceiving faculties.

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory section that first explains the types of predication (haml) and then divides categorical propositions into three types based on the mode of existence of the subject. The author begins by mentioning the division of predication into primary (al-awwal) and common (al-muta'arif). Primary predication involves identity in essence and existence, like 'Man is man'. Though seemingly lacking difference, the author argues that the repeated conceptualization provides a minimal difference. Common predication is mere union in existence, like 'Man is a species', which is widely used in the sciences. Then common predication is subdivided into essential (when the predicate is part of the essence, e.g., 'Man is animal') and accidental (when the predicate is not part of the essence, e.g., 'Man is writer'). After that, the author presents another division of categorical propositions based on the mode of the subject's existence: external (kharijiyya) when judgment is based on external existence, mental (dhihniyya) when based on mental existence, and true (haqiqiyya) when based on absolute reality regardless of external or mental context. Examples are given: 'Zayd is a writer' is external, 'Man is a species' is mental, and 'Four is even' is true. The passage concludes by clarifying that 'external' refers to what is outside the perceiving faculties.

Distinguishing Privative and Non-Privative Propositions
Author defines privative propositions and distinguishes from non-privative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition as privative (ma'dula) or non-privative (ghayr ma'dula), and identify which term contains the negative particle in privative types.

Core Idea
In privative propositions, the negative particle is part of a term, not a negation of the relation.

This lesson introduces the classification of propositions into privative (ma'dula) and non-privative (ghayr ma'dula) based on whether the negative particle is part of the subject, predicate, or both. Understanding this distinction is essential for accurate logical analysis, as it affects how we interpret the terms. The commentary also resolves the common confusion between a simple negative and an affirmative privative by focusing on the position of the copula.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of ma'dula
Read the definition: A privative proposition is one in which the negative particle (like 'la' or 'ghayr') is made part of the subject, predicate, or both.
2
Identify the three subtypes
Note the classification: ma'dulat al-mawdu' (negative in subject), ma'dulat al-mahmul (negative in predicate), and ma'dulat al-tarafayn (both).
The examples given are for affirmative privatives first.
3
Learn the examples for affirmative and negative
Study the examples: affirmative privatives like 'Non-human is a rock' (subject privative) and negative privatives like 'The non-human is not a scholar' (subject privative).
4
Distinguish simple negative from affirmative privative
Use the rule: If the copula (e.g., 'is') follows the negative particle, it is a simple negative; if the negative particle is part of the predicate (i.e., after the copula), it is an affirmative privative. For example, 'Zayd is not a scholar' is simple negative, but 'Zayd is a non-scholar' is affirmative privative.
This is a key distinction from the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: القضية الموجبة وكذا السالبة تنقسمان إلى معدولة وغير معدولة، فالمعدولة: ما يكون فيه حرف السلب جزء من الموضوع، أو من المحمول، أو كليهما، مثال الأول قولنا: "اللاحى جماد" مثال الثاني "زيد" لا "عالم مثال الثالث "اللاحي لا عالم"، هذا في الإيجاب وأما في السلب فمثال الأول "اللاحي ليس بعالم"، ومثال الثاني "العالم" ليس بلاحي، ومثال الثالث "اللاحي ليس بلا جماد". وغير المعدولة بخلافها، ويسمّى غير المعدولة في الموجبة بـ"المحصلة، وفي السالبة" بـ"البسيطة".
عدل عن
فالمعدولة إلخ : اعلم أن حرف السلب موضوع لرفع النسبة الإيجابية، فإذا جعل جزءً من أحد الطرفين أو منهما معناه الأصلي؛ فسمّيت القضية التي جعل حرف السلب جزء منها معدولة تسمية الكل باسم الجزء، فإن جعل جزء من الموضوع فالقضية معدولة الموضوع، وإن جعل جزءً من المحمول فمعدولة المحمول، وإن جعل جزء من الطرفين فمعدولة الطرفين. والأمثلة ظاهرة من المتن.

Chapter: The affirmative proposition and likewise the negative are divided into privative (ma'dula) and non-privative (ghayr ma'dula). The privative is that in which the negative particle is part of the subject, or of the predicate, or of both. Example of the first: our saying 'Non-human is a rock'. Example of the second: 'Zayd is not a scholar' [but this is actually the negative simple? The text says: example of the second: 'Zayd' 'not' 'a scholar' meaning 'Zayd is a non-scholar'? Actually the example given is 'زيد لا عالم' which could be read as 'Zayd is a non-scholar' (affirmative privative). Example of the third: 'The non-human is a non-scholar'. This in affirmative. As for negative, example of the first: 'The non-human is not a scholar'. Example of the second: 'The scholar is not a non-human'. Example of the third: 'The non-human is not a non-rock'. And the non-privative is contrary to it. The non-privative in the affirmative is called 'muhassala' (positive) and in the negative 'basita' (simple).
'Amended from'? 
'Privative etc.': Know that the negative particle is placed to remove the affirmative relation. So if it is made part of one of the two extremes or both with its original meaning, then the proposition of which the negative particle is made a part is called 'ma'dula', naming the whole by the part. If it is made part of the subject, the proposition is ma'dulat al-mawdu'; if made part of the predicate, ma'dulat al-mahmul; if part of both, ma'dulat al-tarafayn. The examples are clear from the text.
'Zayd is not a scholar': Know that confusion may occur in distinguishing between the simple negative (saliba basita) and the affirmative privative in the predicate (mujaba ma'dula al-mahmul), due to the presence of the negative particle in both. They mentioned that the difference between them is: if the copula (al-rabita) follows after the negative word, it is a simple negative; otherwise it is an affirmative privative. Because if the negative word precedes the copula, it necessitates its removal (i.e., negation), and if it follows, it becomes part of the predicate, thus becoming privative. And the non-privative etc.: The affirmative is called 'muhassala' because if the negative particle is not part of its extremes, each of them is existent (wujudi), so it is obtained (hasala). And the negative is called 'basita' (simple) because the simple is that which has no part, and the negative particle, despite being present in it, is not part of its extremes.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the privative (ma'dula) and non-privative (ghayr ma'dula) propositions. The author begins by stating that both affirmative and negative propositions are divided into privative and non-privative. A privative proposition is one in which the negative particle (like 'la' or 'ghayr') is made part of the subject, predicate, or both. Examples are given: 'Non-human is a rock' has the negative particle in the subject; 'Zayd is not a scholar' (interpreted as affirmative privative: 'Zayd is a non-scholar') has it in the predicate; 'The non-human is a non-scholar' has it in both. In negative privative propositions, the negative particle is also part of the extremes, e.g., 'The non-human is not a scholar' (with 'non-human' as subject). The non-privative is the opposite: the negative particle is not part of either extreme; the affirmative is called 'muhassala' (positive) and the negative 'basita' (simple). The commentary then clarifies the distinction between a simple negative (saliba basita) like 'Zayd is not a scholar' (where 'not' negates the relation) and an affirmative privative with predicate modification like 'Zayd is a non-scholar' (where 'non' is part of the predicate). The key is the position of the copula relative to the negative particle: if the negative particle precedes the copula, it is a simple negative; if it follows, it becomes part of the predicate, making it privative. The commentary also explains why the affirmative non-privative is called 'muhassala' (because both terms are positive/existent) and the negative is called 'basita' (because it has no part, i.e., the negative particle is not a part of the terms).

معدولة
محصلة
بسيطة

Key Takeaway
The privative proposition (ma'dula) integrates the negative particle into the term itself, fundamentally altering the subject or predicate, while the non-privative leaves the terms unmodified.

Understanding Modal Propositions: The Three Modalities
Author introduces modal propositions and explains the three inherent qualities of the propositional relation: necessity, impossibility, and possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the modality (jiha) of a proposition as necessary, impossible, or possible based on the relation between subject and predicate.

Core Idea
Every proposition has an implicit modality: necessity, impossibility, or possibility.

In classical Islamic logic, every categorical proposition carries a modal quality (jiha) that describes how the subject relates to the predicate. This lesson introduces the three fundamental modalities — wajib (necessary), mumtani‘ (impossible), and mumkin (possible) — and explains why every proposition must be qualified by one of them. Understanding modality is essential for correctly interpreting propositions in legal, theological, and philosophical discourse. The passage is cut off but clearly establishes the foundation of modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between categorical and modal propositions
A categorical proposition simply asserts a relation (e.g., 'Every human is mortal'). A modal proposition (mujawwaha) explicitly includes the mode of that relation. Notice that the passage starts by saying 'the modality may be mentioned' – it is an addition to the basic proposition.
2
Grasp the three modalities in reality (nafs al-amr)
The author states that every relation in reality is either necessary in existence (wajib), necessary in non-existence (mumtani‘), or neither necessary (mumkin). These are the three exclusive and exhaustive possibilities.
3
Connect modality to the threefold existence
The relation exists in reality, mind, and utterance. The modality is first inherent in reality, then represented in the mind, and finally expressed in language. This mirrors how things themselves have three levels of existence.
4
Identify the modality of a simple proposition
Take a proposition like 'Allah is eternal.' In reality, the relation is necessary (eternity is essential). Contrast with 'The world is eternal' – in reality, it is impossible (the world is created). This exercise trains you to think about the underlying modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: وقد يذكر الجهة في القضية، فيسمى موجّهة ورباعية أيضا.
عدل عن
فالمعدولة إلخ: اعلم أولا أن كل نسبة بين الموضوع والمحمول لا يخلو في نفس الأمر: إما أن تكون ضرورية التحقق، فهي واجبة أي وجودها ضروري وواجب أو ضرورية العدم، فهي ممتنعة أي وجودها ممتنع وعدمها ضرروري. أو لم تكن ضرورية التحقق واللا تحقق فهي ممكنة أي وجودها وعدمها غير ضروري؛ فكل نسبة لا يخلو في نفس الأمر عن تلك الكيفيات الثلاث وثانيا أن للنسبة ثلاثة وجود ١- وجود في نفس الأمر. - ووجود عند العقل. - ووجود في اللفظ. كالأشياء التي لها وجود في نفس الأمر، ووجود عند العقل، ووجود في اللفظ؛ فالنسبة متى كانت ثابتة في نفس الأمر لا بد أن تكون متكيفة بكيفية من الكيفيات الثلاث ثم إذا حصلت عند العقل اعتبر لها كيفية هي إما -
Chapter: And the modality (jiha) may be mentioned in the proposition, so it is called 'mujawwaha' (modal) and also 'ruba'iyya' (quadripartite).
'Amended from'?
'Privative etc.': Know first that every relation between subject and predicate cannot be devoid in reality of: either being necessary in actualization, so it is obligatory (wajiba), i.e., its existence is necessary and obligatory; or necessary in non-existence, so it is impossible (mumtani'a), i.e., its existence is impossible and its non-existence is necessary; or being neither necessary in actualization nor non-actualization, so it is possible (mumkina), i.e., its existence and non-existence are not necessary. So every relation cannot be devoid in reality of these three qualities. And second, that the relation has three existences: 1) existence in reality, 2) existence in the mind, and 3) existence in utterance. Like things that have existence in reality, existence in the mind, and existence in utterance. So the relation when it is established in reality, it must be qualified by one of the three qualities; then when it occurs in the mind, a quality is considered for it that is either -
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage introduces modal propositions (mujawwaha), which include a modality (jiha) indicating the mode of the relation between subject and predicate. The author explains that in reality (nafs al-amr), every relation is necessarily one of three types: necessary existence (wajib), necessary non-existence (mumtani'), or non-necessary (mumkin). These are the three fundamental qualities. Then the author adds that the relation itself has three levels of existence: in reality, in the mind, and in utterance. This mirrors the threefold existence of objects. When the relation is established in reality, it must take one of the three modalities; when it is mentally apprehended, a corresponding quality is considered for it. The text is cut off but likely continues to discuss how modality is expressed in propositions.

Key Terms
الجهة (al-jiha): The modality of a proposition, i.e., the quality of necessity, impossibility, or possibility that qualifies the relation between subject and predicate. [Note: No significant shift; stable across logical works.]
موجهة (mujawwaha): A modal proposition, one that includes a modality (jiha). [Note: No significant shift.]

Identifying Modified and Unmodified Propositions
Author defines modified and unmodified propositions with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any proposition as modified (mu'addala) or unmodified (ghayr al-mu'addala) and identify the type of modification (subject, predicate, or both).

Core Idea
The negation particle can be part of the term itself, creating a modified proposition with embedded negation.

In Islamic logic, propositions are not simply affirmative or negative; they can be modified by embedding the negation particle into the subject or predicate. This lesson teaches you to recognize these modifications using examples from the text, a crucial skill for precise logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between affirmative and negative forms
Note that modification applies in both affirmative and negative propositions. The passage gives examples for each.
2
Locate the negation particle
Identify whether the negation particle (e.g., 'la', 'laysa') is part of a term or serves as the copula. In modified propositions, it is part of a term.
3
Identify the locus of modification
Determine whether the negation is in the subject, predicate, or both. The passage labels these as subject-modified, predicate-modified, or both-modified.
4
Name the proposition type
Classify as modified (mu'addala) or unmodified (ghayr al-mu'addala). For unmodified affirmatives, use 'muhassala'; for unmodified negatives, use 'basita'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Propositions and their divisions. Section: The affirmative proposition and likewise the negative are divided into modified (mu'addala) and unmodified (ghayr al-mu'addala). The modified is that in which the particle of negation is part of the subject, or of the predicate, or both. Example of the first: 'The non-living is inanimate'; example of the second: 'Zayd is not knowledgeable'; example of the third: 'The non-living is not knowledgeable'. This in affirmation. As for negation, example of the first: 'The non-living is not knowledgeable'; example of the second: 'The knowledgeable is not non-living'; example of the third: 'The non-living is not non-inanimate'. And the unmodified is the opposite of that. The unmodified in affirmative is called 'muhassala', and in negative 'basita'. Turn to: 'falmudda'ila etc.': Know that the particle of negation is posited to remove the affirmative relation. So when it is made part of one of the two extremes or both, that is its original meaning; thus the proposition in which the particle of negation is made a part is called modified, naming the whole after the part. If it is made part of the subject, it is a proposition modified in subject; if part of the predicate, modified in predicate; if part of both, modified in both. The examples are evident from the text.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage defines two classes of propositions: modified (mu'addala) and unmodified (ghayr al-mu'addala). It begins by stating that both affirmative and negative propositions can be divided into these two types. A modified proposition is one where the negation particle (e.g., 'la' or 'laysa') forms an integral part of either the subject, the predicate, or both. For each case, the author provides examples in both affirmative and negative forms. In the affirmative, the examples illustrate a subject like 'al-lahi' (non-living) combined with the negation particle either in the subject, predicate, or both. In the negative, similar examples are given using 'laysa'. The unmodified proposition is simply the opposite. The author then notes that unmodified propositions have specific names: 'muhassala' for affirmative and 'basita' for negative. The commentary expands the definition: the negation particle's original function is to negate the affirmative relation; when it becomes part of a term, it retains its meaning but now modifies the term itself. Consequently, the proposition is named after this modification. The commentary clarifies the three subtypes: modified subject, modified predicate, and modified both, noting that examples are self-evident. This careful classification is foundational for understanding logical operations involving negation.
