Distinguishing Simple Negative from Modified Affirmative
Commentator explains the rule to differentiate negative simple from positive modified propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a simple negative proposition from a positive modified proposition by applying the copula-position rule.

Core Idea
The copula's position relative to negation determines whether a proposition is negative or affirmative.

In Arabic logic, both simple negative (saliba basita) and positive modified (mujiba mu'addala) contain a negation particle, causing potential confusion. The commentator provides a precise rule based on the position of the copula (al-rabith) relative to the negation. By mastering this rule, you will never misclassify these propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negation particle and the copula
Find the particle of negation (e.g., 'laysa', 'ma', 'la') and the copula (explicit or implied, such as 'huwa' or 'kana') in the proposition. Note their order.
The copula is often omitted in Arabic but can be inferred from the sentence structure.
2
Check the order between negation and copula
If the negation appears before the copula, the proposition is a simple negative: the negation negates the copula (the relation). If the copula comes before the negation, the negation becomes part of the predicate, making it a positive modified.
The commentator states: 'if the copula comes after the negation, it is a simple negative; otherwise it is a positive modified.'
3
Apply the rule to examples
For 'Zayd is not knowledgeable' (Zayd laysa 'aliman), 'laysa' includes both negation and copula; the copula is part of the negation, so it is a simple negative. For 'Zayd is non-knowledgeable' (Zayd la 'alim), the implied copula 'huwa' would precede 'la', making it positive modified.
Both contain 'la', but the structure differs.
4
Test with explicit copula constructions
Use explicit copulas like 'kana' to see the order clearly. Example: 'Kana Zayd laysa 'aliman' (Zayd was not knowledgeable) vs. 'Kana Zayd la 'aliman' (Zayd was non-knowledgeable). Analyze the position relative to the main copula.
The rule applies to the copula that directly connects subject and predicate, not auxiliary verbs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنه قد يشتبه الأمر في الامتياز بين السالبة البسيطة والموجبة المعدولة المحمول؛ لوجود حرف السلب فيهما، فذكروا أن الفرق بينهما أن الرابط إن تأخر عن لفظ السلب فسالبة بسيطة، وإلا فموجبة معدولة؛ لأن لفظ السلب إذا تقدم على الرابط يقتضي رفعه، وإذا تأخر يصير جزء من المحمول، فتصير معدولة.
Know that confusion may occur in distinguishing between the simple negative (al-salibat al-basita) and the positive modified in predicate (al-mujibat al-mu'addala al-mahmul) because the particle of negation exists in both. They mentioned that the difference between them is that if the copula (al-rabith) comes after the particle of negation, it is a simple negative; otherwise it is a positive modified. Because if the particle of negation precedes the copula, it requires its (the copula's) removal; and if it comes after, it becomes part of the predicate, thus making it modified.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage addresses a common point of confusion: how to tell apart a simple negative proposition (e.g., 'Zayd is not knowledgeable') from a positive modified proposition where the predicate includes negation (e.g., 'Zayd is non-knowledgeable'). Both contain a negation particle, yet one is negative and the other affirmative. The commentator provides a clear rule based on the position of the copula (al-rabith) relative to the negation particle. In Arabic, the copula is often implied or explicit (like 'huwa' or 'kana'). If the copula comes after the negation, the proposition is a simple negative: the negation operates on the whole relation. If the copula comes before the negation, then the negation becomes part of the predicate, making the proposition affirmative but modified. The reasoning is that when negation precedes the copula, it negates the copula (i.e., the relation), whereas when it follows, it only modifies the predicate term. This precision prevents misclassification in logical analysis.

The Three Modal Qualities in Reality
Author explains the three modal qualities and their three levels of existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three modal qualities (wajiba, mumtani'a, mumkina) and determine which applies to a given proposition based on its relation in reality.

Core Idea
Every relation is necessarily either necessary, impossible, or possible.

Before diving into modal propositions, we must understand the raw materials: the three modal qualities that exist in reality itself. These are not just linguistic expressions but real features of the world. The author sets them out clearly: necessarily realized, necessarily unrealized, or neither. This foundation governs everything that follows about modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the trichotomy
The author states that every relation (nisba) between subject and predicate in reality (fi nafs al-amr) must be one of three: wajiba (necessarily realized), mumtani'a (necessarily unrealized), or mumkina (neither necessary nor impossible). Understand that these are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
Wajiba implies existence is necessary; mumtani'a implies nonexistence is necessary; mumkina implies contingency.
2
Distinguish the three qualities
For each quality, note the key Arabic terms and their meanings: ضَرُورِيَّة التَّحَقُّق (necessarily realized) = واجبة; ضَرُورِيَّة العَدَم (necessarily nonexistent) = ممتنعة; غير ضرورية التحقق واللا تحقق (neither necessary) = ممكنة. Memorize these as the three fundamental modal flavors.
The author emphasizes that these are the only possibilities; there is no fourth option in reality.
3
Apply to an example
Consider the proposition 'The world is temporally originated.' In reality, if the world is indeed temporally originated, this relation could be necessary (if it must be so), impossible (if it cannot be so), or possible (if it may or may not be). Decide which modal quality fits based on your theological or philosophical stance—but for now, recognize that in reality it is one of the three.
This is about the actual state of affairs, not about our knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أولا أن كل نسبة بين الموضوع والمحمول لا يخلو في نفس الأمر: إما أن تكون ضرورية التحقق، فهي واجبة أي وجودها ضروري وواجب أو ضرورية العدم، فهي ممتنعة أي وجودها ممتنع وعدمها ضروري. أو لم تكن ضرورية التحقق واللا تحقق فهي ممكنة أي وجودها وعدمها غير ضروري؛ فكل نسبة لا يخلو في نفس الأمر عن تلك الكيفيات الثلاث وثانيا أن للنسبة ثلاثة وجود ١- وجود في نفس الأمر. - وجود عند العقل. - وجود في اللفظ. كالأشياء التي لها وجود في نفس الأمر، ووجود عند العقل، ووجود في اللفظ؛ فالنسبة متى كانت ثابتة في نفس الأمر لا بد أن تكون متكيفة بكيفية من الكيفيات الثلاث ثم إذا حصلت عند العقل اعتبر لها كيفية هي إما عين تلك الكيفية الثابتة في نفس الأمر، أو غيرها. ثم إذا وجدت في اللفظ أوردت عبارة تدل على تلك الكيفية المعتبرة عند العقل؛ إذ الألفاظ إنما هي بإزاء الصور العقلية؛ فالكيفية الثابتة في نفس الأمر هي القضية، والثابتة لها في العقل هي جهة القضية المعقولة، والعبارة الدالة عليها هي جهة القضية الملفوظة، والقضية التي ذكرت فيه الجهة تسمّى موجّهة لاشتمالها على الجهة ورباعية أيضا؛ لاشتمالها على أربعة أجزاء، رابعها الجهة، والعبارة الدالة مطابقة للمادة كانت القضية صادقة وإلا كاذبة ثم لا يخفى عليك أن القدماء ذهبوا إلى أن المادة ليست كيفية كل نسبة، بل كيفية النسبة الإيجابية فقط. والبسط في مبسوطات الفن.
Know first that every relation (nisba) between subject and predicate in reality (fi nafs al-amr) is inevitably either: necessarily realized - so it is wajiba, i.e., its existence is necessary and obligatory; or necessarily non-existent - so it is mumtani'a, i.e., its existence is impossible and its nonexistence necessary; or it is neither necessarily realized nor necessarily unrealized - so it is mumkina, i.e., its existence and nonexistence are not necessary. Thus every relation in reality is inevitably characterized by these three qualities. Secondly, the relation has three existences: 1. existence in reality, 2. existence in the mind, 3. existence in utterance. Just as things have existence in reality, in the mind, and in utterance. So whenever a relation is established in reality, it must be qualified by one of the three qualities. Then when it occurs in the mind, a quality is considered for it which is either exactly that quality established in reality, or other than it. Then when it occurs in utterance, a phrase is brought that indicates that quality considered in the mind; for utterances correspond to mental forms. So the quality established in reality is the proposition (qadiyya), the quality established for it in the mind is the modality (jiha) of the conceived proposition, and the phrase indicating it is the modality of the uttered proposition. The proposition in which the modality is mentioned is called muwajjaha (modal) because it includes the modality, and also ruba'iyya (quadripartite) because it has four parts, the fourth being the modality. The phrase indicating it - if it matches the matter (madda), the proposition is true; otherwise false. Then it is not hidden from you that the ancients held that matter is not a quality of every relation, but only the quality of the affirmative relation. And the elaboration is in the detailed works of the art.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage provides the epistemological foundation for modal propositions. It first establishes that every relation between subject and predicate in external reality (fi nafs al-amr) necessarily has one of three modal qualities: wajiba (necessary), mumtani'a (impossible), or mumkina (possible). These correspond to the three logical modalities. The commentator then introduces a crucial tripartite analysis: the relation has existence in reality, in the mind, and in utterance. This mirrors the ontological levels of things generally. When a relation actually holds in reality, it is qualified by one of the three qualities. When the mind apprehends it, it may attribute the same quality or a different one (depending on knowledge). When expressed in language, the speaker uses a phrase (like 'necessarily' or 'possibly') to indicate the mental quality. Thus the 'matter' (madda) of a proposition is the reality-based quality; the 'modality' (jiha) is the mental or uttered expression. A proposition that explicitly includes this modal expression is called muwajjaha (modal) or ruba'iyya (four-part: subject, predicate, copula, modality). Truthfulness depends on whether the expressed modality matches the actual matter. The passage ends with a note that some earlier scholars restricted matter to affirmative relations only. This sets the stage for detailed classifications of modal propositions.

The Three Existences of the Proposition: Reality, Mind, Utterance
Author explains the three modal qualities and their three levels of existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace a proposition's modal quality through its three levels of existence (reality, mind, utterance) and identify when a modal proposition is true or false based on correspondence.

Core Idea
Modality exists in three realms: reality (madda), mind (jiha conceived), and utterance (jiha expressed); truth depends on alignment.

The author now expands our view: the same relation that has a modal quality in reality also exists in the mind and in language. This tripartite scheme is crucial for understanding how modality functions in logic. The real quality is called 'madda'; the mental quality is 'jiha' (modality); and the uttered expression is also 'jiha' but in speech. A proposition that explicitly includes this modality is called muwajjaha (modal) or ruba'iyya (four-part). Truth is defined as correspondence between the uttered modality and the real madda.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three existences
The author explains that every relation has three levels of existence: in reality (fi nafs al-amr), in the mind (inda al-'aql), and in utterance (fi al-lafẓ). Just like physical objects, propositions exist in these three realms. The real existence of the relation is the actual state of affairs; the mental existence is the concept in the intellect; the uttered existence is the spoken or written statement.
This mirrors the standard ontological framework of Islamic philosophy: things have external, mental, and verbal existence.
2
Distinguish madda from jiha
The quality in reality is called madda (matter); it is the actual modal property (e.g., actual necessity). The quality in the mind is called jiha (modality); it is the judgment or attribution of a modal quality to the relation. When this mental modality is expressed in language, it becomes the uttered jiha. Crucially, the mental jiha may or may not match the real madda—if they match, the proposition is true; otherwise false.
Modality in the mind/utterance is not automatically the same as the real modality; truth depends on correspondence.
3
Identify the four parts of a modal proposition
A modal proposition (muwajjaha) contains: subject, predicate, copula (often implicit), and the modality term (e.g., 'necessarily', 'possibly'). This is why it is called ruba'iyya (four-part). For example: 'The world is necessarily temporally originated.' Here 'necessarily' is the jiha.
The modality term is the fourth part; without it, the proposition is non-modal.
4
Apply the correspondence condition
Take a proposition like 'The world is possibly eternal.' Determine its madda (real modality) and its uttered jiha. If in reality the world is not eternal (i.e., it is necessarily temporally originated), then the madda is wajiba (temporal origination is necessary), but the uttered jiha is mumkina (possible). Since they differ, the proposition is false. This shows how truth hinges on alignment.
The author explicitly says: 'the phrase indicating it - if it matches the matter, the proposition is true; otherwise false.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أولا أن كل نسبة بين الموضوع والمحمول لا يخلو في نفس الأمر: إما أن تكون ضرورية التحقق، فهي واجبة أي وجودها ضروري وواجب أو ضرورية العدم، فهي ممتنعة أي وجودها ممتنع وعدمها ضروري. أو لم تكن ضرورية التحقق واللا تحقق فهي ممكنة أي وجودها وعدمها غير ضروري؛ فكل نسبة لا يخلو في نفس الأمر عن تلك الكيفيات الثلاث وثانيا أن للنسبة ثلاثة وجود ١- وجود في نفس الأمر. - وجود عند العقل. - وجود في اللفظ. كالأشياء التي لها وجود في نفس الأمر، ووجود عند العقل، ووجود في اللفظ؛ فالنسبة متى كانت ثابتة في نفس الأمر لا بد أن تكون متكيفة بكيفية من الكيفيات الثلاث ثم إذا حصلت عند العقل اعتبر لها كيفية هي إما عين تلك الكيفية الثابتة في نفس الأمر، أو غيرها. ثم إذا وجدت في اللفظ أوردت عبارة تدل على تلك الكيفية المعتبرة عند العقل؛ إذ الألفاظ إنما هي بإزاء الصور العقلية؛ فالكيفية الثابتة في نفس الأمر هي القضية، والثابتة لها في العقل هي جهة القضية المعقولة، والعبارة الدالة عليها هي جهة القضية الملفوظة، والقضية التي ذكرت فيه الجهة تسمّى موجّهة لاشتمالها على الجهة ورباعية أيضا؛ لاشتمالها على أربعة أجزاء، رابعها الجهة، والعبارة الدالة مطابقة للمادة كانت القضية صادقة وإلا كاذبة ثم لا يخفى عليك أن القدماء ذهبوا إلى أن المادة ليست كيفية كل نسبة، بل كيفية النسبة الإيجابية فقط. والبسط في مبسوطات الفن.
Know first that every relation (nisba) between subject and predicate in reality (fi nafs al-amr) is inevitably either: necessarily realized - so it is wajiba, i.e., its existence is necessary and obligatory; or necessarily non-existent - so it is mumtani'a, i.e., its existence is impossible and its nonexistence necessary; or it is neither necessarily realized nor necessarily unrealized - so it is mumkina, i.e., its existence and nonexistence are not necessary. Thus every relation in reality is inevitably characterized by these three qualities. Secondly, the relation has three existences: 1. existence in reality, 2. existence in the mind, 3. existence in utterance. Just as things have existence in reality, in the mind, and in utterance. So whenever a relation is established in reality, it must be qualified by one of the three qualities. Then when it occurs in the mind, a quality is considered for it which is either exactly that quality established in reality, or other than it. Then when it occurs in utterance, a phrase is brought that indicates that quality considered in the mind; for utterances correspond to mental forms. So the quality established in reality is the proposition (qadiyya), the quality established for it in the mind is the modality (jiha) of the conceived proposition, and the phrase indicating it is the modality of the uttered proposition. The proposition in which the modality is mentioned is called muwajjaha (modal) because it includes the modality, and also ruba'iyya (quadripartite) because it has four parts, the fourth being the modality. The phrase indicating it - if it matches the matter (madda), the proposition is true; otherwise false. Then it is not hidden from you that the ancients held that matter is not a quality of every relation, but only the quality of the affirmative relation. And the elaboration is in the detailed works of the art.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage provides the epistemological foundation for modal propositions. It first establishes that every relation between subject and predicate in external reality (fi nafs al-amr) necessarily has one of three modal qualities: wajiba (necessary), mumtani'a (impossible), or mumkina (possible). These correspond to the three logical modalities. The commentator then introduces a crucial tripartite analysis: the relation has existence in reality, in the mind, and in utterance. This mirrors the ontological levels of things generally. When a relation actually holds in reality, it is qualified by one of the three qualities. When the mind apprehends it, it may attribute the same quality or a different one (depending on knowledge). When expressed in language, the speaker uses a phrase (like 'necessarily' or 'possibly') to indicate the mental quality. Thus the 'matter' (madda) of a proposition is the reality-based quality; the 'modality' (jiha) is the mental or uttered expression. A proposition that explicitly includes this modal expression is called muwajjaha (modal) or ruba'iyya (four-part: subject, predicate, copula, modality). Truthfulness depends on whether the expressed modality matches the actual matter. The passage ends with a note that some earlier scholars restricted matter to affirmative relations only. This sets the stage for detailed classifications of modal propositions.

Key Terms

الجهة
موجّهة

Key Takeaway
The truth of a modal proposition depends on the correspondence between the uttered modality (jiha) and the real modality (madda), and the proposition has three levels of existence: real, mental, and uttered.

The Necessary Absolute Modal Proposition
Definition of the necessary absolute proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a necessary absolute proposition and distinguish it from other modal propositions.

Core Idea
The necessary absolute binds the predicate to the subject's essence as long as it exists.

Among the fifteen modal propositions in classical logic, the necessary absolute is the simplest yet most fundamental. It expresses a judgment that holds with necessity for the entire duration of the subject's existence. This lesson unpacks its definition and examples.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence defining الضرورية المطلقة in the Arabic: 'وهي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودة'.
2
Extract key conditions
It requires (a) judgment with necessity, (b) affirmation or negation of the predicate, (c) as long as the subject's essence exists.
3
Apply the definition to examples
Check the examples: 'الإنسان حيوان بالضرورة' – man is necessarily an animal; 'الإنسان ليس بحجر بالضرورة' – man is not a stone necessarily. They meet the conditions: necessity holds as long as man exists.
4
Contrast with 'absolute'
Note that it is called 'absolute' because there is no restriction by time or attribute; it is purely essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموجّهات خمسة عشر : ثمانية منها بسيطة، وسبعة منها مركبة. أما البسائط فأحدها : الضرورية المطلقة : وهي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودة، كقولك: "الإنسان حيوان بالضرورة، والإنسان ليس بحجر بالضرورة" والثانية الدائمة المطلقة وهي التي حكم فيها بدوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، أو سلبه عنه، كقولك: "كل فلك متحرك بالدوام، ولا شيء من الفلك بساكن بالدوام". الضرورية المطلقة إلخ إنما سمّيت ضرورية لاشتمالها على "الضرورة، وإنما سمّيت "مطلقة"؛ لأن الحكم فيها غير مقيد بوصف أو وقت الدائمة المطلقة إلخ وجه التسمية على قياس ما مرّ في الضرورية المطلقة. واعلم أن مفهوم الضرورة امتناع انفكاك النسبة عن الموضوع ومفهوم الدوام شمول النسبة جميع الأزمنة والأوقات؛ فالنسبة بين الضرورية والدائمة عموم وخصوص مطلقا، والضرورية أخص.
The modal propositions are fifteen: eight of them are simple (basita) and seven composite (murakkaba). As for the simple ones: the first is the necessary absolute (al-daruriyyat al-mutlaqa): it is that in which it is judged with necessity of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject or its negation as long as the essence of the subject exists, like your saying: 'Man is an animal necessarily, and man is not a stone necessarily.' The second is the perpetual absolute (al-da'imat al-mutlaqa): it is that in which it is judged with perpetuity of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject or its negation, like your saying: 'Every sphere is moving perpetually, and nothing of the sphere is stationary perpetually.' The necessary absolute etc. It is called necessary because it includes 'necessity', and called 'absolute' because the judgment in it is not restricted by a description or a time. The perpetual absolute etc. The reason for the name is analogous to what passed in the necessary absolute. And know that the concept of necessity is the impossibility of the relation being separated from the subject, and the concept of perpetuity is the relation encompassing all times and moments. So the relation between the necessary and the perpetual is absolute generality and particularity, and the necessary is more specific.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage classifies modal propositions into fifteen: eight simple and seven composite. It then defines the first two simple ones. The necessary absolute (al-daruriyyat al-mutlaqa) is a judgment of necessity that holds as long as the subject exists. The example 'Man is an animal necessarily' means it is impossible for a human not to be an animal while he exists. Similarly, 'Man is not a stone necessarily' means it is impossible for a human to be a stone. The perpetual absolute (al-da'imat al-mutlaqa) judges that the relation holds perpetually, i.e., at all times. The example 'Every sphere is moving perpetually' implies that at every moment a sphere exists, it is moving. The commentary explains the naming: 'necessary' due to the modal of necessity, 'absolute' because there is no restriction by time or attribute. For the perpetual, it is analogous. A key distinction is then drawn: necessity (darura) means the relation cannot be separated from the subject; perpetuity (dawam) means the relation holds for all times. Therefore, necessity implies perpetuity but not vice versa, making the necessary a subtype of the perpetual in terms of logical extension. This is a crucial distinction for advanced modal logic.

Key Terms
الضرورية المطلقة
الضرورة
الموجّهات

D) 'Zayd might be standing'

Key Takeaway
A necessary absolute proposition asserts a necessary relationship between subject and predicate for the entire duration of the subject's existence.

The Perpetual Absolute and Its Relation to Necessity
Definition of perpetual absolute and distinction from necessary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between necessity (darura) and perpetuity (dawam) and apply the correct term to Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Perpetuity is always necessary, but necessity is not always perpetual.

The perpetual absolute proposition claims that a predicate holds at every moment of the subject's existence. While similar to the necessary, the passage teaches that necessity is stronger: it implies impossibility of separation, while perpetuity merely covers all times. This lesson explores the difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of الدائمة المطلقة
Find the definition: 'وهي التي حكم فيها بدوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، أو سلبه عنه' – it judges with perpetuity of affirmation or negation.
2
Study the example
The example: 'كل فلك متحرك بالدوام' – every sphere is moving perpetually. Understand that it holds at all times but does not assert essential necessity (since celestial motion could be constant by divine command, not essential).
3
Compare with the necessary
The passage explains that necessity (الضرورة) means the relation cannot be separated from the subject, while perpetuity (الدوام) means it encompasses all times. This is a crucial distinction.
4
Apply the logical relation
The necessary implies perpetual, but not vice versa. So the necessary is a subtype (أخص) of the perpetual, as stated: 'فالنسبة بين الضرورية والدائمة عموم وخصوص مطلقا، والضرورية أخص'.

Understanding Matter and Modality in Modal Propositions
Defining matter and modality in modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between matter (māddah) and modality (jihah) in a modal proposition, and identify the four parts of a muwajjahah.

Core Idea
Matter is reality; modality is the intellect's filter.

To understand modal logic in the Islamic tradition, we must grasp two key components: the matter (māddah) and the modality (jihah). The matter is the actual quality of the relation (affirmation or negation) as it exists in reality, while the modality is how the intellect conceives that quality, expressed by phrases like 'necessarily' or 'always'. A proposition that includes a modality is called 'modal' (muwajjahah) and has four parts: subject, predicate, copula, and modality. Whether the proposition is true or false depends on whether the modality phrase matches the actual matter.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of matter
Find in the Arabic passage the phrase 'مادة عين تلك الكيفية الثابتة في نفس الأمر' and its translation. Matter is the quality (affirmation/negation) fixed in reality itself.
Contrast with later uses that refer to content of premises.
2
Identify the definition of modality
Find 'الكيفية الثابتة لها في العقل هي جهة القضية المعقولة'. Modality is the quality as conceived by the intellect.
3
Notice the four parts of a modal proposition
The passage says the modal proposition is 'رباعية' (quadripartite): subject, predicate, copula, and modality.
These are the four components that make it 'modal'.
4
Understand the truth condition
The phrase must correspond (مطابقة) to the matter. If the modality matches the real quality, the proposition is true; otherwise false.
This grounds truth in reality, not just mental construction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البسائط من الموجهات. والموجّهات خمسة عشر : ثمانية منها بسيطة، وسبعة منها مركبة. مادة عين تلك الكيفية الثابتة في نفس الأمر، أو غيرها. ثم إذا وجدت في اللفظ أوردت عبارة تدل على تلك الكيفية المعتبرة عند العقل؛ إذ الألفاظ إنما هي بإزاء الصور العقلية؛ فالكيفية الثابتة في نفس الأمر هي القضية، والثابتة لها في العقل هي جهة القضية المعقولة، والعبارة الدالة عليها هي جهة القضية الملفوظة، والقضية التي ذكرت فيه الجهة تسمّى موجّهة لاشتمالها على الجهة ورباعية أيضا؛ لاشتمالها على أربعة أجزاء، رابعها الجهة، والعبارة الدالة مطابقة للمادة كانت القضية صادقة وإلا كاذبة. ثم لا يخفى عليك أن القدماء ذهبوا إلى أن المادة ليست كيفية كل نسبة، بل كيفية النسبة الإيجابية فقط. والبسط في مبسوطات الفن. والموجّهات إلخ المشهور أن القضايا الموجّهة التي جرت العادة بالبحث عنها ثلاث عشرة: ست منها بسائط، وسبع مركبات. ولهم موجهات أخرى يبحثون عنها على سبيل القدرة دون العادة وارتقى عددها إلى أكثر من عشرين. وأما الموجهات الغير المبحوث عنها فهي غير محصورة في عدد. المصنف جعل الموجهات المبحوث عنها ههنا خمسة عشر، وعدّ منها الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة أيضا. مي خمسة عشر إلخ لا يخفى أن المعدود ههنا مؤنث وهي قضية؛ فكان يجب تجريد "خمسة" من التاء؛ لأنها تجري على خلاف القياس، ويجب إلحاق التاء لـ "عشرة" ؛ لأنها عند التركيب تجري على القياس، وقد يوجه إلحاق التاء بخمسة ههنا بأن المعدود محذوف، ومحل مخالفة القياس إذا ذكر المعدود.
The simple [modals] from the modals. The modals are fifteen: eight of them are simple, and seven are compound. The matter is that same quality fixed in reality, or other than it. Then when it is found in the utterance, a phrase is produced indicating that quality considered by the intellect; for utterances are only in correspondence with mental forms. So the quality fixed in reality is the proposition (al-qadiyya), and the quality fixed for it in the intellect is the modality of the intelligible proposition (jiha al-qadiyya al-ma'qula), and the phrase indicating it is the modality of the uttered proposition (jiha al-qadiyya al-malfuza). The proposition in which the modality is mentioned is called 'modal' (muwajjaha) because it includes the modality, and also 'quadripartite' because it includes four parts, the fourth being the modality. The phrase indicating it, if it corresponds to the matter, the proposition is true; otherwise false. Then it is not hidden from you that the ancients held that the matter is not a quality of every relation, but only a quality of the affirmative relation. The elaboration is in the detailed works of the art. As for the modals, the well-known [view] is that the modal propositions customarily investigated are thirteen: six simple and seven compound. They have other modals they investigate out of capability, not custom, and their number exceeds twenty. As for the modals not investigated, they are not limited in number. The author made the investigated modals here fifteen, and included among them the absolute temporal and the absolute distributed. 'Mim' [marginal note]: 'Fifteen etc.' It is not hidden that the counted here is feminine, namely 'proposition'; so it was necessary to remove the 'ta' from 'khamsa' because it goes against the analogy, and to attach 'ta' to 'ashara' because in composition it follows analogy. And it may be justified to attach 'ta' to 'khamsa' here by [saying] that the counted is omitted, and the place of violating analogy is when the counted is mentioned.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage introduces the classification of modal propositions (al-muwajjahat). The author states that there are fifteen modals: eight simple and seven compound. Then the commentator defines the two key components: the 'matter' (madda) and the 'modality' (jiha). The matter is the quality of the relation between subject and predicate as it exists in reality—either affirmative or negative. The modality is the quality of that relation as perceived by the intellect, and it is expressed by a phrase (like 'necessarily' or 'always'). A proposition that includes such a modality is called 'modal' (muwajjaha) and also 'quadripartite' because it has four parts: subject, predicate, copula, and modality. The truth of the proposition depends on whether the phrase corresponds to the actual matter. The commentator notes that the ancients believed matter applies only to affirmative relations. He then discusses the count: while the common view is thirteen modals (six simple, seven compound), some exceed twenty. The author of this work chooses fifteen, including some less common ones like the absolute temporal and absolute distributed. Finally, a grammatical note is added about the agreement of numbers with the feminine word 'qadiyya' (proposition) in the count 'fifteen'.

Key Terms

جهة
موجّهة

Key Takeaway
A modal proposition is quadripartite, and its truth depends on the correspondence between the linguistic modality and the real matter (quality of the relation).

Classification of Modal Propositions: Simple vs Compound
Classifying modal propositions into simple and compound with varying counts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify modal propositions as simple (basīṭah) or compound (murakkabah), and recall the different counts given by various logicians.

Core Idea
Not all modals are created equal; the tradition counts them differently.

The passage tells us that according to the author, modals are fifteen: eight simple and seven compound. However, the more common view is that they are thirteen: six simple and seven compound. Why the difference? The classification depends on whether the modality is a single concept or a combination. This lesson explores the basis of this classification and the variance in numbers, including marginal notes on grammar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the total number and breakdown
The author says modals are fifteen: 'ثمانية منها بسيطة، وسبعة منها مركبة' (eight simple, seven compound). Contrast with the common view: thirteen (six simple, seven compound).
Note that the compound count (7) is the same in both; only the simple count differs.
2
Understand why the author includes extra modals
The passage says the author included 'الوقتية المطلقة' (absolute temporal) and 'المنتشرة المطلقة' (absolute distributed). These are normally not counted in the standard thirteen.
This shows the author is expanding the set.
3
Recognize the grammatical note
The marginal note ('مي') discusses the agreement of the feminine 'qadiyya' with the number 'fifteen' (خمسة عشر). It notes an exception to the usual rule.
This is a grammatical aside, typical in shurūh.
4
Reflect on why the number varies
The passage mentions that some logicians investigated up to twenty modals 'على سبيل القدرة دون العادة' (out of capability, not custom). The author chooses a specific set for this work.
This indicates that the classification is not fixed; it depends on the purpose of the study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البسائط من الموجهات. والموجّهات خمسة عشر : ثمانية منها بسيطة، وسبعة منها مركبة. مادة عين تلك الكيفية الثابتة في نفس الأمر، أو غيرها. ثم إذا وجدت في اللفظ أوردت عبارة تدل على تلك الكيفية المعتبرة عند العقل؛ إذ الألفاظ إنما هي بإزاء الصور العقلية؛ فالكيفية الثابتة في نفس الأمر هي القضية، والثابتة لها في العقل هي جهة القضية المعقولة، والعبارة الدالة عليها هي جهة القضية الملفوظة، والقضية التي ذكرت فيه الجهة تسمّى موجّهة لاشتمالها على الجهة ورباعية أيضا؛ لاشتمالها على أربعة أجزاء، رابعها الجهة، والعبارة الدالة مطابقة للمادة كانت القضية صادقة وإلا كاذبة. ثم لا يخفى عليك أن القدماء ذهبوا إلى أن المادة ليست كيفية كل نسبة، بل كيفية النسبة الإيجابية فقط. والبسط في مبسوطات الفن. والموجّهات إلخ المشهور أن القضايا الموجّهة التي جرت العادة بالبحث عنها ثلاث عشرة: ست منها بسائط، وسبع مركبات. ولهم موجهات أخرى يبحثون عنها على سبيل القدرة دون العادة وارتقى عددها إلى أكثر من عشرين. وأما الموجهات الغير المبحوث عنها فهي غير محصورة في عدد. المصنف جعل الموجهات المبحوث عنها ههنا خمسة عشر، وعدّ منها الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة أيضا. مي خمسة عشر إلخ لا يخفى أن المعدود ههنا مؤنث وهي قضية؛ فكان يجب تجريد "خمسة" من التاء؛ لأنها تجري على خلاف القياس، ويجب إلحاق التاء لـ "عشرة" ؛ لأنها عند التركيب تجري على القياس، وقد يوجه إلحاق التاء بخمسة ههنا بأن المعدود محذوف، ومحل مخالفة القياس إذا ذكر المعدود.
The simple [modals] from the modals. The modals are fifteen: eight of them are simple, and seven are compound. The matter is that same quality fixed in reality, or other than it. Then when it is found in the utterance, a phrase is produced indicating that quality considered by the intellect; for utterances are only in correspondence with mental forms. So the quality fixed in reality is the proposition (al-qadiyya), and the quality fixed for it in the intellect is the modality of the intelligible proposition (jiha al-qadiyya al-ma'qula), and the phrase indicating it is the modality of the uttered proposition (jiha al-qadiyya al-malfuza). The proposition in which the modality is mentioned is called 'modal' (muwajjaha) because it includes the modality, and also 'quadripartite' because it includes four parts, the fourth being the modality. The phrase indicating it, if it corresponds to the matter, the proposition is true; otherwise false. Then it is not hidden from you that the ancients held that the matter is not a quality of every relation, but only a quality of the affirmative relation. The elaboration is in the detailed works of the art. As for the modals, the well-known [view] is that the modal propositions customarily investigated are thirteen: six simple and seven compound. They have other modals they investigate out of capability, not custom, and their number exceeds twenty. As for the modals not investigated, they are not limited in number. The author made the investigated modals here fifteen, and included among them the absolute temporal and the absolute distributed. 'Mim' [marginal note]: 'Fifteen etc.' It is not hidden that the counted here is feminine, namely 'proposition'; so it was necessary to remove the 'ta' from 'khamsa' because it goes against the analogy, and to attach 'ta' to 'ashara' because in composition it follows analogy. And it may be justified to attach 'ta' to 'khamsa' here by [saying] that the counted is omitted, and the place of violating analogy is when the counted is mentioned.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage introduces the classification of modal propositions (al-muwajjahat). The author states that there are fifteen modals: eight simple and seven compound. Then the commentator defines the two key components: the 'matter' (madda) and the 'modality' (jiha). The matter is the quality of the relation between subject and predicate as it exists in reality—either affirmative or negative. The modality is the quality of that relation as perceived by the intellect, and it is expressed by a phrase (like 'necessarily' or 'always'). A proposition that includes such a modality is called 'modal' (muwajjaha) and also 'quadripartite' because it has four parts: subject, predicate, copula, and modality. The truth of the proposition depends on whether the phrase corresponds to the actual matter. The commentator notes that the ancients believed matter applies only to affirmative relations. He then discusses the count: while the common view is thirteen modals (six simple, seven compound), some exceed twenty. The author of this work chooses fifteen, including some less common ones like the absolute temporal and absolute distributed. Finally, a grammatical note is added about the agreement of numbers with the feminine word 'qadiyya' (proposition) in the count 'fifteen'.

The Necessary Absolute Modal Proposition (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa)
Author defines the necessary absolute modal proposition and explains its name and logical properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the necessary absolute modal proposition and distinguish it from the permanent modal proposition.

Core Idea
Necessary absolute: predicate holds necessarily as long as the subject's essence exists – no extra conditions.

Modal propositions qualify how the predicate relates to the subject. The first simple modal is the necessary absolute, which declares that the predicate belongs to the subject necessarily for as long as the subject's essence exists. Understanding this distinction is crucial because later modals add temporal or conditional restrictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of necessary absolute
Examine the Arabic definition: 'it is that in which it is judged with the necessity of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject, or its negation from it, as long as the essence of the subject exists.' Note the two examples: 'Man is necessarily an animal' and 'Man is not necessarily a stone.'
The proposition can be affirmative or negative.
2
Analyze the name 'necessary absolute'
The author explains: it is called 'necessary' because the judgment involves necessity (impossibility of separation), and 'absolute' because the judgment is not restricted by any additional description or time. The modality is tied only to the subject's existence.
This contrasts with modals that require a condition like 'while writing' or 'at all times'.
3
Distinguish necessity from permanence
Necessity (ḍarūra) means the relation cannot be separated from the subject; permanence (dawām) means the relation holds at all times. The passage states that necessity is more specific: every necessary proposition is permanent, but not every permanent proposition is necessary. For example, 'The sky is moving' might be permanent but not necessary in the same sense.
This logical relation is a key tool in later debates.
4
Apply the distinction to Islamic examples
Take a Kalam statement: 'God is necessarily knowing.' This is necessary absolute because God's knowledge is inseparable from His essence. Compare: 'The sun rises every day' is permanent but not necessary (it could, in principle, be otherwise). Practice identifying which category a given proposition falls into.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما البسائط فأحدها : الضرورية المطلقة : وهي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودة، كقولك: "الإنسان حيوان بالضرورة، والإنسان ليس بحجر بالضرورة". الضرورية المطلقة إلخ إنما سمّيت ضرورية لاشتمالها على الضرورة، وإنما سمّيت مطلقة؛ لأن الحكم فيها غير مقيد بوصف أو وقت. واعلم أن مفهوم الضرورة امتناع انفكاك النسبة عن الموضوع ومفهوم الدوام شمول النسبة جميع الأزمنة والأوقات؛ فالنسبة بين الضرورية والدائمة عموم وخصوص مطلقا، والضرورية أخص.
As for the simple [modals], the first of them is the necessary absolute: it is that in which it is judged with the necessity of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject, or its negation from it, as long as the essence of the subject exists, like your saying: 'Man is necessarily an animal, and man is not necessarily a stone.' The necessary absolute etc.: It was called 'necessary' because it includes necessity, and it was called 'absolute' because the judgment in it is not restricted by a description or a time. And know that the concept of necessity is the impossibility of the separation of the relation from the subject, and the concept of permanence is the inclusion of the relation [over] all times and seasons; so the relation between the necessary and the permanent is absolute generality and particularity, and the necessary is more specific.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage defines the first simple modal: the necessary absolute (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa). The author explains that it is a judgment affirming or negating the predicate of the subject with necessity as long as the subject's essence exists. For example, 'Man is necessarily an animal' holds true for every human as long as the human exists. The commentator then explains the name: 'necessary' because it contains the modality of necessity (ḍarūra), and 'absolute' because the judgment is not qualified by an additional condition or time—unlike modals that require a specific description or time. The commentator further contrasts necessity with permanence (dawām): necessity means the relation cannot be separated from the subject, while permanence means the relation holds at all times. The necessary modal is more specific than the permanent modal because every necessary proposition is permanent, but not vice versa. For example, 'Every human is an animal' is both necessary and permanent, while 'Every celestial sphere is constantly moving' is permanent but not necessary in the same sense.

Key Takeaway
The necessary absolute modal proposition is the bedrock for understanding modal logic: it asserts a necessary relation tied only to the subject's existence, distinct from mere temporal permanence.

The Permanent Absolute Modal Proposition
Author defines the permanent absolute modal proposition and explains its naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the permanent absolute modal proposition and distinguish it from the necessary absolute.

Core Idea
Permanent absolute: temporal continuity, not necessity.

In the previous lesson, we learned about the necessary absolute, which asserts that the predicate holds for the subject with necessity. Now, we encounter a related but distinct modality: the permanent absolute. It also asserts continuous affirmation or negation, but without the claim of necessity. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing modal propositions in Islamic logic, especially in theological and philosophical contexts where the nature of temporal vs. necessary truths matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality
Locate the phrase 'dāʾima muṭlaqa' in the Arabic. Note that it is a composite term: 'dāʾima' indicates permanence, 'muṭlaqa' indicates absolute (no additional condition).
2
Understand the judgment
The proposition judges that the predicate is permanently affirmed of the subject or permanently negated. This means at all times the subject exists, the predicate holds or does not hold.
The key is continuity over the subject's existence, not necessity.
3
Analyze the example
Consider: 'Every celestial sphere is permanently moving' and 'No celestial sphere is permanently stationary.' This is a factual claim about the actual world, not a claim of metaphysical necessity.
The example illustrates permanence without necessity.
4
Compare with necessary absolute
The necessary absolute asserts that the predicate cannot be separated from the subject. The permanent absolute only asserts continuous attachment over time, not impossibility of separation. Thus permanence is a weaker modality.
This distinction is vital for correct modal reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثانية الدائمة المطلقة وهي التي حكم فيها بدوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، أو سلبه عنه، كقولك: "كل فلك متحرك بالدوام، ولا شيء من الفلك بساكن بالدوام". الدائمة المطلقة إلخ وجه التسمية على قياس ما مرّ في الضرورية المطلقة.
And the second is the permanent absolute: it is that in which it is judged with the permanence of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject, or its negation from it, like your saying: 'Every celestial sphere is permanently moving, and no celestial sphere is permanently stationary.' The permanent absolute etc.: The reason for the naming is analogous to what passed in the necessary absolute.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage defines the second simple modal: the permanent absolute (al-dāʾima al-muṭlaqa). The author states that it involves a judgment of permanent affirmation or negation of the predicate for the subject. The example given is 'Every celestial sphere is permanently moving' and 'No celestial sphere is permanently stationary.' This means that as long as the celestial sphere exists, it is always moving, and never stationary. The commentator simply notes that the naming is analogous to the necessary absolute: it is called 'permanent' because of the modality of permanence (dawām), and 'absolute' because no additional condition is attached. The key difference from the necessary absolute is that permanence does not include the impossibility of separation; it only asserts continuous subject-predicate relation over time, not that it must be so. For instance, 'Every celestial sphere is permanently moving' is true in the actual world, but it does not assert that the movement is metaphysically necessary.

Key Takeaway
The permanent absolute proposition asserts the predicate's continuous attachment to the subject over time, without implying that the relation is necessary.

The General Conditional Modal and the Descriptive Attribute
Defines the general conditional modal and elaborates on the descriptive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the general conditional modal from other modals by identifying the condition of the descriptive attribute, and classify the relationship between the descriptive attribute and the essence of the subject.

Core Idea
A general conditional modal ties necessity to a descriptive attribute, which may be essential or accidental.

Modal logic classifies propositions based on the modality of their judgment. The third simple modal is the 'general conditional' (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma), where the necessity of the predicate depends on the subject being described by a certain attribute. This attribute, called the descriptive attribute (waṣf ʿunwānī), is the concept of the subject term, and its relationship to the actual individuals (dhāt) varies. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments where necessity is conditional rather than absolute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal definition
Locate the passage where the author defines the general conditional modal. Notice the key phrase: 'as long as the essence of the subject is described by the descriptive attribute'. This is the condition that distinguishes it from absolute necessity.
The condition is not on the existence of the subject, but on its description.
2
Analyze the descriptive attribute
The commentator explains that the descriptive attribute is the concept (mafhūm) of the subject, not the individuals. Examples: 'human' (species), 'animal' (genus), 'laugher' (accident). For each, determine whether the concept is identical to, part of, or external to the essence.
This step helps to see that the attribute can be essential or accidental.
3
Distinguish essence from description
The essence (dhāt) refers to the actual individuals. The necessity in a general conditional holds only under the description, not always. Example: 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer' – the necessity applies only during writing, not at all times.
Contrast this with absolute necessity: 'Every human is an animal' (always necessary) vs 'Every writer is moving fingers' (conditional).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالثة: المشروطة العامة وهي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو نفيه عنه ما دام ذات الموضوع موصوفا بالوصف العنواني. والوصف العنواني عندهم ما عبر به عن الموضوع كقولنا: "كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة ما دام كاتبا، ولا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع بالضرورة ما دام كاتبا". المشروطة العامة إلخ أما تسميتها بالمشروطة؛ فلاشتمالها على شرط الوصف وبالعامة؛ فلأنها أعم من المشروطة الخاصة التي ستعرفها في المركبات. والوصف العنواني إلخ: اعلم أن ما صدق عليه الموضوع من الأفراد يسمّى ذات الموضوع، ومفهوم الموضوع يسمّى وصف الموضوع وعنوانه، ويقال له: الوصف العنواني. وهو قد يكون عين الذات إن كان عنوانا للنوع كقولنا كل إنسان حيوان؛ فإن مفهوم الإنسان عين ماهية أفراده. وقد يكون جزء له إن كان عنوانا للجنس والفصل كقولنا كل حيوان حساس؛ فإن مفهوم الحيوان جزء ماهية أفراده. وقد يكون خارجا عنه إن كان عنوانا للخاصة أو العرض العام كقولنا: كل ضاحك، أو كل ماش حيوان، فإن مفهوم الضاحك والماشي خارج عن ذات الموضوع أي أفراده. وبما ذكرنا يحصل الفرق الجلي بين الوصف والذات فليتأمل. كل كاتب إلخ فإن ثبوت التحرك للكاتب وسلب السكون عنه ليس ضروريا ما دام ذاته موجودة بل ضروري بشرط الوصف، وهو الكتابة.
And the third: the general conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma). It is that in which it is judged with the necessity of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject or its negation from it as long as the essence of the subject is described by the descriptive attribute (al-waṣf al-ʿunwānī). The descriptive attribute according to them is that by which the subject is expressed, like our saying: 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, and no writer is necessarily still-fingered as long as he is a writer.' The general conditional etc.: It was called 'conditional' because it includes a condition of the description, and 'general' because it is more general than the specific conditional which you will know among the compound [modals]. The descriptive attribute etc.: Know that the individuals to which the subject applies are called the essence (dhāt) of the subject, and the concept (mafhūm) of the subject is called the description (waṣf) and title (ʿunwān) of the subject, and it is called the descriptive attribute. It may be identical to the essence if it is a title for the species, like our saying 'Every human is an animal'; the concept of human is the same as the quiddity of its individuals. It may be a part of it if it is a title for the genus or differentia, like our saying 'Every animal is sentient'; the concept of animal is a part of the quiddity of its individuals. And it may be external to it if it is a title for a property (khāṣṣa) or a general accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm), like our saying 'Every laugher' or 'Every walker is an animal'; the concept of laugher or walker is external to the essence of the subject, i.e., its individuals. With what we have mentioned, the clear distinction between the description and the essence is obtained, so let it be pondered. 'Every writer etc.': The affirmation of movement for the writer and the negation of stillness from it is not necessary as long as his essence exists, but rather necessary under the condition of the description, i.e., writing.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage defines the third simple modal: the general conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma). The author explains that it asserts necessity of the predicate for the subject as long as the subject is qualified by a specific descriptive attribute (al-waṣf al-ʿunwānī). For example, 'Every writer is necessarily moving his fingers as long as he is a writer'—the necessity holds only while the writer is actually writing. The commentator then clarifies the concept of 'descriptive attribute': it is the meaning (concept) of the subject expression itself. He distinguishes between the essence (dhāt) of the subject—the actual individuals—and the description (waṣf) which is the concept. The description can be identical to the essence (as in species terms like 'human'), a part of it (as in genus terms like 'animal'), or external to it (as in accidents like 'laugher'). The example of 'writer' (kātib) is an accident; writing is not part of the essence of a human, so the necessity of moving fingers is conditional upon the act of writing, not upon the existence of the human. Thus, this modal is called 'general conditional' because the condition is a descriptive attribute, and it is 'general' because it encompasses both essential and accidental descriptions.

Key Terms
مشروطة عامة (mashrūṭa ʿāmma): A modal proposition that asserts necessity of the predicate as long as the subject is described by a certain attribute (waṣf ʿunwānī), which may be essential or accidental. [Note: In later texts, 'mashrūṭa' often refers specifically to conditional necessity; here it includes both essential and accidental conditions.]
وصف عنواني (waṣf ʿunwānī): The descriptive attribute is the concept (mafhūm) of the subject term, which may be identical to, part of, or external to the essence of the subject's individuals. [Note: This term is specific to modal logic; it is used to distinguish the subject's meaning from its instances.]
ذات الموضوع (dhāt al-mawḍūʿ): The essence of the subject refers to the actual individuals that fall under the subject term, as opposed to the concept. [Note: Used consistently in Islamic logic for the referent of a term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The existence of the writer's essence
B) The writer being described as a writer
C) The writer being human
D) The writer being alive

Key Takeaway
The general conditional modal asserts necessity under a descriptive attribute that may be essential or accidental, and understanding the relation between description and essence is key to applying it correctly.

The Absolute Temporal Modal Proposition
Definition of the fifth simple modal: absolute temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an absolute temporal modal proposition and distinguish it from the permanent and conditional modals.

Core Idea
Absolute temporal necessity: true only at a specific moment, not forever.

Modal propositions in Islamic logic classify how a predicate relates to a subject across time. The absolute temporal modal (al-waqtiyya al-muṭlaqa) captures events that are necessarily true only at a particular time, like a lunar eclipse. Understanding this modal helps in analyzing temporary states in kalam, fiqh, and other Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Note that the proposition is called 'temporal' because it includes a specific time (waqt), and 'absolute' because it is not constrained by permanence or its absence.
2
Extract the definition
The passage defines it: a judgment of necessity of the predicate for the subject (or its negation) at a specific time among the times of the subject's existence.
3
Analyze the example
The example 'Every moon is necessarily eclipsed when the earth comes between it and the sun' shows that the necessity holds only at the moment of occlusion, not before or after.
4
Distinguish from the permanent modal
The permanent modal asserts necessity at all times of the subject's existence; the absolute temporal does not. The example is necessary only at a specific time, so it is not permanent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخامسة: الوقتية المطلقة وهي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، أو نفيه عنه في وقت معين من أوقات الذات كما تقول : "كل قمر منخسف بالضرورة وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس". الوقتية المطلقة إلخ أما تسميتها بالوقتية فلاشتمالها على الوقت، وبالمطلقة؛ فلعدم تقييدها باللادوام.
And the fifth: the absolute temporal (al-waqtiyya al-muṭlaqa). It is that in which it is judged with the necessity of the affirmation of the predicate to the subject, or its negation from it, at a specific time among the times of the essence, as you say: 'Every moon is necessarily eclipsed when the earth comes between it and the sun.' The absolute temporal etc.: It was called 'temporal' because it includes time, and 'absolute' because it is not restricted by the lack of permanence (i.e., it does not require or deny permanence).
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth simple modal: the absolute temporal (al-waqtiyya al-muṭlaqa). It asserts the necessity of the predicate for the subject at a specific time within the subject's existence. The example is 'Every moon is necessarily eclipsed when the earth comes between it and the sun'—this necessity holds only at that specific time, not before or after. The commentator explains the name: 'temporal' (waqtiyya) because it includes a time (waqt), and 'absolute' (muṭlaqa) because it is not qualified by the absence of permanence (i.e., it does not claim that the necessity holds at all times, nor that it fails to hold at other times; it simply states a temporal event). This modal is important for describing events that occur at a specific moment, such as lunar eclipses. Unlike the permanent or necessary modals, it does not claim that the predicate holds for the entire duration of the subject's existence.

Key Terms
وقتية مطلقة

Key Takeaway
The absolute temporal modal describes a necessary relation that holds only at a specific moment, making it a vital tool for analyzing temporary events in Islamic sciences.

Understanding al-'Urfiyya al-'Amma (Customary General Proposition)
Author defines the fourth modal type with permanent judgment conditional on attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct examples of al-'urfiyya al-'amma and distinguish it from necessity-based modal propositions.

Core Idea
Permanence, not necessity, is the key – the predicate holds as long as the description applies.

In this passage, the author defines the fourth type of modal proposition: al-'urfiyya al-'amma (customary general). Unlike earlier types that involve necessity (wujub), this type involves permanence (dawam). The judgment is that the predicate holds continuously for the subject as long as the subject is described by a certain attribute. This distinction is crucial in Islamic logic, particularly when evaluating conditional claims in theological and jurisprudential arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core concept
The passage defines a new modal type. Notice the key term 'دَوَام' (permanence). It is not necessity but continuous duration conditional on the subject's attribute.
Contrast with earlier types that use 'ضرورة' (necessity).
2
Extract the condition
The judgment holds 'مَا دَامَ ذَاتُ الْمَوْضُوعِ مُتَّصِفًا بِالْوَصْفِ الْعُنْوَانِيِّ' (as long as the subject's essence is characterized by the attributive description). This is the critical condition.
The description is the 'title' (عنوان) that defines when the judgment applies.
3
Analyze the examples
The examples: 'Permanently every writer is a mover of fingers as long as he is a writer' and 'Permanently no sleeper is awake as long as he is sleeping.' Notice that the predicate is not necessary in itself; it is only permanent given the condition.
The negative example shows the same structure with negation.
4
Understand the naming rationale
The author explains why it is called 'customary' (عرفية) – because common custom understands this meaning from absolute negative statements, e.g., 'No sleeper is awake' implies the negation lasts as long as the subject is sleeping. 'General' (عامة) because it applies to any subject meeting the description, unlike a more specific type.
This naming connects logical terminology to everyday understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابعة: العرفية العامة: التي حكم فيها بدوام ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه ما دام ذات الموضوع متصفا بالوصف العنواني، كقولنا: "بالدوام كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع مادام كاتبا، وبالدوام لا شيء من النائم بمستيقظ ما دام نائما. العرفية العامة إلخ إنما سميت عرفية؛ لأن العرف العام إنما يفهم هذا المعنى من السالبة إذا أطلقت، حتى إذا قيل: "لا شيء من النائم بمستيقظ" يفهم منه أن المستيقظ مسلوب عن النائم ما دام نائما. وعامة؛ لأنها أعم من العرفية الخاصة التي هي من المركبات.
And the fourth: the customary general, which is that in which it is judged with permanence of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or its negatiom from it as long as the subject's essence is characterized by the attributive description, like our saying: 'Permanently every writer is a mover of fingers as long as he is a writer, and permanently no sleeper is awake as long as he is sleeping.' The customary general etc.: It is only called customary because the common custom understands this meaning from the negative when it is absolute, such that when it is said: 'No sleeper is awake', it is understood from it that wakefulness is negated from the sleeper as long as he is sleeping. And 'general' because it is more general than the specific customary which is among the compound propositions.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type, al-'urfiyya al-'amma (customary general). Unlike the previous conditional type that deals with necessity, this type deals with permanence (dawam). The judgment is that the predicate holds permanently for the subject as long as the subject is described by the attributive description. Examples: 'Permanently every writer is a mover of fingers as long as he is a writer' and 'Permanently no sleeper is awake as long as he is sleeping.' The author explains the naming: 'customary' because common custom understands this meaning from an absolute negative statement; for example, if one says 'No sleeper is awake', it is understood that wakefulness is negated as long as the person is sleeping. 'General' because it is more general than the specific customary type which will be discussed in compound propositions.

Key Terms

دوام

Key Takeaway
Al-'urfiyya al-'amma describes a permanent judgment that holds as long as the subject's attribute applies, not a necessary or unconditional relation.

Defining the Temporal Absolute (al-Waqtiyya al-Mutlaqa)
Author defines the fifth modal type with necessity at a specific time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the temporal absolute modal proposition using its genus (modal judgment) and differentia (necessity at a specific time), and apply it to an example from Islamic astronomy.

Core Idea
Temporal absolute binds necessity to a specific time, not to all times.

In this lesson, we examine the fifth modal type: al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa (temporal absolute). This type involves a judgment that is necessary at a particular moment within the subject's existence, but not necessarily permanent. The author defines it and illustrates it with the example of lunar eclipses, which only occur at a specific astronomical configuration. Understanding this modality sharpens your ability to analyze time-bound claims in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definiendum and its genus
Find the term 'al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa' in the Arabic. The genus is a modal proposition (qadiyya wujubiya) that involves necessity.
2
Extract the differentia (fasl)
The differentia is that the judgment of affirmation or negation is necessary 'at a specific time from among the times of the essence'. This time-specific necessity distinguishes it from other modal types.
The word 'mutlaqa' here means 'not restricted by permanence', not 'unconditional'.
3
Analyze the example provided
The author gives two statements: 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of opposition' and 'No moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of quadrature'. Check that each statement is necessary only at that specific time, not at all times.
The example uses an astronomical phenomenon familiar in Islamic science.
4
Examine the commentator's expansion
The commentator explains the astronomical terms (quadrature and opposition) and why the moon eclipses only at opposition: because the moon borrows light from the sun and is eclipsed when the earth interposes. This reinforces the time-specific nature of the necessity.
This expansion shows how a concrete example grounds an abstract logical definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخامسة: الوقتية المطلقة وهي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، أو نفيه عنه في وقت معين من أوقات الذات كما تقول: "كل" قمر منخسف بالضرورة وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس، ولا شيء من القمر بمنخسف بالضرورة وقت التربيع. الوقتية المطلقة إلخ أما تسميتها بالوقتية فلاشتمالها على الوقت، وبالمطلقة؛ فلعدم تقييدها باللادوام. وقت التربيع إلخ التربيع كون القمر في البرج الرابع من البرج الذي فيه الشمس؛ فلا ينخسف القمر في هذا الوقت، وإنما ينخسف عند حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس، وهو وقت المقابلة، وذلك بأنه يقع ظلّ الأرض على وجه القمر فيظلم؛ لأن القمر ليس بنوراني بالذات عندهم، بل جرمه كدر، إنما يقتبس النور من الشمس. والتفصيل في كتب الهيأة.
And the fifth: the temporal absolute, which is that in which it is judged with necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject, or its negation from it, at a specific time from among the times of the essence, as you say: 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun, and no moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of quadrature.' The temporal absolute etc.: As for its naming 'temporal', it is because it includes time, and 'absolute' because it is not restricted by permanence. The time of quadrature etc.: Quadrature is the moon being in the fourth house from the house containing the sun; so the moon does not eclipse at this time, but rather eclipses only when the earth interposes between it and the sun, which is the time of opposition, and that is because the earth's shadow falls on the moon's face and it darkens, because the moon according to them is not luminous by itself, but its body is turbid; it only borrows light from the sun. The details are in the books of astronomy.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth type, al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa (temporal absolute). The judgment here is necessary at a specific time within the subject's existence. The example given is the lunar eclipse: 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun' and 'No moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of quadrature.' The author explains the naming: 'temporal' because it includes a specific time, and 'absolute' because it is not restricted by permanence (i.e., it does not claim the judgment holds at all times). The commentary further explains the astronomical concept of quadrature and opposition, noting that the moon is not self-luminous but borrows light from the sun. The author refers to astronomy books for details.

Key Terms
الوقتية المطلقة
التربيع

Defining the Diffused Absolute: Necessity at an Unspecified Time
Author defines the sixth modal type with necessity at an unspecified time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish al-muntashira al-mutlaqa from other modal types and identify its defining feature of unspecified time.

Core Idea
Diffused absolute: necessary at some time, but we don't know when.

We have seen modal propositions that specify a time (waqtiyya). Now we encounter a subtle variation: the judgment is necessary, but the time is left unspecified. This type, called the diffused absolute, is crucial for understanding how necessity can attach to a subject without commitment to a particular moment. Let us unpack its definition and naming.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic. What is it called?
The term is 'المنتشرة المطلقة' (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa).
2
Extract the essential condition
What is the core logical condition? It is judgment with necessity at an unspecified time of the subject's existence.
The time is 'غير معين' (unspecified).
3
Analyse the examples
How do the examples (every animal breathes necessarily at some time, and no stone breathes necessarily) illustrate the condition?
They show both affirmative and negative cases with necessity at an unspecified time.
4
Understand the naming
Why 'diffused' and 'absolute'? How does the name reflect the logical property?
'Diffused' because the judgment can apply at any time, and 'absolute' as in the previous type, meaning not restricted by permanence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسادسة: المنتشرة المطلقة وهي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، أو نفيه عنه في وقت غير معين من أوقات الذات نحو كل حيوان متنفس بالضرورة وقتا ما، ولا شيء من الحجر بمتنفس بالضرورة. المنتشرة المطلقة إلخ إنما سميت "منتشرة"؛ لاحتمال الحكم فيها كل وقت؛ فيكون منتشرا في الأوقات، و"مطلقة"؛ لما ذكرنا في الوقتية المطلقة.
And the sixth: the diffused absolute, which is that in which it is judged with necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject, or its negation from it, at an unspecified time from among the times of the essence, such as every animal breathes necessarily at some time, and no stone breathes necessarily. The diffused absolute etc.: It is only called 'diffused' because the judgment is possible at every time; thus it is diffused throughout times, and 'absolute' for what we mentioned in the temporal absolute.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth type, al-muntashira al-mutlaqa (diffused absolute). The judgment is necessary at some unspecified time within the subject's existence. Examples: 'Every animal breathes necessarily at some time' and 'No stone breathes necessarily.' The author explains the naming: 'diffused' because the judgment can apply at any time, so it is spread (diffused) across times, and 'absolute' as in the previous type, meaning not restricted by permanence. The key difference from al-waqtiyya is that the time is not specified.

Key Terms

Key Takeaway
The diffused absolute modal proposition expresses necessity at some indefinite time of the subject's existence, spreading the judgment across all times without fixing it.

The Absolute General Proposition (al-Mutlaqa al-'Amma)
Author defines the seventh modal type with actual existence in one of three times.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the characteristics of al-mutlaqa al-'amma and distinguish it from modal propositions.

Core Idea
Absolute general: the unmodalized categorical that asserts actuality in one of three times.

You have already encountered modal propositions that qualify the relation with necessity, possibility, or permanence. Now we turn to the simplest form: the absolute general, which makes a plain categorical assertion about actual existence or non-existence. This is the default proposition when no modal qualification is added.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Notice that the passage defines a proposition that simply judges the existence or non-existence of the predicate for the subject. No modality like necessity or possibility is mentioned.
2
Understand the temporal reference
The judgment is about 'one of the three times' (past, present, or future). The proposition asserts that at some time, the predicate actually holds or does not hold for the subject.
This makes it temporal but not modal.
3
Analyze the naming
The author explains why it is called 'absolute' (mutlaqa): when a proposition is uttered without restriction, the listener understands actuality. And 'general' ('amma) because it is broader than non-permanent and non-necessary existential propositions.
The name comes from the absence of modal qualification.
4
Contrast with modal types
Compare this to necessary or permanent propositions. For example, 'Every human is mortal' is necessary (permanent), while 'Every human is laughing' is only actual at some times—the latter is absolute general.
The absolute general is the least restrictive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسابعة المطلقة العامة: وهي التي حكم فيها بوجود المحمول للموضوع، أو سلبه عنه أي في أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة كقولك: كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل، ولا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل. المطلقة العامة إلخ: إنما سميت "مطلقة"؛ لأن القضية إذا أطلقت من غير تقييد باللادوام أو اللاضرورة يفهم منه فعلية النسبة؛ فسميت القضية التي حكم فيها بفعلية النسبة "مطلقة" تسمية للمدلول باسم الدال، و"عامة"؛ لأنها أعم من الوجودية اللادائمة، والوجودية اللا ضرورية كما ستعرفها في المركبات.
And the seventh: the absolute general, which is that in which it is judged with existence of the predicate for the subject, or its negation from it, i.e., in one of the three times, like your saying: Every human is actually laughing, and no human is actually laughing. The absolute general etc.: It is only called 'absolute' because when a proposition is uttered absolutely without restriction by non-permanence or non-necessity, actuality of the relation is understood from it; so the proposition judged with actuality of the relation is called 'absolute' as a naming of the signification by the name of the signifier, and 'general' because it is more general than the non-permanent existential and the non-necessary existential as you will know in the compound propositions.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defines the seventh type, al-mutlaqa al-'amma (absolute general). The judgment asserts the actual existence of the predicate for the subject in one of the three times (past, present, or future). Examples: 'Every human is actually laughing' and 'No human is actually laughing.' The author explains the naming: 'absolute' (mutlaqa) because when a proposition is uttered without qualification, the actuality of the relation is understood; thus a proposition that judges actuality is called 'absolute' by metonymy. 'General' because it is more general than the non-permanent existential and non-necessary existential propositions that will be discussed in compound propositions. This type is the simplest categorical assertion without modal force.

Key Terms
المطلقة العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The absolute general proposition is the default categorical assertion of actual existence at some time, without any modal qualification.
