Understanding al-Mumkina al-'Amma (Possible General)
Author defines the eighth modal type with negation of necessity of the opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-mumkina al-'amma and explain the logical meaning of affirmative and negative general possible propositions using the negation of necessity of the opposite.

Core Idea
Possibility = negation of necessity of the opposite.

In this lesson we encounter the eighth modal proposition type: al-mumkina al-'amma (the general possible). Unlike everyday language where 'possible' suggests uncertainty, here it has a precise logical meaning: the opposite of the judgment is not necessary. This definition underpins classical Islamic modal logic and appears frequently in theological and legal reasoning about contingency and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum 'الممكنة العامة' (al-mumkina al-'amma). Note that it is the eighth modal type, and the author distinguishes it from other types by its unique judgment.
The term 'possible general' indicates it applies broadly, not limited to a specific modality.
2
Extract the logical definition
The passage states it is 'the proposition in which it is judged with negation of the necessity of the opposite side' (سلب ضرورة الجانب المخالف). This is the core: possibility is defined as the absence of necessity of the contradictory.
For an affirmative proposition, the opposite is the negation; for a negative, the opposite is the affirmation.
3
Distinguish affirmative and negative forms
Apply the definition: an affirmative general possible like 'كل نار حارة بالإمكان العام' means 'the negation of heat from fire is not necessary' (i.e., it is not necessarily not hot). A negative like 'لا شيء من النار ببارد بالإمكان العام' means 'the affirmation of cold for fire is not necessary' (i.e., it is not necessarily cold).
Thus, possibility does not assert actuality, only the non-necessity of the opposite.
4
Test understanding with an Islamic example
Consider the proposition 'The world is temporally originated with general possibility.' According to the definition, this means 'It is not necessary that the world is not temporally originated' – i.e., its temporal origination is not impossible. Recognise how this differs from asserting the world is actually temporally originated.
This logical tool allows theologians to discuss contingency without assuming actual existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثامنة: الممكنة العامة وهي التي حكم فيها بسلب ضرورة الجانب المخالف كقولك: "كل نار حارة بالإمكان العام ولا شيء من النار ببارد بالإمكان العام. الممكنة العامة إلخ سميت لاشتمالها على معنى الإمكان، و"عامة"؛ لكونها أعم من الممكنة الخاصة التي ستعرفها في المركبات. كل نار إلخ حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة السلب؛ إذ السلب خلاف النسبة، ولو لم يكن عدم ضرورة السلب لم يكن الإيجاب ممكنا. وقوله: "لا شيء من النار ببارد" بالإمكان العام حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة الإيجاب؛ إذ الإيجاب خلاف النسبة، ولو لم يكن عدم ضرورة الإيجاب لم يكن السلب ممكنا. فمعنى الموجبة أن سلب الحرارة عن النار ليس بضروري، ومعنى السالبة أن إيجاب البرودة للنار ليس بضروري.
And the eighth: the possible general, which is that in which it is judged with negation of the necessity of the opposite side, like your saying: 'Every fire is hot with general possibility, and no fire is cold with general possibility.' The possible general etc.: It is named for its inclusion of the meaning of possibility, and 'general' because it is more general than the specific possibility which you will know in the compound propositions. 'Every fire' etc.: It is judged with negation of the necessity of negation; because negation is the opposite of the relation, and if the necessity of negation were not absent, the affirmation would not be possible. And His saying: 'No fire is cold' with general possibility is judged with negation of the necessity of affirmation; because affirmation is the opposite of the relation, and if the necessity of affirmation were not absent, the negation would not be possible. So the meaning of the affirmative is that the negation of heat from fire is not necessary, and the meaning of the negative is that the affirmation of cold for fire is not necessary.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defines the eighth type, al-mumkina al-'amma (possible general). The judgment here is that the opposite side is not necessary. For an affirmative possible proposition, it means the negation of the predicate is not necessary; for a negative possible proposition, it means the affirmation of the predicate is not necessary. Examples: 'Every fire is hot with general possibility' means it is not necessary that fire is not hot (i.e., it can be hot). 'No fire is cold with general possibility' means it is not necessary that fire is cold (i.e., it can be not cold). The author explains that possibility is defined as the negation of necessity of the opposite. The term 'general' indicates it is more general than the specific possible proposition in compound propositions. This corresponds to the modern notion of 'possible' as 'not necessarily not'.

Key Terms
الممكنة العامة
الإمكان العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-mumkina al-'amma, what does the statement 'Every fire is hot with general possibility' mean?
A) Fire is necessarily hot.
B) Fire is actually hot.
C) The negation of heat for fire is not necessary.
D) Fire is cold.

Key Takeaway
Al-mumkina al-'amma defines possibility as the negation of necessity of the opposite, a key modal concept in Islamic logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Task 2: Consider the statement 'It is possible that the Prophet ﷺ performed the Isra' journey.' Identify whether this is an instance of al-mumkina al-'amma, and explain why by referring to the negation of necessity of the opposite.

6 min
The Widespread Absolute Proposition
Author defines the sixth simple modal proposition with example and gloss.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a widespread absolute proposition and distinguish it from other modal types.

Core Idea
The widespread absolute predicates necessity at an indefinite time, bridging permanence and temporality.

This lesson introduces the sixth simple modal proposition, the widespread absolute. It is called 'widespread' because the judgment applies at any time, and 'absolute' because it is unrestricted. Understanding this type is crucial for analyzing modal claims about natural kinds in disciplines like kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: al-muntashira al-mutlaqa (المنتشرة المطلقة). Note that it is the sixth simple modal proposition.
2
Extract the genus
What kind of thing is it? It is a modal proposition – a statement with a modal qualification.
The genus is 'modal proposition'.
3
Identify the differentia
What distinguishes it? The judgment is of necessity of the predicate's affirmation or denial at an unspecified time from the times of the essence.
The key is 'at an unspecified time' (وقت غير معين).
4
Analyze the example
Examine 'Every animal breathes by necessity at some time'. This fits: breathing is necessary for animals, but not at every moment; only at some indefinite time does it occur.
Contrast with 'every human laughs actually' – that is a general absolute, not necessity.
5
Compare with other absolute types
The widespread absolute differs from the temporal absolute (specific time) and the general absolute (actuality without necessity). Recognize that 'widespread' refers to the time being indefinite, not the predicate being spread over all times.
This distinction is often confused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البسائط من الموجهات الشمس، ولا شيء من القمر بمنخسف بالضرورة وقت التربيع. والسادسة: المنتشرة المطلقة وهي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، أو نفيه عنه في وقت غير معين من أوقات الذات نحو كل حيوان متنفس بالضرورة وقتا ما، ولا شيء من الحجر بمتنفس بالضرورة. والسابعة المطلقة العامة: وهي التي حكم فيها بوجود المحمول للموضوع، أو سلبه عنه أي في أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة كقولك: كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل، ولا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل. والثامنة: الممكنة العامة وهي التي حكم فيها بسلب ضرورة الجانب المخالف كقولك: كل نار حارة بالإمكان العام ولا شيء من النار ببارد بالإمكان العام. وقت التربيع إلخ التربيع كون القمر في البرج الرابع من البرج الذي فيه الشمس؛ فلا ينخسف القمر في هذا الوقت، وإنما ينخسف عند حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس، وهو وقت المقابلة، وذلك بأنه يقع ظلّ الأرض على وجه القمر فيظلم؛ لأن القمر ليس بنوراني بالذات عندهم، بل جرمه كدر، إنما يقتبس النور من الشمس. والتفصيل في كتب الهيأة المنتشرة المطلقة إلخ إنما سميت "منتشرة"؛ لاحتمال الحكم فيها كل وقت؛ فيكون منتشرا في الأوقات، و"مطلقة"؛ لما ذكرنا في الوقتية المطلقة. المطلقة العامة إلخ : إنما سميت "مطلقة"؛ لأن القضية إذا أطلقت من غير تقييد باللادوام أو اللاضرورة يفهم منه فعلية النسبة؛ فسميت القضية التي حكم فيها بفعلية النسبة "مطلقة" تسمية للمدلول باسم الدال، و"عامة"؛ لأنها أعم من الوجودية اللادائمة، والوجودية اللاضرورية كما ستعرفها في المركبات. الممكنة العامة إلخ سميت لاشتمالها على معنى الإمكان، و"عامة"؛ لكونها أعم من الممكنة الخاصة التي ستعرفها في المركبات كل نار إلخ حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة السلب؛ إذ السلب خلاف النسبة، ولو لم يكن عدم ضرورة السلب لم يكن الإيجاب ممكنا. وقوله: لا شيء من النار ببارد بالإمكان العام حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة الإيجاب؛ إذ الإيجاب خلاف النسبة، ولو لم يكن عدم ضرورة الإيجاب لم يكن السلب ممكنا فمعنى الموجبة أن سلب الحرارة عن النار ليس بضروري ومعنى السالبة أن إيجاب البرودة للنار ليس بضروري.
The simple modalities: The sun, and nothing of the moon is eclipsed by necessity at the time of quadrature. The sixth: the widespread absolute, which is the proposition in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate's affirmation to the subject, or its negation from it, at an unspecified time from the times of the essence, such as 'Every animal breathes by necessity at some time' and 'Nothing of stone breathes by necessity'. The seventh: the general absolute, which is the proposition in which judgment is made of the existence of the predicate for the subject, or its negation from it, i.e., in one of the three times, as you say: 'Every human laughs actually' and 'Nothing of human laughs actually'. The eighth: the general possible, which is the proposition in which judgment is made of the negation of necessity of the contradictory side, as you say: 'Every fire is hot with general possibility' and 'Nothing of fire is cold with general possibility'. 'Time of quadrature etc.': quadrature is the moon being in the fourth sign from the sign in which the sun is; so the moon is not eclipsed at this time, but is eclipsed when the earth interposes between it and the sun, which is the time of opposition, because the earth's shadow falls on the moon's face and it darkens, for the moon is not luminous in itself according to them, but its body is dark, it only borrows light from the sun; the detail is in astronomical books. 'The widespread absolute etc.': it is called 'widespread' because the judgment in it can apply to any time, so it is spread over times, and 'absolute' for what we mentioned in the temporal absolute. 'The general absolute etc.': it is called 'absolute' because when the proposition is uttered without restriction by permanence or necessity, the actuality of the relation is understood; so the proposition in which actuality of the relation is judged is called 'absolute' – naming the signified by the name of the signifier, and 'general' because it is more general than the non-permanent existential and the non-necessary existential as you will know in the compounds. 'The general possible etc.': it is called that because it includes the meaning of possibility, and 'general' because it is more general than the special possible which you will know in the compounds. 'Every fire etc.': in it, the judgment is made of the non-necessity of the negation; for the negation is contrary to the relation, and if the non-necessity of the negation were not, the affirmation would not be possible. And his statement: 'Nothing of fire is cold with general possibility' – in it the judgment is made of the non-necessity of the affirmation; for the affirmation is contrary to the relation, and if the non-necessity of the affirmation were not, the negation would not be possible. So the meaning of the affirmative is that the negation of heat from fire is not necessary, and the meaning of the negative is that the affirmation of cold for fire is not necessary.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defines and explains four specific types of simple modal propositions, namely the fifth through eighth categories. The text first gives examples and definitions for each: the 'widespread absolute' (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa) where the predicate is affirmed or denied of the subject at some indefinite time, as in 'every animal breathes at some time'; the 'general absolute' (al-mutlaqa al-amma) where the predicate is actual at one of the three times (past, present, future), like 'every human laughs actually'; and the 'general possible' (al-mumkina al-amma) where the contradictory is not necessary, exemplified by 'every fire is hot with general possibility'. The commentary then clarifies the terminology: 'widespread' because the judgment can apply to any time, 'absolute' because it is unrestricted; 'general absolute' because it is more general than the non-permanent types; 'general possible' because it includes possibility and is more general than the special possible. The commentary also explains the astronomical background of the moon eclipse example. The key logical point is that in the general possible proposition, the judgment denies the necessity of the opposite side, so the affirmative denies the necessity of the negation, and the negative denies the necessity of the affirmation. This establishes the basic framework for understanding modal propositions in the logic tradition.

Key Terms
المنتشرة المطلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Every human laughs actually (general absolute)
B) Every animal breathes by necessity at some time
C) Every fire is hot with general possibility
D) Nothing of the moon is eclipsed at quadrature (temporal absolute)

Key Takeaway
The widespread absolute captures necessary but temporally unfixed attributes, essential for distinguishing permanent from temporary necessities.

5 min
The General Absolute Proposition
Author defines the seventh simple modal proposition with example and terminological explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a general absolute proposition and explain why it is called 'absolute'.

Core Idea
The general absolute asserts actuality at one of the three times, the simplest modal form.

This lesson examines the seventh simple modal proposition, the general absolute. It is the most straightforward: it simply affirms or denies the predicate at some time (past, present, or future). It is called 'absolute' because it is the default understanding when a proposition is uttered without modal qualification, and 'general' because it is more general than the non-permanent and non-necessary existentials.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term: al-mutlaqa al-amma (المطلقة العامة). It is the seventh simple modal proposition.
2
Extract the genus
It is a modal proposition, but with a weaker modal force than the widespread absolute.
The genus remains 'modal proposition'.
3
Identify the differentia
The judgment is of the existence (or negation) of the predicate for the subject in one of the three times (past, present, future). There is no necessity; it is an actual occurrence.
The key phrase is 'في أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة'.
4
Analyze the example
Consider 'Every human laughs actually'. This means that at the present time (or some time) humans laugh. It does not claim they always laugh or must laugh.
The example uses 'بالفعل' (actually) to indicate the predicate holds.
5
Understand the naming
It is called 'absolute' because when a proposition is uttered without restriction, this is what is understood. 'General' because it is broader than the non-permanent existential (which excludes permanent predicates) and the non-necessary existential.
The commentary explains the tarmination.

Propositions

6 min
The General Possible Proposition
Author defines the eighth simple modal proposition with examples and logical explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a general possible proposition and explain what it means to negate the necessity of the contradictory side.

Core Idea
The general possible denies the necessity of the opposite, not the possibility of the claim itself.

This lesson covers the eighth simple modal proposition, the general possible. It expresses possibility in a broad sense: it simply says that the contradictory side is not necessary. This is the weakest form of possibility, as it only requires that the opposite is not necessary, not that it is actually possible in reality. The examples involve fire being hot and not cold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find: al-mumkina al-amma (الممكنة العامة). It is the eighth simple modal proposition.
2
Extract the genus
It is a modal proposition, but its modality is possibility (imkan).
Genus: modal proposition.
3
Identify the differentia
The judgment is the negation of necessity of the contradictory side. For an affirmative proposition, it denies that the negation is necessary; for a negative, it denies that the affirmation is necessary.
The key phrase is 'سلب ضرورة الجانب المخالف'.
4
Analyze the examples
'Every fire is hot with general possibility' means that the negation (fire is not hot) is not necessary. 'Nothing of fire is cold with general possibility' means that the affirmation (fire is cold) is not necessary.
The commentary clarifies: for affirmative, the opposite is negation; for negative, the opposite is affirmation.
5
Understand the logical implications
If the negation of the opposite were necessary, then the proposition itself would be impossible. So general possibility simply rules out the necessity of the opposite, ensuring the proposition is not impossible.
This is the weakest possibility; stronger forms add actual possibility.

البسائط من الموجهات الشمس، ولا شيء من القمر بمنخسف بالضرورة وقت التربيع. والسادسة: المنتشرة المطلقة وهي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، أو نفيه عنه في وقت غير معين من أوقات الذات نحو كل حيوان متنفس بالضرورة وقتا ما، ولا شيء من الحجر بمتنفس بالضرورة. والسابعة المطلقة العامة: وهي التي حكم فيها بوجود المحمول للموضوع، أو سلبه عنه أي في أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة كقولك: كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل، ولا شيء من الإنسان بضاحك بالفعل. والثامنة: الممكنة العامة وهي التي حكم فيها بسلب ضرورة الجانب المخالف كقولك: كل نار حارة بالإمكان العام ولا شيء من النار ببارد بالإمكان العام. وقت التربيع إلخ التربيع كون القمر في البرج الرابع من البرج الذي فيه الشمس؛ فلا ينخسف القمر في هذا الوقت، وإنما ينخسف عند حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس، وهو وقت المقابلة، وذلك بأنه يقع ظلّ الأرض على وجه القمر فيظلم؛ لأن القمر ليس بنوراني بالذات عندهم، بل جرمه كدر، إنما يقتبس النور من الشمس. والتفصيل في كتب الهيأة المنتشرة المطلقة إلخ إنما سميت "منتشرة"؛ لاحتمال الحكم فيها كل وقت؛ فيكون منتشرا في الأوقات، و"مطلقة"؛ لما ذكرنا في الوقتية المطلقة. المطلقة العامة إلخ : إنما سميت "مطلقة"؛ لأن القضية إذا أطلقت من غير تقييد باللادوام أو اللاضرورة يفهم منه فعلية النسبة؛ فسميت القضية التي حكم فيها بفعلية النسبة "مطلقة" تسمية للمدلول باسم الدال، و"عامة"؛ لأنها أعم من الوجودية اللادائمة، والوجودية اللاضرورية كما ستعرفها في المركبات. الممكنة العامة إلخ سميت لاشتمالها على معنى الإمكان، و"عامة"؛ لكونها أعم من الممكنة الخاصة التي ستعرفها في المركبات كل نار إلخ حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة السلب؛ إذ السلب خلاف النسبة، ولو لم يكن عدم ضرورة السلب لم يكن الإيجاب ممكنا. وقوله: لا شيء من النار ببارد بالإمكان العام حكم فيها بعدم ضرورة الإيجاب؛ إذ الإيجاب خلاف النسبة، ولو لم يكن عدم ضرورة الإيجاب لم يكن السلب ممكنا فمعنى الموجبة أن سلب الحرارة عن النار ليس بضروري ومعنى السالبة أن إيجاب البرودة للنار ليس بضروري.
The simple modalities: The sun, and nothing of the moon is eclipsed by necessity at the time of quadrature. The sixth: the widespread absolute, which is the proposition in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate's affirmation to the subject, or its negation from it, at an unspecified time from the times of the essence, such as 'Every animal breathes by necessity at some time' and 'Nothing of stone breathes by necessity'. The seventh: the general absolute, which is the proposition in which judgment is made of the existence of the predicate for the subject, or its negation from it, i.e., in one of the three times, as you say: 'Every human laughs actually' and 'Nothing of human laughs actually'. The eighth: the general possible, which is the proposition in which judgment is made of the negation of necessity of the contradictory side, as you say: 'Every fire is hot with general possibility' and 'Nothing of fire is cold with general possibility'. 'Time of quadrature etc.': quadrature is the moon being in the fourth sign from the sign in which the sun is; so the moon is not eclipsed at this time, but is eclipsed when the earth interposes between it and the sun, which is the time of opposition, because the earth's shadow falls on the moon's face and it darkens, for the moon is not luminous in itself according to them, but its body is dark, it only borrows light from the sun; the detail is in astronomical books. 'The widespread absolute etc.': it is called 'widespread' because the judgment in it can apply to any time, so it is spread over times, and 'absolute' for what we mentioned in the temporal absolute. 'The general absolute etc.': it is called 'absolute' because when the proposition is uttered without restriction by permanence or necessity, the actuality of the relation is understood; so the proposition in which actuality of the relation is judged is called 'absolute' – naming the signified by the name of the signifier, and 'general' because it is more general than the non-permanent existential and the non-necessary existential as you will know in the compounds. 'The general possible etc.': it is called that because it includes the meaning of possibility, and 'general' because it is more general than the special possible which you will know in the compounds. 'Every fire etc.': in it, the judgment is made of the non-necessity of the negation; for the negation is contrary to the relation, and if the non-necessity of the negation were not, the affirmation would not be possible. And his statement: 'Nothing of fire is cold with general possibility' – in it the judgment is made of the non-necessity of the affirmation; for the affirmation is contrary to the relation, and if the non-necessity of the affirmation were not, the negation would not be possible. So the meaning of the affirmative is that the negation of heat from fire is not necessary, and the meaning of the negative is that the affirmation of cold for fire is not necessary.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defines and explains four specific types of simple modal propositions, namely the fifth through eighth categories. The text first gives examples and definitions for each: the 'widespread absolute' (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa) where the predicate is affirmed or denied of the subject at some indefinite time, as in 'every animal breathes at some time'; the 'general absolute' (al-mutlaqa al-amma) where the predicate is actual at one of the three times (past, present, future), like 'every human laughs actually'; and the 'general possible' (al-mumkina al-amma) where the contradictory is not necessary, exemplified by 'every fire is hot with general possibility'. The commentary then clarifies the terminology: 'widespread' because the judgment can apply to any time, 'absolute' because it is unrestricted; 'general absolute' because it is more general than the non-permanent types; 'general possible' because it includes possibility and is more general than the special possible. The commentary also explains the astronomical background of the moon eclipse example. The key logical point is that in the general possible proposition, the judgment denies the necessity of the opposite side, so the affirmative denies the necessity of the negation, and the negative denies the necessity of the affirmation. This establishes the basic framework for understanding modal propositions in the logic tradition.

Key Terms
الممكنة العامة
الجانب المخالف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The heat of fire
B) The necessity of the heat
C) The necessity of fire not being hot
D) The possibility of coldness

Key Takeaway
The general possible is the weakest modal, requiring only that the opposite is not necessary, thus removing impossibility.

Propositions

Defining the Compound Modal Proposition
Author defines compound modal propositions and naming rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a compound modal proposition and determine whether it is affirmative or negative based on its first part.

Core Idea
A compound modal proposition unites affirmation and negation in one logical unit.

In this passage, the author defines the compound modal proposition as a single proposition whose reality is built from both an affirmation and a negation. This may seem paradoxical — how can one proposition be both? The key is that the first part determines the overall sign, while the second part introduces a restriction of non-permanence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing nuanced arguments in Islamic logic, especially those involving temporal modalities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Find the sentence 'المركبة قضية ركبت حقيقتها من إيجاب وسلب'. Understand that a compound proposition is a single proposition, not two.
2
Note the naming rule
The author states that the proposition is called affirmative or negative based on its first part. Look at the example: 'بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما' — the first part is affirmative, so the whole is affirmative.
3
Contrast with simple propositions
How does this differ from a simple modal proposition? A simple proposition has one modality; a compound has two aspects: a necessity/permanence part and a non-permanence part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المركبات المركبة قضية ركبت حقيقتها من إيجاب وسلب والاعتبار في تسميتها موجبة أو سالبة للجزء الأول، فإن كان الجزء الأول موجبا كقولك: بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما سميت موجبة، وإن كان الجزء الأول سالبا كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما سميت سالبة. ومن المركبات المشروطة الخاصة وهي المشروطة العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات ومر مثالها إيجابا وسلبا. ومنها العرفية الخاصة: وهي العرفية العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات، كما تقول: دائما كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما، ودائما لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما. ومنها الوجودية اللاضرورية: والاعتبار في تسميتها إلخ هذا جواب إيراد وهو أن حقيقة القضية المركبة ملتئمة من الإيجاب والسلب، فكيف تكون موجبة أو سالبة ؟ اللادوام بحسب الذات إلخ إنما قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات؛ لأن المشروطة العامة هي الضرورة بحسب الوصف والضرورة بحسب الوصف دوام بحسبه، والدوام بحسب الوصف ممتنع أن يقيد باللادوام بحسب الوصف، فلا بد من أن يقيد باللادوام بحسب الذات حتى تكون النسبة فيها ضرورية أو دائمة في جمع أوقات وصف الموضوع، لا دائمة في بعض أوقات ذات الموضوع، فافهم، كذا في العرفية الخاصة. ومر مثالها إلخ وهي إن كانت موجبة كقولنا: بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما فتركيبهما من موجبة مشروطة عامة، وسالبة مطلقة عامة. أما المشروطة العامة الموجبة وهي الجزء الأول من القضية، وأما السالبة المطلقة العامة فهي الجزء الثاني من القضية، أي قولنا: لا شيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الأصابع بالفعل، فهو مفهوم اللادوام؛ لأن إيجاب المحمول للموضوع إذا لم يكن دائما كان معناه أن الإيجاب ليس متحققا في جميع الأوقات، وإذا لم يتحقق الإيجاب في جميع الأوقات يتحقق السلب في الجملة وهي معنى السالبة المطلقة العامة وإن كانت سالبة فهي كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما فتركيبهما من مشروطة عامة سالبة وموجبة مطلقة عامة، فافهم.
Chapter on compounds: The compound is a proposition whose reality is composed of affirmation and negation, and the consideration in its being called affirmative or negative is the first part. If the first part is affirmative, as when you say: 'By necessity, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently', it is called affirmative. If the first part is negative, as when we say: 'By necessity, nothing of the writer is still in fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently', it is called negative. Among the compounds are the special conditional, which is the general conditional with the restriction of non-permanence with respect to the essence; its example has passed in affirmation and negation. Among them is the special customary: which is the general customary with the restriction of non-permanence with respect to the essence, as you say: 'Permanently, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently', and 'Permanently, nothing of the writer is still in fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently'. Among them is the non-necessary existential. 'And the consideration in its naming etc.' is the answer to an objection: that the reality of the compound proposition is composed of affirmation and negation, so how can it be affirmative or negative? 'Non-permanence with respect to the essence etc.': The restriction of non-permanence is with respect to the essence because the general conditional is necessity with respect to the description, and necessity with respect to the description is permanence with respect to it, and permanence with respect to the description cannot be restricted by non-permanence with respect to the description. So it must be restricted by non-permanence with respect to the essence so that the relation in it is necessary or permanent in all times of the description of the subject, not permanent in some times of the essence of the subject; understand, and similarly in the special customary. 'And its example has passed etc.': If it is affirmative, as our saying: 'By necessity, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently', its composition is from an affirmative general conditional and a negative general absolute. As for the affirmative general conditional, it is the first part of the proposition; as for the negative general absolute, it is the second part of the proposition, i.e., our saying: 'Nothing of the writer is moving his fingers actually', which is the concept of non-permanence because if the affirmation of the predicate for the subject is not permanent, its meaning is that the affirmation is not realized in all times, and if the affirmation is not realized in all times, the negation is realized in some measure, which is the meaning of the negative general absolute. If it is negative, as our saying: 'By necessity, nothing of the writer is still in fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently', its composition is from a negative general conditional and an affirmative general absolute; understand.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines compound modal propositions (al-murakkabat) as those whose reality is composed of an affirmation and a negation. The author first explains that the name (affirmative or negative) is given based on the first part. For example, 'By necessity, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently' is affirmative because the first part ('by necessity, every writer...') is affirmative. Then three types of compounds are listed: the special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa), the special customary (al-urfiyya al-khassa), and the non-necessary existential (al-wujubiyya la-daruriyya). The commentary, likely from a gloss, addresses an objection: how can a proposition composed of both affirmation and negation be called simply affirmative or negative? The answer is that the first part determines the naming, and the second part introduces the non-permanence. The restriction 'non-permanence with respect to the essence' is necessary because the general conditional (al-mashruta al-amma) involves necessity with respect to the description, which implies permanence for the duration of the description; restricting with non-permanence with respect to the essence avoids contradiction. The passage then breaks down the composition of the affirmative special conditional as an affirmative general conditional (first part) and a negative general absolute (second part), explaining that the negative general absolute captures the meaning of 'not permanent' because if the affirmation is not permanent, then the negation holds at some time. Similarly, the negative special conditional is composed of a negative general conditional and an affirmative general absolute. This illustrates the precise logical structure of compound modalities, where two simple propositions are combined to express a temporal restriction.

المركبة
الجزء الأول

Types of Compound Modal Propositions
Author enumerates types of compound modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of compound modal propositions: special conditional, special customary, and non-necessary existential.

Core Idea
Three types of compound propositions refine how non-permanence is applied to different modal bases.

The passage introduces three specific types of compound modal propositions: the special conditional, the special customary, and the non-necessary existential. Each combines a basic modal form (general conditional or general customary) with a restriction of non-permanence with respect to the essence. Understanding these distinctions is essential for advanced logical analysis in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
List the three types
From the passage: المشروطة الخاصة, العرفية الخاصة, الوجودية اللاضرورية.
2
Understand the special conditional
It is the general conditional (necessity with respect to description) plus the restriction of non-permanence with respect to essence. Example: 'بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما'.
3
Understand the special customary
It is the general customary (permanence with respect to description) plus non-permanence with respect to essence. Example: 'دائما كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما'.
4
Note the non-necessary existential
Only listed; no elaboration in this passage.

Chapter on compounds: The compound is a proposition whose reality is composed of affirmation and negation, and the consideration in its being called affirmative or negative is the first part. If the first part is affirmative, as when you say: 'By necessity, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently', it is called affirmative. If the first part is negative, as when we say: 'By necessity, nothing of the writer is still in fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently', it is called negative. Among the compounds are the special conditional, which is the general conditional with the restriction of non-permanence with respect to the essence; its example has passed in affirmation and negation. Among them is the special customary: which is the general customary with the restriction of non-permanence with respect to the essence, as you say: 'Permanently, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently', and 'Permanently, nothing of the writer is still in fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently'. 'And the consideration in its naming etc.' is the answer to an objection: that the reality of the compound proposition is composed of affirmation and negation, so how can it be affirmative or negative? 'Non-permanence with respect to the essence etc.': The restriction of non-permanence is with respect to the essence because the general conditional is necessity with respect to the description, and necessity with respect to the description is permanence with respect to it, and permanence with respect to the description cannot be restricted by non-permanence with respect to the description. So it must be restricted by non-permanence with respect to the essence so that the relation in it is necessary or permanent in all times of the description of the subject, not permanent in some times of the essence of the subject; understand, and similarly in the special customary. 'And its example has passed etc.': If it is affirmative, as our saying: 'By necessity, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently', its composition is from an affirmative general conditional and a negative general absolute. As for the affirmative general conditional, it is the first part of the proposition; as for the negative general absolute, it is the second part of the proposition, i.e., our saying: 'Nothing of the writer is moving his fingers actually', which is the concept of non-permanence because if the affirmation of the predicate for the subject is not permanent, its meaning is that the affirmation is not realized in all times, and if the affirmation is not realized in all times, the negation is realized in some measure, which is the meaning of the negative general absolute. The three compound types refine the same basic idea: combining a modal base with a non-permanence restriction tied to essence rather than description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Composition of the Special Conditional
Author explains composition of the special conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decompose an affirmative special conditional proposition into its two component propositions: an affirmative general conditional and a negative general absolute.

Core Idea
The special conditional is a single compound built from two simple modals: a general conditional and a general absolute.

The passage goes deeper into the internal structure of the special conditional. It explains that an affirmative special conditional is composed of an affirmative general conditional as the first part and a negative general absolute as the second part. This decomposition reveals how the 'non-permanence' condition is logically expressed. Understanding this composition is key to mastering the mechanics of compound modalities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example
Take the affirmative special conditional: 'بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما'.
2
Find the first part
The first part is the affirmative general conditional: 'بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا' (By necessity, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer).
3
Find the second part
The second part is the negative general absolute: 'لا شيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الأصابع بالفعل' (Nothing of the writer is moving his fingers actually), which is implicit in 'لا دائما'.
4
Understand the logical relation
The negation of permanence ('لا دائما') is equivalent to the statement that there is some time when the predicate does not hold. That is exactly the meaning of the negative general absolute proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المركبات المركبة قضية ركبت حقيقتها من إيجاب وسلب والاعتبار في تسميتها موجبة أو سالبة للجزء الأول، فإن كان الجزء الأول موجبا كقولك: بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما سميت موجبة، وإن كان الجزء الأول سالبا كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما سميت سالبة. ومن المركبات المشروطة الخاصة وهي المشروطة العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات ومر مثالها إيجابا وسلبا. ومنها العرفية الخاصة: وهي العرفية العامة مع قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات، كما تقول: دائما كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما، ودائما لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما. ومنها الوجودية اللاضرورية: والاعتبار في تسميتها إلخ هذا جواب إيراد وهو أن حقيقة القضية المركبة ملتئمة من الإيجاب والسلب، فكيف تكون موجبة أو سالبة ؟ اللادوام بحسب الذات إلخ إنما قيد اللادوام بحسب الذات؛ لأن المشروطة العامة هي الضرورة بحسب الوصف والضرورة بحسب الوصف دوام بحسبه، والدوام بحسب الوصف ممتنع أن يقيد باللادوام بحسب الوصف، فلا بد من أن يقيد باللادوام بحسب الذات حتى تكون النسبة فيها ضرورية أو دائمة في جمع أوقات وصف الموضوع، لا دائمة في بعض أوقات ذات الموضوع، فافهم، كذا في العرفية الخاصة. ومر مثالها إلخ وهي إن كانت موجبة كقولنا: بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما فتركيبهما من موجبة مشروطة عامة، وسالبة مطلقة عامة. أما المشروطة العامة الموجبة وهي الجزء الأول من القضية، وأما السالبة المطلقة العامة فهي الجزء الثاني من القضية، أي قولنا: لا شيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الأصابع بالفعل، فهو مفهوم اللادوام؛ لأن إيجاب المحمول للموضوع إذا لم يكن دائما كان معناه أن الإيجاب ليس متحققا في جميع الأوقات، وإذا لم يتحقق الإيجاب في جميع الأوقات يتحقق السلب في الجملة وهي معنى السالبة المطلقة العامة وإن كانت سالبة فهي كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما فتركيبهما من مشروطة عامة سالبة وموجبة مطلقة عامة، فافهم.
Chapter on compounds: The compound is a proposition whose reality is composed of affirmation and negation, and the consideration in its being called affirmative or negative is the first part. If the first part is affirmative, as when you say: 'By necessity, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently', it is called affirmative. If the first part is negative, as when we say: 'By necessity, nothing of the writer is still in fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently', it is called negative. Among the compounds are the special conditional, which is the general conditional with the restriction of non-permanence with respect to the essence; its example has passed in affirmation and negation. Among them is the special customary: which is the general customary with the restriction of non-permanence with respect to the essence, as you say: 'Permanently, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently', and 'Permanently, nothing of the writer is still in fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently'. Among them is the non-necessary existential. 'And the consideration in its naming etc.' is the answer to an objection: that the reality of the compound proposition is composed of affirmation and negation, so how can it be affirmative or negative? 'Non-permanence with respect to the essence etc.': The restriction of non-permanence is with respect to the essence because the general conditional is necessity with respect to the description, and necessity with respect to the description is permanence with respect to it, and permanence with respect to the description cannot be restricted by non-permanence with respect to the description. So it must be restricted by non-permanence with respect to the essence so that the relation in it is necessary or permanent in all times of the description of the subject, not permanent in some times of the essence of the subject; understand, and similarly in the special customary. 'And its example has passed etc.': If it is affirmative, as our saying: 'By necessity, every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently', its composition is from an affirmative general conditional and a negative general absolute. As for the affirmative general conditional, it is the first part of the proposition; as for the negative general absolute, it is the second part of the proposition, i.e., our saying: 'Nothing of the writer is moving his fingers actually', which is the concept of non-permanence because if the affirmation of the predicate for the subject is not permanent, its meaning is that the affirmation is not realized in all times, and if the affirmation is not realized in all times, the negation is realized in some measure, which is the meaning of the negative general absolute. If it is negative, as our saying: 'By necessity, nothing of the writer is still in fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently', its composition is from a negative general conditional and an affirmative general absolute; understand.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines compound modal propositions (al-murakkabat) as those whose reality is composed of an affirmation and a negation. The author first explains that the name (affirmative or negative) is given based on the first part. For example, 'By necessity, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer, not permanently' is affirmative because the first part ('by necessity, every writer...') is affirmative. Then three types of compounds are listed: the special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa), the special customary (al-urfiyya al-khassa), and the non-necessary existential (al-wujubiyya la-daruriyya). The commentary, likely from a gloss, addresses an objection: how can a proposition composed of both affirmation and negation be called simply affirmative or negative? The answer is that the first part determines the naming, and the second part introduces the non-permanence. The restriction 'non-permanence with respect to the essence' is necessary because the general conditional (al-mashruta al-amma) involves necessity with respect to the description, which implies permanence for the duration of the description; restricting with non-permanence with respect to the essence avoids contradiction. The passage then breaks down the composition of the affirmative special conditional as an affirmative general conditional (first part) and a negative general absolute (second part), explaining that the negative general absolute captures the meaning of 'not permanent' because if the affirmation is not permanent, then the negation holds at some time. Similarly, the negative special conditional is composed of a negative general conditional and an affirmative general absolute. This illustrates the precise logical structure of compound modalities, where two simple propositions are combined to express a temporal restriction.

الجزء الثاني
المشروطة العامة
المطلقة العامة

Key Takeaway

Naming Compound Propositions by Their First Part
Definition and naming rule for compound propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a compound proposition and determine whether it is positive or negative based on its first part.

Core Idea
A compound proposition's classification depends only on its first component, despite containing both affirmation and negation.

In Islamic logic, a compound proposition is one whose reality includes both affirmation and negation. At first glance, it seems contradictory to call it simply 'positive' or 'negative'. The author resolves this by explaining that the naming follows the first part only. Let's work through the definition and examples to master this rule.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
A compound proposition (qadiyyah murakkabah) is defined as one whose reality (haqiqah) is composed of both affirmation (ījāb) and negation (salab). This means it contains both a positive and a negative component.
This is different from simple propositions which are either purely affirmative or purely negative.
2
Identify the first part
The author states that the naming (positive or negative) is based on the first part (al-juz' al-awwal) of the proposition. Locate the very first component of the compound statement.
The first part is not necessarily the subject; it is the first constituent in the logical order.
3
Apply the rule to examples
Two examples are given: 'necessarily every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is writing, not always' – here the first part 'necessarily every writer is moving his fingers' is affirmative, so the whole proposition is called positive. The second example: 'necessarily no writer is still in his fingers as long as he is writing, not always' – the first part 'necessarily no writer is still' is negative, so it is called negative.
The modal 'not always' is part of the compound but does not affect the naming.
4
Understand the objection and response
The commentary notes that this rule answers a potential objection: if the proposition contains both affirmation and negation, how can it be called simply positive or negative? The response is that the classification is by the first part, so the whole proposition takes the quality of its beginning.
This shows the precision of Islamic logicians in resolving apparent contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المركبات المركبة قضية ركبت حقيقتها من إيجاب وسلب والاعتبار في تسميتها موجبة أو سالبة للجزء الأول، فإن كان الجزء الأول موجبا كقولك: بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما" سمّيت موجبة، وإن كان الجزء الأول سالبا كقولنا: "بالضرورة لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما" سمّيت سالبة. والاعتبار في تسميتها إلخ هذا جواب إيراد وهو أن حقيقة القضية المركبة ملتئمة من الإيجاب والسلب، فكيف تكون موجبة أو سالبة ؟
Chapter on compound propositions: a compound proposition is one whose reality is composed of affirmation and negation. The consideration in naming it positive or negative is based on the first part. If the first part is positive, as you say: 'necessarily every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is writing, not always,' it is called positive. If the first part is negative, as we say: 'necessarily no writer is still in his fingers as long as he is writing, not always,' it is called negative. 'And the consideration in naming it, etc.' This is the answer to an objection: that the reality of a compound proposition is composed of affirmation and negation, so how can it be positive or negative?
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines a compound proposition (al-qadiyyah al-murakkabah) and clarifies how it can be classified as positive or negative despite containing both affirmation and negation. The rule is that the naming follows the first part of the compound. The author gives examples from the conditional special (mashrutah khassah) category. Then the hashya notes that this naming rule itself serves as the answer to a potential objection: if the proposition is composed of both affirmation and negation, how can it be called simply positive or negative? The answer is that the classification is based on the first component.
